1601 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jones


1996/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, BRONX COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 782; 781 N.Y.S.2d 852; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1114



July 9, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Jones, 25 A.D.3d 431, 806 N.Y.S.2d 416, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 272 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for two counts of attempted murder in the second degree, four counts of assault in the first degree, two counts criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree and other related crimes. Defendant raised a justification defense, and the trial court so charged the jury as to all counts. The trial court issued its opinion to explain the ruling as to the weapon possession charge in light of the Pons doctrine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was contacted by his girlfriend, who told defendant that the victim had thrown a drink in her face, and that if defendant did not come soon, she was going to kill the victim. Defendant went to the bar and confronted the victim, who was known to carry a gun. The girlfriend then confronted the victim. Defendant attempted to escort the girlfriend away, and felt a gun tucked into the back of her pants. When the victim appeared to reach for a gun, defendant pulled the gun from the back of the girlfriend's pants and fired, injuring the victim. Under the Pons decision, justification did not apply to weapons possession counts even when intent to use the weapon unlawfully was an element of the crime. The court held that the Pons holding had to be understood as applying only to those cases such as Pons where the defendant possessed the weapon for some "continuum" of time other than when he justifiably used it. In cases where no continuum of possession existed, the court held that Pons was inapplicable and that justification therefore remained a defense.


OUTCOME: The court determined that in the case before it, justification was a valid defense to the charge of criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Justification -- Criminal Possession of Weapon -- Justification Defense Applicable in Absence of "Continuum" of Possession 

Where the defense of justification was applicable with respect to the attempted murder and assault charges against defendant arising from a shooting incident in which defendant pulled a gun from the back of his girlfriend's pants during an altercation with a person he thought possessed a gun, justification was also properly charged as a defense to the two criminal possession of a weapon counts of the indictment. Justification is not a defense to a charge of weapon possession when the accused possessed the weapon for some "continuum" of time prior to the otherwise justified use of the weapon. However, justification remains a defense when, as here, the possession and use occur at the same moment and there is thus no "continuum" of possession.

 

1602 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Grinberg


2004KN003490


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 670; 781 N.Y.S.2d 584; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 957



June 25, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: At the conclusion of the People's case and after the People rested, defendant moved for a trial order of dismissal of all the charges, arguing that there was insufficient evidence on the record to make a prima facie case as to any of the charges and, in particular, that the People had failed to prove that defendant was operating a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol as required by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(1) and (3).


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that there was not legally sufficient evidence to conclude that defendant was driving while under the influence of alcohol because while the People established that his ability to operate a motor vehicle was impaired, the evidence did not establish that such impairment was caused by the consumption of alcohol. Therefore, defendant could only be found guilty if N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(3) proscribed operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of drugs. Based on the legislative pronouncements in connection with the enactment and subsequent amendments of the New York Vehicle and Traffic Law, the trial court concluded that § 1192(3) did not cover intoxication caused by a substance other than alcohol. The trial court's conclusion was buttressed by the case law, which similarly concluded that § 1192(3) referred exclusively to alcohol intoxication, and by the language of the pattern jury instruction for § 1192(3), which clearly limited "intoxication" to the state caused by alcohol consumption. Thus, the People failed to prove that defendant was under the influence of alcohol, an element of both counts in their accusatory instrument.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion for a trial order of dismissal with respect to all counts was granted.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs -- Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (3) Applicable Exclusively to Alcohol Intoxication

An accusatory instrument charging defendant with driving while intoxicated under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (3) was dismissed at the conclusion of the People's case due to the lack of legally sufficient evidence proving that defendant's impairment was caused by the consumption of alcohol. The arresting officer, who observed defendant unconscious in the driver's seat of a vehicle that had crashed into a parked car, initially arrested defendant for driving under the influence of drugs. However, defendant refused to submit to a chemical test, and no test was performed to confirm the presence of drugs. Under these circumstances, defendant could not be convicted of driving while intoxicated under section 1192 (3) since that statute applies exclusively to alcohol intoxication. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (3) does not apply to impairment caused solely by drugs or other unidentified substances.

1604 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Huron Group, Inc. v. Pataki


2004-4425


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 648; 785 N.Y.S.2d 827; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 992



June 16, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Related proceeding at Concern, Inc. v. Pataki, 7 Misc. 3d 1030A, 801 N.Y.S.2d 232, 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1088 (2005)
Affirmed by Huron Group, Inc. v. Pataki, 23 A.D.3d 1051, 803 N.Y.S.2d 465, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12703 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Nov. 10, 2005)



DISPOSITION: The Court granted summary judgment to Plaintiffs, granting certain declaratory and injunctive relief; the Court denied all cross-motions for summary judgment made by Defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the City of Buffalo (New York) and others, sought a declaration that the Nation-State Gaming Compact entered into by defendant governor and the Seneca Nation of Indians, was unconstitutional in part and void under N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1 to the extent that it authorized a casino anywhere in Erie County outside of the City of Buffalo. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The governor and the Seneca Nation entered into a memorandum of understanding (MOU) establishing certain terms for a Tribal-State Compact to authorize the development of Class III gaming casinos, and the legislature enacted N.Y. Exec. Law § 12 to authorize the governor to execute a Compact pursuant to the Indian Gaming Regulatory Act (IGRA), 25 U.S.C.S. §§ 2701-2721; 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 1166-1168. Granting plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and denying defendants' motions, the court held, inter alia, that (1) plaintiffs had standing under N.Y. State Fin. Law § 123-b to challenge the allegedly unconstitutional or illegal expenditures of state funds contemplated to be expended; (2) the court's resolution of the issues did not require the presence of the Seneca Nation; (3) the issues raised concerned state constitutional law not preempted by the IGRA; (4) the Compact was not consistent with the MOU, and the portion of the Compact which authorized a casino outside the City was unconstitutional under N.Y. Const. art. I, § III because the location of casinos was a matter of public policy for the legislature to decide; and (5) the invalid portion of the Compact was severable.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and declared that the portion of the contract that permitted the Seneca Nation to locate a Class III gaming facility in any location in Erie County outside of the City of Buffalo violated separation of powers principles. The court severed the invalid provision, granted plaintiffs' application for a permanent injunction, and denied defendants' cross-motions.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Legality of Gaming Compact with Indian Tribe 

1. Plaintiffs had standing as citizen-taxpayers under State Finance Law § 123-b to challenge the legality of a gaming compact with an Indian tribe that authorized the development of class III gaming casinos on Indian lands in Erie County. The statute provided standing to plaintiffs to bring an action to challenge the allegedly unconstitutional or illegal expenditure of state funds contemplated under the memorandum of understanding (MOU) between the Governor and the tribe; the statute authorizing the Governor to enter into a compact consistent with the MOU (Executive Law § 12); and the compact itself, without regard to whether there was an alternative, legal expenditure of the same funds.

Courts -- Ripeness Doctrine

2. Declaratory relief was available in a citizen-taxpayers' action under State Finance Law § 123-b challenging the legality of planned state expenditures in connection with a gaming compact with an Indian tribe that authorized the development of class III gaming casinos on Indian lands in Erie County. A number of steps had already been taken toward the establishment of the casino. The United States Department of Interior tacitly approved the Compact. The tribe planned to use funds received from the State in settling certain land claims to purchase property in order to construct the casino. Under these circumstances, the action was not premature.

Parties -- Necessary Parties -- Challenge to Legality of Gaming Compact with Indian Tribe

3. The Indian tribe that entered into a compact with the State authorizing it to develop class III gaming casinos in Erie County was not an indispensable party to the citizen-taxpayers' action under State Finance Law § 123-b challenging the legality of planned state expenditures in connection with the Tribal-State Compact. The presence of the tribe was not required to resolve any issue of contractual intent or any factual dispute. The resolution of the case rested solely upon the court's interpretation of the unambiguous provisions of the memorandum of understanding (MOU) between the Governor and the tribe, the statute authorizing the Governor to enter into a compact consistent with the MOU (Executive Law § 12), and the Compact itself, together with relevant constitutional provisions relating to the separation of powers (NY Const, art III, § 1; art IV, § 1).

Statutes -- Federal Preemption -- Challenge to Legality of Gaming Compact with Indian Tribe Not Preempted by Indian Gaming Regulatory Act 

4. A citizen-taxpayers' action under State Finance Law § 123-b challenging the legality of a gaming compact that authorized the development of class III gaming casinos on Indian lands in Erie County was not preempted by the Indian Gaming Regulatory Act of 1988 (IGRA) (25 USC § 2701 et seq.). While the federal statute preempts the area of regulation of gaming on Indian lands, the statute does not negate the applicability of state constitutional and statutory law to issues involving the execution of a Tribal-State Compact. The question of whether the legislation that authorized the Governor to enter into a compact "consistent with" a memorandum of understanding regarding the development of a casino in the City of Buffalo (Executive Law § 12) lawfully delegated to the Governor any power concerning the location of a casino outside the City of Buffalo raised issues of state constitutional law not preempted by IGRA or any other federal law.

Constitutional Law -- Separation of Powers -- Governor's Execution of Gaming Compact with Indian Tribe without Legislative Approval

5. The Governor lacked authority under the separation of powers doctrine to enter into a gaming compact with an Indian tribe that authorized the development of a class III gaming casino on Indian lands outside the City of Buffalo. Under the Indian Gaming Regulatory Act of 1988 (25 USC § 2701 et seq.), and as a matter of state constitutional law (see NY Const, art III, § 1; art IV, § 1), the location of a class III gaming casino must be authorized by the Legislature. Here, the initial memorandum of understanding (MOU) between the Governor and the tribe referred to the establishment of a casino in Erie County within the City of Buffalo. The MOU further provided that, in the event a site in the City of Buffalo was not available for any reason, the tribe "would propose an alternate site." The enabling legislation authorized the Governor to enter into a gaming compact "consistent with" the terms of the MOU (Executive Law § 12). The Compact was not "consistent with" the MOU to the extent that it authorized the tribe to select a location for a casino in Erie County outside the City of Buffalo. Any change in the municipal location of the casino outside the City of Buffalo required additional legislative authorization. Moreover, the certification by the Governor that the Compact was "consistent with" the MOU did not remedy the constitutional infirmity. However, the invalid provision of the Compact was severed, leaving the remainder of the Compact in full force and effect.

 

1605 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. West


04030054


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, TOWN OF NEW PALTZ, ULSTER COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 605; 780 N.Y.S.2d 723; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 954; 2004 NY Slip Op 24224



June 10, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Subsequent appeal at People v. West, 4 N.Y.3d 892, 831 N.E.2d 982, 798 N.Y.S.2d 737, 2005 N.Y. LEXIS 1814 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss an information filed in the trial court (New York) charging defendant with solemnizing marriages for individuals who had not obtained marriage licenses in violation of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 13 and 17.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the licensing requirement was unconstitutional as applied as it had the effect of preventing same-sex marriages. The People argued that the law was violated and that it was obligated to prosecute defendant. The trial court held that court decisions and legislative enactments addressing related issues had left no doubt that New York policy favored ameliorating the discriminatory effect of the laws on homosexuals. The trial court applied the rational basis standard to defendant's equal protection challenge. As the People did not show that there was a legitimate state interest in refusing marriage to same-sex partners, and as the New York Attorney General did not exercise its right to intervene to defend the state's interest in a statute that had the effect of preventing same-sex couples from marrying, the trial court found that the defense rebutted the presumption of constitutionality of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 13. Thus, the burden of proof on that issue shifted to the People. The equal protection guarantees enshrined in the state and federal constitutions were paramount to the reasons given in cases from other states for opposing same-sex marriages.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Unauthorized Performance of Marriage Ceremonies for Same-Sex Couples 

An accusatory instrument charging defendant, a village mayor, with solemnizing same-sex marriages for individuals without marriage licenses in violation of state law (Domestic Relations Law §§ 13, 17) was dismissed because the denial of marriage licenses to same-sex couples is unconstitutional as being violative of equal protection. Defendant rebutted the presumption of constitutionality, thereby shifting the burden of proof on that issue to the People. The People failed to demonstrate that the State has a legitimate governmental purpose in refusing marriage to same-sex couples, especially in view of the State's established policy otherwise prohibiting discrimination based upon sexual preference (see Civil Rights Law § 40-c; Insurance Law § 2701; Penal Law § 240.30 [3]; § 485.05 [1]).

 

1606 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bonitto


2004NY016647


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 386; 777 N.Y.S.2d 900; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 664



June 9, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with second degree aggravated harassment, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30(1), and second degree harassment, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.26(1), for writing two letters to a person unknown to him while he was incarcerated. Defendant moved to dismiss the complaint as facially insufficient, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 100.40 and 170.30.


OVERVIEW: The first letter apologized for the invasion of privacy and, essentially, requested permission to call. The second letter, referencing the lack of a response, spoke of having to call if the recipient did not write the inmate back. While receiving mail from an inmate unknown to the recipient may have been unsettling, the letters did not seriously express an intent to do an act of unlawful violence and could not reasonably be so interpreted. A statement that defendant would continue to write was not a "true threat" or its equivalent. A reference to "Plan B," if the letter was not responded to, was ambiguous and explained by the letter's final sentence which said defendant, absent a response, would call the recipient. Had defendant continued to write or made unwelcome telephone calls, he might have committed unlawful harassment, but the two letters he sent were not within any proscribable class of speech or conduct and were protected, requiring dismissal of the aggravated harassment charges. As no facts were stated to base harassment on, because no physical contact was allegedly attempted or threatened, the harassment charge had to be dismissed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Aggravated Harassment--Letters Sent by Prison Inmate to Person Selected at Random

The accusatory instrument charging defendant, a prison inmate, with aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) based upon his having written two letters to a person selected at random from a telephone directory in the hope that he might get a visit, telephone call or a written reply, was dismissed for facial insufficiency. The statute provides that a person is guilty of aggravated harassment in the second degree "when, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another person, he . . . communicates with a person . . . by mail . . . in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm." The complaint, as a matter of law, failed to allege facts which constituted harassing conduct or true threats. While letters written by defendant one month apart may have been unsettling to complainant, they did not fall within the proscribable classes of speech or conduct.

 

1607 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miles


347/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


5 Misc. 3d 271; 783 N.Y.S.2d 748; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1208



May 25, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Miles, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9514 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, July 20, 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion denied in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: When defendant, who was convicted of robbery, learned that he was subject to sentencing as a persistent violent felony offender under N.Y. Penal Law § 78.08, he orally moved the court not to impose an enhanced sentence, arguing that the statute violated the Apprendi rule.


OVERVIEW: New York's highest court had already held that discretionary sentence enhancement based on prior convictions did not violate the Apprendi rule that any sentencing factor other than a prior conviction which enhanced a defendant's sentence had to first be included in an indictment and found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt. The instant court held that the rationale applied as well to the persistent felony offender statute. Defendant's prior convictions of violent crimes were notorious and open enough that they could be judicially noticed, and the mere fact that the court had to make a purely ministerial determination that a period of incarceration had tolled a 10-year limitations period applicable to the calculation of prior violent felony convictions in a 10-year period did not mean that there had been a violation of the right to trial by jury.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion and sentenced defendant as a persistent violent felony offender.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Constitutionality of Mandatory Sentencing Procedure 

The mandatory sentencing procedure under which defendant received an enhanced sentence as a persistent violent felony offender (Penal Law § 70.08; CPL 400.16), based upon his two prior violent felony convictions occurring within the previous 10 years (see Penal Law § 70.04 [1] [b]), is not unconstitutional. A criminal defendant has no constitutional right to a jury trial to establish the fact of a prior conviction for purposes of enhanced sentencing. A prior conviction is a sentencing factor, not an element of the crime, and thus need not be pleaded in the indictment in order for it to serve as the basis for enhanced sentencing. Furthermore, the sentencing court's ministerial act of calculating the amount of time defendant was incarcerated for purposes of determining the number of predicate violent felony convictions he sustained over the requisite 10-year period did not require a jury's finding beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

1608 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Allen v. Cattaraugus County Bd. of Health


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, CATTARAUGUS COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 383; 777 N.Y.S.2d 886; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 652



May 18, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bar owner brought an N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondent county health board, contending the board acted improperly in fining him for violations of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1399-o(2), which was part of New York's Clean Indoor Air Act and which provided that smoking was not permitted in bars.


OVERVIEW: The owner operated a saloon and was twice found in violation of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1399-o(2) by allowing people to smoke at the bar. He was fined both times and was unsuccessful on his appeals of both fines. The owner commenced an action claiming the board exceeded its authority by imposing liability on him where his patrons were smoking and he was powerless to stop them. He also argued that the board was selectively enforcing the statute by targeting the bar owners rather than the smokers, that he was denied due process, and that the board's determination was not supported by substantial evidence. The court found that the evidence showed it was not a case where the bar's patrons were smoking in violation of the owner's good faith efforts to get them to stop, and the evidence showed the bartender blatantly allowed customers to smoke in the bar. Moreover, the owner received sufficient notice and opportunity to present his arguments, and since smokers were not a protected class for equal protection purposes, the owner could not justify his selective enforcement argument. Finally, the court rejected a challenge to the statute's constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the owner's petition.


HEADNOTES

Health -- No Smoking Regulations -- Bars 

Petitioner was lawfully fined by petitioner Board of Health for permitting smoking in his bar in violation of the Clean Indoor Air Act (Public Health Law § 1399-o [2]). The act was not unconstitutional as implemented. Respondent did not exceed its lawful authority or violate its lawful procedures by citing petitioner for violating the act but not the individual smokers. Furthermore, petitioner was not denied due process. Petitioner was notified of the allegations of smoking and was given an opportunity to rebut them. In addition, respondent's determination was supported by substantial evidence.

1610 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garson


Indictment Nos. 3515/03, 5332/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


4 Misc. 3d 258; 775 N.Y.S.2d 827; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 473



April 29, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by People v Garson, 17 A.D.3d 695, 793 N.Y.S.2d 539, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4427 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Apr. 25, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a supreme court judge, moved to dismiss two indictments charging him with one count of bribe receiving in the third degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 200.10; six counts of receiving reward for official misconduct in the second degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 200.25; three counts of official misconduct, N.Y. Penal Law § 195.00; and one count of receiving unlawful gratuities, N.Y. Penal Law § 200.35.


OVERVIEW: All of the charges related to defendant's conduct as a judge presiding over matrimonial cases in the supreme court. Granting defendant's motion to dismiss in part, the court held that the evidence was legally sufficient to support the count for bribe receiving; one, but only one, count of official misconduct; and the count for receiving unlawful gratuities. However, the grand jury evidence demonstrating that defendant violated the Rules of the Chief Administrator, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 100.3(B)(6) and § 100.2(C), was legally insufficient to establish that defendant violated a duty he had as a public servant within the meaning of the Penal Law. In addition, the court held that the grand jury proceeding which resulted in the second indictment was not defective because the prosecutor was not required to submit all at once every piece of available evidence and every charge, and it held that the eavesdropping and video surveillance evidence was admissible because the applications made out probable cause sufficient to support the issuance of the warrants and established that normal investigative procedures were unlikely to succeed in obtaining the evidence sought.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to the extent of dismissing the six counts of receiving reward for official misconduct in the second degree and two counts of official misconduct, and it denied defendant's motion in all other respects. The court also order that all discovery disputes be referred to the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Receiving Reward for Official Misconduct -- Prosecution of Judge Improperly Based upon Violation of Rules Governing Judicial Conduct 

1. Defendant, a Justice of the Supreme Court, could not be criminally prosecuted for the class E felony of receiving reward for official misconduct in the second degree (Penal Law § 200.25) based upon violations of the Rules Governing Judicial Conduct (22 NYCRR part 100). In any prosecution for receiving reward for official misconduct, the evidence must identify a duty the defendant had as a public servant, and must establish that the defendant violated that duty and thereafter solicited, accepted or agreed to accept a benefit for having done so. Defendant herein allegedly engaged in ex parte conversations with an attorney representing a party in a case then pending before him, and later accepted a box of expensive cigars for having done so, in violation of the rule that prohibits a judge from engaging in unauthorized ex parte communications (22 NYCRR 100.3 [b] [6]). Defendant also allegedly accepted "referral fees" from a lawyer in violation of the rule that prohibits a judge from lending "the prestige of judicial office to advance the private interests of the judge or others" (22 NYCRR 100.2 [c]). However, the Rules Governing Judicial Conduct were not designed or intended as a basis for criminal prosecution. Consequently, the evidence presented to the grand jury that defendant violated two ethical rules was insufficient to establish that he violated a duty he had as a public servant within the meaning of the Penal Law.

Crimes -- Official Misconduct -- Judge's Acceptance of Compensation for Giving Advice in Pending Matter 

2. Defendant, a Justice of the Supreme Court, was lawfully charged with official misconduct (Penal Law § 195.00) for having allegedly engaged in unauthorized ex parte conversations with an attorney representing a party in a case then pending before him, and later accepting a box of expensive cigars for having done so. The official misconduct charge was not based upon defendant's mere violation of a rule of judicial conduct (see 22 NYCRR 100.3 [b] [6]), but, rather, was based upon defendant's violation of Judiciary Law § 18. That statute prohibits a judge from demanding or receiving compensation for giving advice in a matter pending before that judge. The evidence presented to the grand jury was legally sufficient to establish that defendant, acting with the intent to obtain a benefit and knowing that he was not authorized to do so, received compensation in the form of a box of expensive cigars for giving advice to an attorney who was representing a party in a case then pending before him, and thereby committed an act relating to his office but constituting an unauthorized exercise of his official functions. However, defendant could not be charged with separate official misconduct counts for having given advice and then having received compensation for that advice. The evidence was legally sufficient to support only one count of official misconduct. In addition, the evidence was also legally sufficient to support the charges of receiving unlawful gratuities (Penal Law § 200.35).

Grand Jury -- Defective Proceeding -- Submission of Additional Charges to Grand Jury after Obtaining Indictment 

3. A grand jury proceeding that resulted in a second indictment against defendant, a Justice of the Supreme Court, adding a new charge of bribe receiving in the third degree to the first indictment and additional charges of official misconduct, was not defective because of the prosecutor's failure to secure court authorization before submitting the additional charges. A prosecutor's withdrawal of a case from the grand jury after presentation of the evidence is the equivalent of a dismissal, and therefore triggers the requirement that judicial authorization be obtained before the matter is resubmitted (see CPL 190.75 [3]). Here, after obtaining the first indictment, the People presented further evidence to the same grand jury and asked it to consider indicting defendant for additional crimes. The People did not present charges to a second grand jury after they had been rejected by a first, and did not engage in forum shopping by withdrawing a case from one grand jury in the hope of later presenting the same evidence to a more compliant second one. The People are not required, when presenting a case to a grand jury, to submit for consideration all at once every piece of available evidence and every charge arguably arising out of the criminal transaction under investigation.

Crimes -- Eavesdropping Warrants -- Probable Cause 

4. Eavesdropping and video surveillance evidence was lawfully obtained for use against defendant, a Justice of the Supreme Court, in his prosecution for bribe receiving and other related charges. The warrant applications sufficiently established probable cause to believe that defendant committed or was about to commit certain designated offenses and that communications or observations concerning those offenses would be obtained through the proposed electronic surveillance (see CPL 700.15). In addition, the warrant applications sufficiently established that normal investigative procedures were unlikely to succeed in obtaining the evidence sought. Furthermore, the electronic surveillance did not continue for a longer period than was necessary to achieve the objectives stated in the warrants.

 

1611 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Bobbijean P.


Docket No. NN 03626-03


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


2 Misc. 3d 1011(A); 784 N.Y.S.2d 919; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 390; 2004 NY Slip Op 50286(U)



March 31, 2004, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Adhered to In re Bobbijean P., 6 Misc. 3d 1012[A], 800 N.Y.S.2d 342, 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 53 (2005)
Vacated by, in part Matter of Bobbijean P. v. Stephanie P., 46 A.D.3d 12, 842 N.Y.S.2d 826, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10288 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Sept. 28, 2007)



DISPOSITION: The Court entered its Orders regarding the future family planning required of Respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county department filed a petition for an order determining a minor child to be a neglected child by defendant parents.


OVERVIEW: The parents had a history of drug abuse. Three of the mother's previous children and one of the father's prior children with the mother were subjected to neglect proceedings. Three of the mother's children were born "cocaine babies;" however, the fourth child was not tested for cocaine upon her birth. All of the mother's children and two of the father's children were in foster care. A caseworker testified at the hearing. The court found that clear and convincing evidence showed that the parents neglected their new baby so that her physical condition was in imminent danger of becoming impaired, under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1012(f)(1), by their history of drug usage, their failure to get substance abuse and mental health treatment as previously ordered, by their failure to have suitable housing for her, and by their failure to plan for the baby and have baby supplies. The court found that the parents should not have further children until they could provide for their children.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted, and the parents were ordered to not have any more children until all of their children were being raised by a natural parent, or were no longer being cared for at the expense of the public.

 [*1011A]  [**919] Parent and Child--Abused or Neglected Child.

Keenan R. v. Julie L.


Decision No. V41189/03


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 819; 775 N.Y.S.2d 468; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 292



March 25, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Remanded by Matter of Keenan R. v. Julie L., 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3854 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 27, 2007)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's motion to dismiss the petition granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner foster child sought visitation with his biological siblings. Respondent adoptive parents sought to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: The foster child was 14 years old. The adoptive parents adopted his biological siblings, eight-year-old twins. The foster child had been a foster child in the home of the parents prior to the placement of his sisters there, but was removed due to sexually inappropriate behavior and temper tantrums. The adoptive parents strongly opposed contact between the foster child and the twins. The foster child filed a petition seeking visitation with his biological siblings. The adoptive parents filed a motion to dismiss. The court granted the motion to dismiss, finding that even if the child's standing to apply for visitation had not been extinguished by the twins' adoption, he still had to establish that in the circumstances of the case equity would see fit to intervene. The court then found that the child had not met his first-step burden of showing a basis to overrule the adoptive parents' presumptively proper parental decision prohibiting visitation because he failed to show the existence of an ongoing and affectionate relationship with his siblings. Therefore, the court held that it would not proceed to the second step of conducting a best interest hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Visitation -- Visitation Rights of Siblings 

A petition seeking visitation between the 14-year-old petitioner and his 8-year-old twin sisters was dismissed where respondents, who had adopted the girls, objected to the proposed visitation. Petitioner, who had been a foster child in respondents' home but was removed owing to inappropriate behavior, failed to meet his initial burden of showing a basis to overrule the presumptively proper parental decision prohibiting visitation. Therefore, there was no reason to proceed to the next step of conducting a best interest hearing.

Kardas v. Union Carbide Corp.


Index No.: 9011/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY

1614 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wong


Docket No. 2003NY070859


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 274; 776 N.Y.S.2d 194; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 205



March 15, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motions ruled upon.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in an information with one count of stalking in the fourth degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 120.45(1). Defendant moved for an order dismissing the information for facial insufficiency under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.40. Defendant also challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 120.45.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the factual allegations in the information supporting the count of stalking in the fourth degree were sufficient to meet the reasonable cause and prima facie requirements of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.40. A fair reading of the information provided defendant with notice that he needed to defend against a charge that defendant had engaged in a course of conduct directed at the complainant, against her express wishes, and that the course of conduct was likely to cause reasonable fear of material harm. The fact that defendant was not charged with making any verbal threats did not make the information insufficient given the sheer number of unwelcome communications made to the complainant. The court found that N.Y. Penal Law § 120.45 did not violate defendant's free speech rights under the First and Fourteenth Amendments of the U.S. Constitution or under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8 because the criminal liability did not arise from the content of the speech but from defendant's alleged course of conduct.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information on the grounds of facial insufficiency and on constitutional grounds.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Stalking -- Facial Sufficiency 

1. The information charging defendant with fourth degree stalking for allegedly making multiple phone calls late at night to the complainant at hotels she hadn't told defendant she was staying at, sending her a room service meal and telling her by phone that he was at her hotel, sending multiple e-mails to her at work, following her from the subway toward her home for several blocks, following her from work and getting on the same subway and then off at her station, and leaving multiple copies of a magazine he worked for in front of her home was facially sufficient. Although it was not alleged that defendant made any verbal threats, the sheer number of communications and their context made defendant's course of conduct one which was likely to cause the complainant reasonable fear of material harm.

Crimes -- Stalking -- Constitutionality of Statute 

2. Penal Law § 120.45 (1), the fourth degree stalking statute, does not violate a defendant's right to freedom of speech under the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the US Constitution and article I, § 8 of the NY Constitution. Liability under the statute arises not from the content of any speech, but from a course of conduct likely to instill a fear of harm in the target of the conduct.

 

1615 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Diaz


Indictment No. 4735/01


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 686; 777 N.Y.S.2d 856; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 200



March 15, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Diaz, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1963 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Mar. 17, 2009)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Examination of the Defendant by the People's psychiatric expert found constitutional and not violative of Defendant's right against self-incrimination; the use of Defendant's statements given to the mental health expert limited to the issue of "extreme emotional disturbance" and no other use.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was indicted on various homicide counts, had been previously directed by the court to file a late notice of intent that he was claiming a defense of "extreme emotional disturbance" (EED) under N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25. The court then granted the People's request to have defendant examined by their psychological expert. Before the court was the admissibility and permissible scope of the People's expert's testimony.


OVERVIEW: Defendant strangled his former girlfriend, and, after the People made a Molineux application, defense counsel stated that defendant was claiming that the murder was done under circumstances evincing EED and that the defense would present only non-expert testimony on the issue. Establishing the parameters of the People's expert's testimony in light of defendant's Fifth Amendment rights against self-incrimination, the court held, inter alia, that (1) the examination of the expert could be conducted in front of the jury because, even if the court had not ordered the examination, the People could have had a mental health expert sit in the courtroom, cross-examined defendant, and then called the expert to testify; (2) the court-ordered examination was constitutional and did not violate the Fifth Amendment because the expert's testimony would only be admitted after defendant had in fact waived his Fifth Amendment right by testifying to a mental state or to facts from which the jury could infer a mental state defense; and (3) at this stage, the court did not need to decide whether the People could use a statement made to the People's expert for impeachment purposes.


OUTCOME: The court limited the prosecution's use of defendant's statements given to the mental health expert to the issue of "extreme emotional disturbance" and no other use.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Insanity -- Extreme Emotional Disturbance -- Defendant as Sole Witness 

Defendant in a homicide prosecution who intended to offer only his own testimony that he was acting under the influence of an extreme emotional disturbance at the time he killed his girlfriend was required to submit to a psychiatric examination by a prosecution expert. The court was authorized to order such an examination by its inherent power to achieve justice. Although the government's need for evidence cannot override a defendant's right against self-incrimination, if the defendant testifies as to his mental state at the time of the crime, he waives his Fifth Amendment rights on that issue. Accordingly, a court-ordered examination of defendant was constitutional and did not violate defendant's right against self-incrimination. The expert's testimony will only be admitted after the defendant has in fact waived his Fifth Amendment right by testifying to a mental state or to facts from which the jury can infer a mental state defense. If defendant places his mental state in issue, whether by offering an insanity defense or otherwise, the People's expert is limited to testifying to the mental state issue and no other.

 

1616 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garcia


Ind. No. 4439/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 699; 777 N.Y.S.2d 846; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 198



March 11, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Garcia, 812 N.Y.S.2d 66, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3714 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 28, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with numerous offenses in a violent outburst at the home of the victim and her children, moved to dismiss on grounds that the statute defining the offense was vague and overbroad. The count charged him with aggravated cruelty to a companion animal, in violation of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353-a.


OVERVIEW: Section 353-a, also called Buster's Law in honor of a cat that had been horribly tortured in a celebrated incident, made intentional acts of cruelty toward a companion animal a felony. Defendant had been charged with that offense, among others, after smashing a fish bowl that contained three goldfish belonging to the victim and her children. While the victim attempted to rescue the fish, defendant called the eldest child into the room and crushed the goldfish named for that child under his heel. The court held that a goldfish was clearly an animal within the meaning of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 350 and was also a companion animal within the meaning of that statute since the children cared for and named the goldfish, after themselves. Any person of ordinary understanding would have understood that the goldfish were pets protected against defendant's cruel behavior by the criminal statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Aggravated Cruelty to Animals -- Goldfish as "Domesticated Animal" 

1. A goldfish may qualify as a "domesticated animal" entitled to protection under Agriculture and Markets Law § 353-a, making aggravated cruelty to animals a felony. A protected animal must be cared for and maintained by human beings in or near a human household. There is no requirement that the domesticated animal be a mammal. It is not uncommon to see a goldfish as a child's pet, dutifully maintained and cared for by its young human owner. The statutory definition of "companion animal" uses ordinary terms to express ideas which find adequate interpretation in common usage. Both a potential offender of ordinary intelligence and a police officer would be adequately informed of the nature of the offense prohibited by the statute.

Crimes -- Aggravated Cruelty to Animals -- Goldfish as Household Pets 

2. In a prosecution for aggravated cruelty to animals pursuant to Agriculture and Markets Law § 353-a, the goldfish that defendant crushed under his heel after destroying its aquarium was a household pet entitled to protection under the statute. All three of the goldfish in the aquarium had names given to them by the children in the household and were cared for in the manner of pets. It is beyond cavil that a person of ordinary intelligence, including a police officer charged with enforcing the law, would know that the common household pet known as a goldfish is a companion animal intended to be protected under the statute, and that, in particular, the crushed goldfish was such a companion animal. There is no requirement that a protected animal must necessarily experience pain in order to come within the purview of the statute.

Crimes -- Aggravated Cruelty to Animals -- Goldfish as Household Pets -- Constitutionality of Statute 

3. Agriculture and Markets Law § 353-a, making it a felony to engage in aggravated cruelty to animals, is not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who destroyed an aquarium and crushed a pet goldfish under his heel, after summoning the pet's child owner into the room. Therefore defendant's as-applied and facial challenge to the statute must fail. People of ordinary intelligence understand that goldfish are pets, beloved by their owners, and are not to be killed or eaten. While humans can and do kill and eat fish on a regular basis, they do not do so if the fish at issue is a pet belonging to a young child. The statutory definition of "companion animal" does not require that the animal be trained or provide company and interaction with a human. Rather, the animal need only be cared for and maintained in or near the household of its human owner. Moreover, eating food fish is for a "justified purpose," which the statute permits. The sadistic actions defendant took against the goldfish in this case speak to defendant's understanding that he was killing a companion animal.

 

1617 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lugo


INDICT. NO. 2428/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


2 Misc. 3d 1003(A); 784 N.Y.S.2d 922; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 179; 2004 NY Slip Op 50126(U)



March 5, 2004, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence granted; indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a weapons possession case, defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence that was obtained by means of a warrantless search of his apartment.


OVERVIEW: A confidential informant told a police detective that defendant's son was making pipe bombs in defendant's apartment and that he possessed a handgun and was suicidal. The detective spent several hours talking with the informant and with his supervisors. Later that day, without obtaining a warrant, police entered defendant's apartment and conducted a search. An emergency team, an arson and explosion team, and a bomb squad with trained dogs searched the apartment and found no explosives of any kind. The detective then entered the apartment and found a handgun. Defendant and his family were taken to the precinct, where defendant signed statements admitting responsibility for the gun. The court, in granting the motion to suppress, concluded that there was no emergency such as would justify the warrantless entry. During the hours spent discussing the information, the police could have been preparing an affidavit and going to court for a search warrant. Moreover, by that time the detective entered, it was clear that no emergency existed. Defendant's statements were inextricably connected to the illegal search and, because his family was detained, were not shown to be voluntary.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence and dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

 [*1003A]  [**922] Crimes--Unlawful Search and Seizure.

 

1618 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Arroyo


Docket No. 2002KN021091


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 668; 777 N.Y.S.2d 836; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 175



March 4, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss granted; A.M.L. § 353 found unconstitutionally vague as applied to the facts of this case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with animal cruelty under N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353, sought dismissal of the information on the ground that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned a dog that was suffering from terminal cancer and had a large and apparently painful tumor. Defendant stated that he chose not to provide veterinary treatment for the dog because of his limited finances and because he believed that a natural death would be less painful for the dog than undergoing invasive surgery. The court determined that the plain meaning of the term "sustenance" in N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353 did not include veterinary care. Therefore, the provision making it a violation of the statute to fail to provide necessary sustenance did not afford notice to a person of ordinary intelligence that not providing veterinary care was a violation of the statute. Moreover, as used in N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 350, the term "unjustifiable physical pain" was too vague to warn pet owners that not providing veterinary care for their pets was a crime. The court concluded that a pet owner could not be prosecuted under N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353 for failing to provide a terminally ill pet with veterinary care.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Animals -- Cruelty to Animals -- "Necessary Sustenance" Not to Include Medical Care 

1. In a prosecution alleging that defendant failed to provide "necessary sustenance" to his dog within the meaning of Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 by failing to provide the terminally ill animal with medical care, the term "sustenance" as used in the statute does not include the provision of medical care. Statutory language is generally construed according to its natural and most obvious sense, without resorting to an artificial or forced construction. The dictionary defines sustenance as "means of sustaining life; food, victuals"; "a kind of quantity of food"; "nourishment"; and "the action of sustaining life by food." This comports with the popular meaning of the term as used in the vernacular, that is, that sustenance refers to food and nourishment, not medical care.

Animals -- Cruelty to Animals -- Provision of Medical Care to Ill Animals 

2. Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 was unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who failed to provide medical care to his terminally ill dog. The term "unjustifiable physical pain" is too vague to warn pet owners that not providing medical care for their pets is a crime. What is "unjustifiable" in the context of anticruelty statutes is what is not reasonable, defensible, right, unavoidable or excusable. Merely adding the term "unjustifiable" to the word "pain" does not transform conduct that is inherently innocent, like allowing an animal to die of natural causes without providing medical care, into a crime. Purely statutory offenses cannot be established by implication, and acts otherwise innocent and lawful do not become crimes, unless there is a clear and positive expression of the legislative intent to make them criminal. Neither society's current practice nor the moral standards of the community expand the meaning of the term "unjustifiable" to include a duty on owners to provide their animals with medical care.

People v. Saul


DOCKET NUMBER 2003CN007601


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 260; 776 N.Y.S.2d 189; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 97



February 19, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss, Dunaway motion, and motion to suppress are denied; the use of prior convictions or bad acts to cross-examine Defendant is referred to the Trial Court for resolution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with selling, on the street, playing cards without a general vendor's license in violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 20-453, and he moved to dismiss the complaint alleging that the facts alleged were insufficient to make out the crime charged and that U.S. Const. amend. I and N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 8 prohibited the State from requiring a license to sell "art."


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the playing cards were "art" entitled to constitutional protection because they bore the photographic images of military and political figures associated with the Iraqi war. Denying defendant's motion, the court held that (1) the factual allegations of the complaint, if true, were sufficient to establish that defendant was committing the crime of acting as an unlicensed general vendor; and (2) the playing cards allegedly offered for sale by defendant were not a form of written expression, such that defendant would not require a license by reason of the exemption contained within § 20-453, and they were not a form of non-verbal, artistic or visual expression, such that the license requirement of § 20-453 could not be constitutionally enforced against defendant, because there was nothing artistically noteworthy about the photographic images on the cards, nor did they constitute the seller's own artistic endeavor, and there was nothing about the cards which, reasonably interpreted, communicated ideas, opinions, emotions, or a point of view. The court also denied other branches of defendant's motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint and referred the use of prior convictions or bad acts to cross-examine defendant to the trial court for resolution.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- Street Vendors -- Selling Playing Cards 

1. In a prosecution for selling, on the street, playing cards without a general vendor's license in violation of a local law, the factual allegations of the complaint and the supporting deposition established, if true, every element of the crime charged. The defendant was engaged in offering for sale more than 10 decks of playing cards displayed on a table behind which defendant stood for five minutes. During that time he was seen exchanging the merchandise with one customer in return for currency. Further, defendant did not display the required license nor could he produce one when asked.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Selling Playing Cards without Vendor's License 

2. Defendant, who was selling playing cards bearing the photographic images of military and political figures associated with the war in Iraq, was not engaged in a form of protected expression that would make the application of the vendor's license requirement contained in a local ordinance unconstitutional as applied to him. The cards, in and of themselves, were not art, nor did they depict works of art. Moreover, the visual images on the cards did not constitute a form of nonverbal expression which, like books, pamphlets or other writings, effectively communicated ideas, concepts or emotions. There was nothing about the cards which, reasonably interpreted, communicated ideas, opinions, emotions or a point of view.

People v. Yafee


Docket No.: 2003SKN015823


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 367; 776 N.Y.S.2d 443; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 111



January 27, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Statute declared unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with selling beer at retail at 3:30 on a Sunday morning, in violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 105-a; defendant claimed that the statute violated the Establishment Clause.


OVERVIEW: The court held that there was no secular reason why beer could not be sold on Sunday morning as opposed to any other morning. Other than an entanglement with religion in violation of the Establishment Clause, there was no rational basis for mandating Sunday as a day of rest as opposed to any other day. Thus, the constitutionality of § 105-a failed on all prongs of the Lemon/Agostini test. The statute became more onerous when alternative and less intrusive government regulation was considered. There was no reason why defendant should not be able to designate with the State Liquor Authority the day in which beer would not be sold for extended hours.


OUTCOME: The charges were dismissed and the statute was declared unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Sale on Sunday 

 Alcoholic Beverage Control Law § 105-a, a "Sunday Blue Law" prohibiting the retail sale of beer on Sunday between 3:00 a.m. and 12:00 p.m., is unconstitutional. Statutes which touch religion, as Blue Laws do, must have a secular purpose if they are to be permissible under the Establishment Clause (US Const 1st Amend). There is no secular reason why beer cannot be sold on Sunday morning as opposed to any other morning.

People v. Bigger


CR#: 24885-03


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, TOWN OF WEBSTER, MONROE COUNTY


2 Misc. 3d 937; 771 N.Y.S.2d 826; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 19



January 10, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Court dismissed the charges against the Defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving while intoxicated, per se, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law. § 1192(2), and common law driving while intoxicated, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law. § 1192(3). The motion to suppress the evidence in the matter, as being obtained as a result of an unconstitutional sobriety checkpoint, was denied. However, the court reserved on the issue of reasonable cause to stop defendant.


OVERVIEW: The officer who stopped defendant was not part of a sobriety checkpoint, and the officer stopped defendant's vehicle on the mere supposition that defendant was attempting to evade the sobriety checkpoint. Thus, the court concluded that the stop was flawed on three counts. First, the officer did not have an objective articulable reason to stop defendant's vehicle. Thus, he lacked "reasonable suspicion" as required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.50(1) to stop the vehicle in question. Second, the officer executing the stop was not part of the sobriety checkpoint. Third, there was no written established procedure for the stopping of vehicles that appeared to be avoiding said sobriety checkpoint. Therefore, the charges against defendant were dismissed, because the stop of defendant's vehicle was not justified.


OUTCOME: The charges against defendant were dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs -- Stop of Vehicle Suspected of Avoiding Sobriety Checkpoint 

A charge of driving while intoxicated, arising out of the stop of a vehicle by a police officer who suspected that defendant was attempting to avoid a sobriety checkpoint, was dismissed because the stop was not justified. The officer did not have an objective articulable reason to stop the defendant's vehicle and thus lacked "reasonable suspicion" as required by CPL 140.50 (1). Further, the officer who executed the stop was not part of the sobriety checkpoint. Moreover, there was no written established procedure for the stopping of vehicles that appeared to be avoiding the sobriety checkpoint.

 

1624 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Orange & Rockland Utils. v. Vill. of Kiryas Joel


Index No. 4890/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ORANGE COUNTY


3 Misc. 3d 201; 771 N.Y.S.2d 819; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 46



January 5, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's motion granted; petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility company sought a judgment setting aside a tax invoice imposed by respondent village. The village moved to dismiss the N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The village began a walkway project that required the relocation of the company's utility poles. The village told the company of its responsibility for the costs and the company eventually paid the costs, with a reservation of rights and under protest. The village's board of trustees then passed a local law that established assessments for the removal of obstructions. The company was assessed its special franchise assessment and after it failed to appear at a hearing, the amount to move the poles was assessed against it. The court first held that the village's local law was not inconsistent with New York law because it imposed a special assessment and not a tax. The assessment was not discriminatory because the local law was in conformity with the provisions of general law and did not arbitrarily or unreasonably "single out" a specific entity or group, especially where it was a general principle that utility companies were to bear the cost of relocation in matters of health, safety, and welfare. Finally, the company had failed to exhaust its administrative remedies by failing to timely challenge the village's authority to impose the costs and by not attending the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted the village's motion and dismissed the petition in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Validity -- Village Law -- Tax on Public Utility 

1. In a proceeding challenging the validity of a tax invoice whereby a village sought payment for the cost of relocating utility poles as part of a pedestrian walkway renovation project, the local law pursuant to which the invoice was issued carries a presumption of constitutionality and petitioner utility bears the burden of demonstrating beyond a reasonable doubt that it is unconstitutional. Although petitioner maintained that the law contravened Village Law § 5-530, which authorizes taxes on public utilities based only upon gross income or gross operating income, the local law in issue did not impose a tax, but rather a special assessment. As such, there was no inconsistency.

Public Utilities -- Relocation 

2. In a proceeding challenging the validity of a tax invoice whereby a village sought payment for the cost of relocating utility poles as part of a pedestrian walkway renovation project, the village's imposition of the special assessment did not unfairly discriminate against petitioner by shifting to it costs that should be borne by the taxpayer. Highway Law § 319, which imposes upon the owner of a permanent highway obstruction the cost of its removal, is applicable to villages as well as to towns, and as a general principle of law utility companies must bear the cost of relocation in matters relating to health, safety and welfare. The privilege granted to a utility to maintain its facilities in public streets gives the utility no property interest in the right-of-way, only a license to maintain its facilities there.

 

1625 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Caflisch v. Crotty


Index No. 2003-1579


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, CHEMUNG COUNTY


2 Misc. 3d 786; 774 N.Y.S.2d 653; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1681; 161 Oil & Gas Rep. 779



December 31, 2003, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition/complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowners brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding challenging a decision made by respondent Commissioner of the New York Department of Environmental Conservation, holding that the landowners were not entitled to a 7/8 interest in production revenue from oil and gas leases, but that they were entitled to receive a 1/8 royalty payment. The landowners also sought a declaratory judgment directing the entitlement to future royalties.


OVERVIEW: The landowners argued that the Commissioner's decision was contrary to N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 23-0901(3). The reviewing court held that the Commissioner did not violate any of the public hearing provisions or the procedures for compulsory integration. The determination that the landowners were only entitled to a 1/8 working interest was not arbitrary and capricious or unreasonable. The Commissioner's application of § 23-0901(3) was supported by statutory construction, resulted in a reasonable allocation of production revenues, and was consistent with the Commissioner's prior rulings. The owners were not owners under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 23-0101(11), as they did not have or qualify for a drilling permit, and they were not operators. The Commissioner's interpretation of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 23-0901(3) was consistent with the rule of capture. Deference was given to the Commissioner's ruling as the Department was entrusted with the administration of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 23, which required specialized knowledge and expertise. Finally, a declaratory judgment action was not the proper method of challenging an administrative determination.


OUTCOME: The petition/complaint was dismissed in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Gas and Oil -- Gas and Oil Lease -- Entitlement to Production Revenue 

The determination of the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation that petitioners, in their capacity as landowner and/or lessee in connection with certain oil and gas leases, were not entitled to a seven-eighths interest in the production revenue allocated to the parcel in which they had an interest, was not arbitrary and capricious, irrational or unreasonable. Petitioners' interest in the subject drilling unit comprised .30% of the unit's total acreage. Neither petitioner participated in or contributed to the costs of exploration or drilling, qualified for or was granted a drilling permit, or entered into voluntary integration in connection with the drilling unit (see ECL 23-0701). Petitioners did not qualify as an owner within the meaning of ECL 23-0901 (3), which would have entitled them to a seven-eighths working interest in the production revenue, since neither was granted a drilling permit or controlled enough acreage to qualify for one. Further, the Commissioner's interpretation of the provisions of 23-0901 (3) was consistent with the common law of New York which adheres to the rule of capture, i.e., title to subsurface oil and gas vests in the party which first brings it to the surface and reduces it to possession. Moreover, the Commissioner's interpretation and application of the provisions of ECL 23-0901 (3) was consistent with prior rulings of the Commissioner.

New York v. Hawkins


DOCKET NUMBER 2003NY061559


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


1 Misc. 3d 905(A); 781 N.Y.S.2d 627; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1806; 2003 NY Slip Op 51516(U)



December 18, 2003, Decision Date


NOTICE:  [***1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE PRINTED OFFICIAL REPORTS.



DISPOSITION: Motions ruled upon.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charges against him for facial insufficiency. Defendant was charged with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.01(1), loitering for the purpose of gambling, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2), and disorderly conduct, in violation of § 240.20(5). Defendant's motion also sought other relief.


OVERVIEW: The charges arose from defendant having been seen at the same place where dice were observed. Defendant asserted that he was merely watching. The complaint did not allege facts that anything of value was risked or any wagering occurred. Defendant's motion also sought the suppression of statement evidence or, alternatively, a Dunaway/Huntley hearing; preclusion of any unnoticed statement or identification evidence; preclusion of the use at trial of defendant's prior criminal history or misconduct; and discovery. The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss as to the charge of loitering for the purpose of gambling, because there was nothing in the complaint from which it was reasonably inferable that defendant was engaged in loitering for the purpose of gambling based on his mere presence at the place where dice were being rolled. The court found defendant's intent to cause a disturbance was inferred from his obstruction of pedestrian traffic. Further, the gravity knife recovered from defendant's pant's pocket opened and locked when flipped, which established an evidentiary basis to conclude that the knife recovered from defendant was a gravity knife.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss regarding the loitering charge and denied it as to the disorderly conduct and possession charges. The court granted defendant's motion to suppress or, alternatively, for a Dunaway/Mapp/Huntley hearing; his motion to preclude any unnoticed statement or identification evidence; and his motion for discovery. Preclusion or use of defendant's prior criminal history was referred to the trial court.


HEADNOTES

 [*905A]  [**627] Crimes--Information.

 

1628 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


475 Ninth Ave. Assocs. LLC v. Bloomberg


Index. No. 113914/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2 Misc. 3d 597; 773 N.Y.S.2d 790; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1579



December 2, 2003, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion for injunction denied and dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner initiated a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 challenge to the decision of respondent city officials to construct a permanent pedestrian barricade near its building, and sought a preliminary injunction. The owner argued that no environmental quality review had occurred, that the officials had exceeded their powers, and that it had been deprived of the value of its property.


OVERVIEW: The officials decided to erect a barricade in the interests of pedestrian safety in order to prevent foot traffic across a ramp leading into a heavily traveled tunnel. The owner claimed that this reduction in pedestrian access caused it to lose several valuable tenants. The court held, first, that environmental quality review was not needed in the case of a Type II action involving control of traffic control devices, including barricades. Moreover, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1640(3) empowered the officials to prohibit, restrict, or regulate pedestrian use of highways in the city. Since the owner had not shown a property interest in the barricaded area, there was no taking in violation of due process rights or eminent domain laws. The record showed that the officials had made an extensive study of pedestrian traffic safety matters and considered various alternatives, so there was no basis to allege arbitrary and capricious action. Finally, since the owner's only alleged losses were financial, preliminary injunctive relief was not appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court denied the owner's motion for preliminary injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Environmental Quality Review--Installation of Traffic Control Devices--Construction of Permanent Pedestrian Barricade on Public Street 

1. Respondents were not required to prepare an environmental impact study prior to constructing a permanent pedestrian barricade on a public street adjacent to petitioner's mixed use building in order to prevent pedestrians from crossing a dangerous vehicular traffic entrance to a tunnel. The pedestrian barricade is a "traffic control device" erected by respondents "for the purpose of regulating, warning or guiding traffic" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 153), and is thus exempt from environmental review (6 NYCRR 617.5 [c] [16]).

Municipal Corporations -- Sidewalks -- Construction of Permanent Pedestrian Barricade on Public Street 

2. Respondents did not exceed their statutory authority in constructing a permanent pedestrian barricade on a public street adjacent to petitioner's mixed use building in order to prevent pedestrians from crossing a dangerous vehicular traffic entrance to a tunnel. Respondents' determination to install the pedestrian barricade as a "traffic control device" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 153) was properly made pursuant to their authority to "prohibit, restrict, or regulate traffic on or pedestrian use of any highway" in New York City and to otherwise "regulate the crossing of any roadway by pedestrians" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1642 [a]; § 1640 [a] [3]). Furthermore, respondents' determination had a rational basis and was not arbitrary or capricious. Respondents weighed alternatives such as the placement and use of signs or a traffic light before determining that the pedestrian barricade was the most effective means of alleviating the dangers posed to pedestrians from vehicles turning onto the entrance ramp.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Right to Jury Trial -- Challenge to Construction of Permanent Pedestrian Barricade on Public Street 

3. Petitioner was not entitled to a jury trial in a CPLR article 78 proceeding challenging respondents' determination to construct a permanent pedestrian barricade on a public street adjacent to petitioner's mixed use building for safety purposes. The issues raised by petitioner as to whether respondents were required to conduct an environmental impact study and whether respondents acted in excess of their jurisdiction presented questions of law to be determined by the court.

Eminent Domain -- De Facto Appropriation -- Construction of Permanent Pedestrian Barricade on Public Street -- Due Process 

4. Respondents did not violate due process by failing to provide notice, public hearings or publication of any relevant determination or fact-findings prior to constructing a permanent pedestrian barricade on a public street adjacent to petitioner's mixed use building for safety purposes. Petitioner was not deprived of a property interest which warranted due process protection. Furthermore, petitioner failed to demonstrate a "de facto" taking without just compensation which deprived it of all economically viable use of its property (see EDPL 104). Petitioner's possible loss of rental income from commercial ground floor retail tenants because of the construction of the pedestrian barricade did not constitute a compensable taking.

People v. Ogarra


Indictment No. 8338/98
