1661 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kopp


Indictment No. 98-2555-S01


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


195 Misc. 2d 475; 756 N.Y.S.2d 830; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 211



February 6, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Kopp, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9002 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, July 7, 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In defendant's prosecution for second degree murder, intervenor media organizations sought permission to take still photography or to record and broadcast the proceedings, and a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52, regarding media coverage of judicial proceedings, was unconstitutional. The State and amicus curiae United States objected.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 prohibited televising, broadcasting, or taking motion pictures of judicial proceedings. The legislature had experimented with allowing cameras in the courts, but those experiments were ended and § 52 was allowed to once again take effect. The public's right of access to a criminal trial was not absolute, and defendant's right to a public trial did not encompass the use of cameras in the courtroom. The media organizations did not show they had a constitutional right to bring cameras into the courtroom. The legislature clearly intended to ban cameras from the courts. While cameras could be constitutionally permitted in the courtroom, the ban on their use was not unconstitutional. The fact that neither defendant nor the People objected to video coverage was not controlling. Section 52's ban did not apply to still photography, and such photography would not adversely affect defendant's rights to a fair trial and due process. The use of still cameras was governed by N.Y. Stands. & Admin. Policies, R. Chief Adm'r Cts. § 131.


OUTCOME: The applications for declaratory judgment were denied and dismissed and permission to videotape and/or televise defendant's judicial proceedings was denied. The application to allow still photography of the proceedings was granted, subject to further order.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Audiovisual Coverage of Judicial Proceedings - Still Photographic Coverage of Murder Trial Permitted

 Civil Rights Law § 52, which prohibits the "televising, broadcasting, or taking of motion pictures" in New York courtrooms, is constitutional. There is no federal or state constitutional right to televise or videotape trial proceedings. The right to a public trial does not encompass the use of video cameras in the courtroom. However, still photography is not covered by the statutory prohibition. The trial judge has broad discretion to determine whether to permit still photography in the courtroom (see Rules of Chief Administrator of Courts [22 NYCRR] part 131). Accordingly, still photographic coverage will be permitted during defendant's murder trial for the shooting death of an abortion provider, subject to any reasonable restrictions the court may impose after consultation with the applicant and the parties.

 

1662 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


N.Y. County Lawyers' Ass'n v. State


Index No. 102987/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


196 Misc. 2d 761; 763 N.Y.S.2d 397; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 453



February 5, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Counsel Amended May 13, 2003. As Corrected August 13, 2003.
Appeal withdrawn by N.Y. County Lawyers' Ass'n v. State, 2 A.D.3d 1489, 767 N.Y.S.2d 603, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12728 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 2, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff's motion for a permanent injunction granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lawyers' association filed an action seeking a declaratory judgment and a motion for a permanent injunction against defendants, the City and the State of New York, for their failure to increase the compensation rates for assigned counsel with respect to portions of N.Y. County Law § 722-b, N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 245, and N.Y. Jud. Law § 35, which allegedly caused ineffective representation.


OVERVIEW: The declaratory judgment action alleged that due to inadequate compensation fees paid to assigned counsel in criminal and family court matters, there was a shortage of available attorneys to be assigned. This resulted in a lack of counsel in many matters and at many stages of those types of proceedings, including juvenile and parental rights termination matters. The court found that this resulted in an obstruction of the judiciary's ability to function, and accordingly, it held that N.Y. County Law § 722-b, N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 245, and N.Y. Jud. Law § 35 were unconstitutional as applied to assigned counsel cases because assigned counsel was necessary and there was an insufficient number available, which resulted in delays and denials of counsel, all of which was directly due to the current inadequate compensation. The court directed a set amount paid per hour, without distinction between in-court and out-of-court work, and without caps on each case, pending further legislative action to correct the situation. The court noted that an adequate compensation scheme with set increases within a statute would prevent this type of situation. The elements for an injunction were satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court granted the association's motion and issued a mandatory permanent injunction and directed a payment of $ 90 per hour to assigned counsel, without distinction between in-court and out-of-court work, and without a cap on each case, pending further action on the issue by the legislature.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client--Assignment of Counsel--Compensation

The statutory compensation rates paid to assigned counsel in criminal matters and Family Court proceedings (County Law § 722-b; Family Ct Act § 245; Judiciary Law § 35) are unconstitutional as applied to the representation of children and indigent adults in the City of New York. The State's failure to increase the compensation rates for assigned counsel since 1985 violates the constitutional and statutory right to meaningful and effective representation. The current inadequate statutory rates have resulted in a critical shortage of assigned counsel, which in turn has resulted in denial of counsel, delay in proceedings and excessive case loads, thereby seriously impairing the ability of the courts to function. Until the Legislature addresses the issue, plaintiff bar association is granted a mandatory permanent injunction raising the compensation rates to $90 an hour, consistent with the federal assigned counsel rate, without a ceiling on total per case compensation or a distinction between in- and out-of-court work. The distinction between the compensation for in- and out-of-court work creates an economic disincentive for lawyers to perform adequate investigations and seek speedy disposition of all cases.

 

1663 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Donaldson


Indictment No. 8443/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


194 Misc. 2d 614; 755 N.Y.S.2d 564; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 63



January 16, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended February 20, 2003. Counsel Amended February 25, 2003.
Subsequent appeal at People v. Donaldson, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11377 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Oct. 24, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to set aside a verdict of guilty to depraved indifference murder, based on his claims that N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25 was unconstitutional as applied to him and that his double jeopardy or collateral estoppel rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: The Grand Jury determined that defendant should not be indicted for intentional murder, but an indictment was issued for depraved indifference murder. Evidence at trial revealed that the dead body of the victim was found, that the death was caused by a combination of beating and asphyxiation, and that defendant admitted that he lost control and could not think properly. Defendant was convicted, but sentence had not yet been determined. The court denied defendant's post-trial motion to set aside the guilty verdict, holding that double jeopardy did not apply to Grand Jury proceedings and that collateral estoppel did not apply to a vote of a "no true bill" by a Grand Jury. The court noted that defendant's "as applied" challenge to equal protection was to be determined on a case by case basis and found no merit to the claim. The court disagreed with the federal decision of Jones v. Keane and held that identical elements of two types of homicide did not constitute a violation of equal protection, absent a showing of actual racial or other discrimination based upon a suspect classification, which was not found in the instant matter.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Depraved Indifference Murder - Reckless Manslaughter

Merely because reckless manslaughter and depraved indifference murder are similar, their alleged identical elements do not violate equal protection guarantees. Differentiating those two different degrees of homicide has never been a problem; nor has there been any abuse by the prosecution or a jury in alleging or finding depraved indifference homicide where intentional homicide is warranted. Accordingly, in a homicide prosecution in which defendant was found guilty of depraved indifference murder, defendant's motion to set aside the verdict is denied. Defendant's beating of the victim and the act causing his asphyxiation clearly could have been reckless in light of the defendant's statements that he lost control and could not think properly. There is nothing unreasonable or vague that would not give a reasonable person notice that those circumstances create a grave risk of death.

 

1664 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Orendorff v. B.P.O.E. Lodge No. 96


Index No. 01-01812, RJI No. 32-01-0847


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ONEIDA COUNTY


195 Misc. 2d 53; 753 N.Y.S.2d 703; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 40



January 9, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Counsel Amended February 4, 2003. As Amended March 31, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Matter remanded to the DHR with instructions; Article 78 proceeding against the Elks Lodge dismissed without prejudice as premature.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a woman, sought to challenge in an N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding a decision of respondent New York State Division of Human Rights (DHR) that dismissed her sex discrimination complaint against respondent, a local lodge of a national benevolent order, with a finding of no probable cause after investigation.


OVERVIEW: When the all-male lodge turned down her membership application, the woman argued that although it was a private club exempt under N.Y. Exec. Law § 292(9) from discrimination legislation, the lodge was not following the policies of its own national organization. She filed a complaint with DHR, which later conceded that it had never investigated her complaint, since it lacked jurisdiction over the lodge. The decision communicated to the woman, however, was that her claim had failed on the merits. The court held that the failure to advise the woman of the real reason for dismissal, and to advise her that her election of an administrative remedy under N.Y. Exec. Law § 297(9) had been annulled (which would have enabled her to pursue a judicial remedy), made the administrative decision arbitrary and capricious. For the time being, a judicial remedy was premature, because until a proper dismissal for lack of jurisdiction occurred, the administrative proceeding would not be properly concluded.


OUTCOME: The court set aside DHR's decision and ordered it to issue a determination that the woman's previous election of remedies was annulled because of DHR's lack of jurisdiction over her complaint. It dismissed without prejudice her administrative review proceeding against the lodge, as premature.


HEADNOTES

Civil Rights - State Division of Human Rights - Notice of Dismissal of Discrimination Complaint on Jurisdictional Grounds

Respondent State Division of Human Rights acted arbitrarily and capriciously when it notified petitioner that it was dismissing her gender discrimination claim against a benevolent order based on a finding of no probable cause, instead of for lack of jurisdiction by reason of the statutory privacy exemption for benevolent orders (Executive Law § 292 [9]). Respondent has an affirmative duty to clearly and specifically advise a petitioner that a determination dismissing a complaint is jurisdictional and not on the merits. Consequently, respondent was required to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction, and to issue an annulment of the election of remedies (Executive Law § 297 [9]), thereby allowing petitioner to pursue other remedies. The statute of limitations for commencing a CPLR article 78 proceeding will be tolled until respondent annuls the election of remedies, leaving petitioner several weeks for the commencement of a timely proceeding against the benevolent order.

 

1665 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Dolan v. Linnen


L & T Index No. 05826/02, L & T Index No. 086018/02


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, RICHMOND COUNTY


195 Misc. 2d 298; 753 N.Y.S.2d 682; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 76



January 1, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 22, 2003. As Corrected May 6, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Default money judgments granted for the arrears demanded in a nonpayment proceeding and a holdover proceeding against tenants who each defaulted after receiving petition and notice of petition by substituted or duly diligent conspicuous-place service.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff first landlord brought a nonpayment proceeding against defendant first tenant, and plaintiff second landlord brought a holdover proceeding against defendant second tenant. Both tenants defaulted, and the landlords each sought a money judgment by default against their respective tenants under N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law § 732(3). The instant court consolidated the cases for purposes of its opinion.


OVERVIEW: After four duly diligent but failed attempts at personal service, a process server affected conspicuous service by affixing a petition and notice of petition on the first tenant's door, and she was sent the pleadings by first class and certified mail. She failed to answer. An order granting possession but not money was entered, but a warrant of eviction was never executed. After a process server served the first tenant, the first landlord moved for a money judgment. The court held the first landlord had been entitled to judgment all along, because when conspicuous service conformed to the due-diligence standard of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(4) and N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law § 735(1), the landlord was not obliged to bring separate proceedings for possession and money. The second landlord's process server served the petition and notice of petition under § 735(1) by substitute service -- that is, on a person of "suitable age and discretion" living at the second tenant's home. The court held that as substituted service equaled in-hand personal delivery in summary proceedings, the court had personal jurisdiction to grant a default money judgment for the arrears after the tenant defaulted.


OUTCOME: The court awarded a money judgment for the rent, but only the rent, demanded in the first landlord's petition. The court awarded only the rent demanded in the second landlord's nonpayment petition and the use and occupancy through the holdover inquest date.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Summary Proceedings - Default Money Judgment Allowed for Substituted or Duly Diligent Conspicuous Service

In order to avoid unnecessary duplicative litigation, a court in a nonpayment summary proceeding may award a money judgment against a tenant who defaults after receiving a petition and notice of petition by substituted or duly diligent conspicuous-place service in accordance with CPLR 308 and RPAPL 735 (1). Substituted and duly diligent conspicuous service are the equivalent of personal delivery in summary proceedings, and are thus sufficient to confer personal jurisdiction to support a money judgment against a defaulting tenant. No constitutional, statutory or practical reason prevents substituted service and conspicuous service from conferring personal jurisdiction in summary proceedings. Consequently, when substituted service and conspicuous service conform to CPLR 308 and RPAPL 735 (1), a landlord need not bring separate proceedings for possession and money or use and occupancy. However, the money judgment is limited to the amount demanded in the petition and cannot include legal or other fees.

 

1666 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Hinspeter v. Senkowski


Index No. 10255/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


194 Misc. 2d 302; 752 N.Y.S.2d 821; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1591



December 11, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended January 3, 2003. Counsel Amended January 7, 2003.
Affirmed by People ex rel. Hinspeter v. Senkowski, 783 N.Y.S.2d 386, 2004 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11557 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a habeas corpus action, petitioner asked the trial court to declare N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.50 unconstitutional on the grounds that it violated petitioner's rights to due process of law and equal protection of the law under both the federal and state constitutions because it deprived him of the right to bail or release on his own recognizance pending appeal from his conviction of several sexual offenses committed against a child.


OVERVIEW: From the time of his arrest on sexual assault charges involving a child-victim, through the conclusion of his trial, petitioner was free on bail and appeared in court as required. He was immediately remanded, without bail, upon the jury's return of guilty verdicts on 14 of the 36 counts of the indictment, including those charging him with rape, sodomy, aggravated sexual abuse, and sexual abuse. Petitioner then commenced a habeas corpus. The trial court found that it was evident that based upon the data included in the legislative history, the question of whether the recidivism rate for individuals convicted of violent sexual offenses against young victims was so substantial that denial of release of such individuals pending appeal was justified was at least a debatable question. As such, petitioner did not sustain his burden of showing that varying treatment of a group was totally unrelated to the legislation. Consequently, his equal protection attack failed. Finally, it could not be credibly denied that protecting young victims from violent sexual assaults was a legitimate legislative goal.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.


HEADNOTES

Bail - Excessive Bail - Denial of Postconviction Bail to Defendants Convicted of Felony Sexual Assault against Minor

1. CPL 530.50, which denies postconviction bail to defendants convicted of a felony sexual assault against a victim less than 18 years old, is not violative of the Eighth Amendment's proscription against excessive bail. There is no federal constitutional right to bail pending appeal after a state court conviction. Consequently, defendant's Eighth Amendment rights have not been violated by the denial of bail following his conviction of sexual felonies involving a child victim. Furthermore, defendant is not entitled to release on bail pending appeal under the Federal Bail Reform Act (18 USC § 3143), since the federal statute applies only to federal prosecutions and is thus inapplicable to state court proceedings.

Bail - Right to Bail - Constitutionality of Denial of Postconviction Bail to Defendants Convicted of Felony Sexual Assault against Minor

2. CPL 530.50, which denies postconviction bail to only those defendants convicted of a felony sexual assault against a victim less than 18 years old, is not unconstitutional as violative of equal protection of the laws under the "rational relation" test. In view of the heightened recidivism rate among convicted pedophiles and the high incidence of child victims among victims of serious sexual crimes in general, the statutory classification is rationally related to the legitimate governmental interest of protecting children, a special class of victims, from becoming future victims of violent sexual offenders awaiting appellate review of their convictions. The fact that the Legislature chose to limit the statute to a small class of convicted felons rather than denying bail to all convicted felons does not render the statute unconstitutional. Since the potential for recidivist behavior by sexual predators is not "wholly irrelevant" to the Legislature's goal of protecting young children from becoming victims of sexual predators, the legislative decision to deny sexual predators postconviction release does not violate their equal protection rights. Furthermore, since there is no constitutional right to bail pending appeal, the statute is not violative of due process.

George v. City of Albany


INDEX NO. SC 02-0591


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY


195 Misc. 2d 544; 760 N.Y.S.2d 624; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1965



December 2, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Amended May 23, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Judgment entered in favor of plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner sued defendant city, seeking to recover $ 774 which the city charged him for clean-up of his property.


OVERVIEW: The city had posted a notice on the door of the property, stating that the condition of the property required it to be cleaned up, and that if the property was not cleaned up within 24 hours, the city would clean up the property and charge the property owner the cost of doing so. Albany, N.Y., Code § 313-40 authorized this procedure. The court ruled that notice was an essential element of due process of law. The notice needed to be reasonably calculated, under the circumstances, to apprise interested parties of the pendency of the action or proceeding and to afford them an opportunity to present their objections. The court held that Albany, N.Y., Code § 313-40 did not take into account the possibility that the property owner did not reside at the property in question. Additionally, 24 hours notice to the property owner, even if he/she resided at the property in question, was not reasonably calculated to apprise the owner of the claims and intentions of the city and to afford the owner an opportunity to present his/her objections. Thus, Albany, N.Y., Code § 313-40 was unconstitutional as it failed to provide reasonable notice and due process of law to a property owner.


OUTCOME: Judgment was entered in favor of the property owner and against the city in the sum of $ 774.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Ordinance Authorizing Municipality to Charge Property Owner for "Clean Up" Costs - Sufficiency of Prior Notice

Plaintiff may recover the "clean up" costs defendant municipality assessed against his property in view of the inadequate prior notices provided by defendant pursuant to municipal ordinance. The ordinance, which authorizes defendant to charge a property owner for work necessary to correct a violation "detrimental to the public health ... after giving the property owner ... 24 hours' notice, in writing, posted on the property, of the condition of the property" to be corrected, is unconstitutional. It fails to provide reasonable notice and due process of law to a property owner with regard to the City's findings, and fails to afford the owner the opportunity to present objections and/or correct the problem. Here, plaintiff never received the notices posted on the property prior to defendant's "clean up," because the property was not his legal residence. Moreover, even if the property owner lives at the property in question, 24 hours' notice is not enough time to respond to the notice.

DEZER ENTERTAINMENT CONCEPTS, INC. v. CITY OF NEW YORK

1670 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


GH Ville Inc. v. N.Y. City Envtl. Control Bd.


Index No. 116307/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


194 Misc. 2d 503; 755 N.Y.S.2d 784; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1724



November 15, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Counsel Amended February 11, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Relief granted in part; determination of the ECB vacated; the ruling of the ALJ set aside; amount paid as penalty set by the ALJ ordered returned.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 which sought to set aside an order of respondent New York City Environmental Control Board (New York), made through its Motions and Appeals Unit. The order found that the owner had committed a noise violation of § 24-220(b) of the New York City Administrative Code, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 24, ch. 2, § 220.


OVERVIEW: The property owner operated a restaurant and was issued a notice of violation of § 24-220(b) of the New York City Administrative Code, based on a noise complaint. The record revealed that the inspector had testified before the administrative law judge (ALJ) that he could hear music emanating from the restaurant while standing on the sidewalk. The restaurant owner indicated that there was music inside and that it could be heard outside but not from the street. The ALJ determined that a violation had occurred, which was affirmed on administrative appeal to the Board. On further review, the court found that the decision was arbitrary and capricious because two of the five elements necessary to establish such a violation had not been proved. The court found that the owner had successfully rebutted the Board's presumption that the music was for a commercial or business advertising purpose by showing that it was being played for the enjoyment of the patrons and not to attract customers. Additionally, the speakers and sound equipment were not in front of or outside of the building, as required under the Code. Accordingly, there was no proved violation.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and set aside the determination and directed return of the penalty to the owner.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Noise Control - Sound Reproduction Devices - Presumptions

The determination of respondent New York City Environmental Control Board finding that petitioner's restaurant illegally used a "sound reproduction device for commercial or business advertising purposes" by permitting music emanating from speakers located inside the restaurant to be heard upon a public street in violation of the City's noise control ordinance (Administrative Code of City of NY § 24-220) is vacated as being arbitrary and capricious. Any presumption that music made by a sound reproduction device played within a commercial establishment is intended to be for a commercial or business advertising purpose was sufficiently rebutted by petitioner's assertion that music was played for the enjoyment of the patrons and not to attract customers. Respondent cannot rely on a further presumption that the fact that music was permitted to be heard on the street established a business or advertising purpose. Furthermore, respondent failed to demonstrate that there was a device "in front or outside the building," or that the music was heard "in or through any aperture" of the building as required by the ordinance. The purpose of the ordinance was to ban outside loudspeakers and to prevent the circumvention of such ban by also banning inside loudspeakers playing through a hole in the wall or window or door of the premises.

People v. Smith


INDICTMENT 01-2449-001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 462; 751 N.Y.S.2d 356; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1475



November 6, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Amended November 27, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Previous order of this court dated 10/30/2002 reaffirmed and supplemented.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to have the court (New York) reconsider its decision that convicted him of murder, claiming, inter alia, that the decision was unconstitutional, and that the prosecution had an affirmative obligation to prove that he was not mentally retarded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.27(12) was constitutionally infirm as a result of decisions by the United States Supreme Court. The court held that defendant's arguments were premised upon the misconception that the prosecution had an affirmative obligation to prove that he was not mentally retarded. In addition, defendants arguments concerning jurors and jury instructions were based upon pure, unsubstantiated speculation. Finally if a pre-trial mental retardation hearing was held pursuant to § 400.27(12)(e), the court could not thereafter conduct a post-conviction hearing pursuant to § 400.27-12(a) revisiting the issues decided therein.


OUTCOME: To the extent that the order could be construed to allow mental retardation hearings under two statutory subdivisions, they were precluded. The order was affirmed in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute 

1. Atkins v Virginia (536 US 304, 153 L. Ed. 2d 335, 122 S. Ct. 2242) and Ring v Arizona (536 US 584, 153 L. Ed. 2d 556, 122 S. Ct. 2428), which provide that the prosecution in a capital murder case has an affirmative obligation to prove that a defendant is not mentally retarded, do not render CPL 400.27 (12), which provides procedures for determining a sentence upon conviction of first degree murder, constitutionally infirm. This would be tantamount to requiring the prosecution to prove beyond a reasonable doubt the absence of any factor which would render the defendant ineligible for the death penalty. Such is not constitutionally mandated.

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Mental Retardation Hearing

2. In a capital murder trial, if a pretrial mental retardation hearing is held pursuant to CPL 400.27 (12) (e), the court may not thereafter conduct a postconviction hearing pursuant to CPL 400.27 (12) (a) revisiting the issues decided therein.

Anonymous v. Office of Prof'l Med. Conduct


Index No. 115505/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


194 Misc. 2d 433; 755 N.Y.S.2d 551; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1702



November 4, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Counsel Amended February 4, 2003. Case Name and Opinion Amended March 11, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Application denied; petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician anonymously sought a judgment to determine that respondent disciplinary body's determination to publish certain charges issued in connection with his disciplinary proceeding was violative of lawful procedure, arbitrary and capricious, and an abuse of discretion, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7803, and thus it should be ordered to withdraw publication.


OVERVIEW: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, the physician argued that professional disciplinary proceedings were confidential and that publication of charges against him, including the charges that were not sustained, was a violation of the confidentiality laws, and impermissibly subjected his professional reputation to irreparable harm. Alternatively, he requested that if the court should agree with the disciplinary body, then it should convert the proceeding into one for declaratory relief and proceed to declare the subject statute unconstitutional. The court held that confidential medical disciplinary proceedings had been a long-standing policy until that tradition was reversed by the New York Department of Health. Additionally, the physician's argument lacked persuasiveness because it ignored the provisions of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2995-a(1)(b), (2), which required the Department to maintain physician profiles. The intent was to grant public access to a physician's profile and any disciplinary charges and determinations critical to the public's making of informed decisions in their choice of physicians.


OUTCOME: The application was denied and the petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and Surgeons - Disciplinary Proceedings - Disclosure of Charges after Conclusion of Proceeding - Maintenance of "Physician Profiles"

Respondent Department of Health did not act unlawfully in publishing on its official Internet Web site the five factual specifications of professional misconduct filed against petitioner physician after the conclusion of the disciplinary proceeding in which four of the charges were dismissed, and a technical record-keeping violation was found with respect to the remaining charge. Public Health Law § 230 (10) (g) authorizes public dissemination of the statement of charges and determination and order once the disciplinary proceedings are finally determined. Moreover, respondent has the discretionary authority to publish and disseminate information concerning physicians licensed to practice within the state as part of its statutory duty to maintain "physician profiles" (see Public Health Law § 2995-a). There is no indication that the Legislature intended to define for respondent the content and scope of the information to be included in the "physician profiles." Respondent's interpretation of the statutory scheme is consistent with a general governmental policy of preserving the confidentiality of information pertaining to disciplinary proceedings until a determination has been reached. Furthermore, respondent applied the statutory scheme in a constitutional manner.

 

1674 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


INDICTMENT 01-2449-001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ERIE COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 538; 753 N.Y.S.2d 809; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1477



October 30, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Corrected December 11, 2002.
Reaffirmed, Supplemented by People v. Smith, 193 Misc. 2d 462, 751 N.Y.S.2d 356, 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1475 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., 2002)
Subsequent appeal at People v. Smith, 2006 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5744 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Apr. 28, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought an order: (1) declaring N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.27(12) unconstitutional (a) on its face, (b) as applied to him, as he was mentally retarded, and (2) striking the death notice against the defendant because he could not be sentenced to death pursuant to an unconstitutional statute, and (3) granting such other and further relief as just and proper.


OVERVIEW: The key question which arose herein was whether case precedent in a United States Supreme Court decision required a determination that portions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.27 were unconstitutional, because the statute cited mental retardation as a mitigating factor to be considered by a jury during the sentencing phase, rather than requiring that a finding of mental retardation by a jury must preclude direction by the jury of a sentence of death. The instant court held that it did. The next question which arose was: could a portion of the statute be stricken as unconstitutional, leaving the balance intact to operate as intended by the legislature, when construed within the confines of that case precedent. The instant court held that it could. The court thus found that portion of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.27-9(b) was unconstitutional. To remedy the situation, a simple addition to the jury charge during the sentencing proceeding was recommended, such that: if the jury found that defendant was mentally retarded, then it could not impose a death sentence. This charge would be given in conjunction with all other relevant instructions required to be given to the jury at that time.


OUTCOME: The motion was granted to the extent that it prohibited a jury from finding mental retardation based on the evidence. The motion was denied insofar that the balance of the statute could be left intact to operate as intended by the legislature.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Mental Retardation as Mitigating Factor in Sentencing

 CPL 400.27 (9) (b) is unconstitutional to the extent that the statute cites mental retardation as a "mitigating factor" to be considered by a jury during the sentencing phase of a death penalty case, rather than requiring that a finding of mental retardation by a jury must preclude direction by the jury of a sentence of death. Atkins v Virginia (536 US 304, 153 L. Ed. 2d 335, 122 S. Ct. 2242) prohibits the execution of mentally retarded criminals. Under Atkins, mental retardation is not merely a factor which may be considered by the jury in mitigation of a death sentence. It is a prohibition. Any statute which states otherwise cannot stand.

L.A.M. Recovery v. Dep't of Consumer Affairs


Index No.: 13641/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 754; 749 N.Y.S.2d 862; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1430



October 25, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Amended November 13, 2002.
Related proceeding at L.A.M. Recovery Inc. v. Dep't of Consumer Affairs, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 23701 (S.D.N.Y., Nov. 22, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Petitioner's motion for preliminary injunction denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This was a N.Y.C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to review a determination of respondent Department of Consumer Affairs affirming a determination of a hearing officer finding petitioner tow truck company guilty of unlawful towing. The company moved for a preliminary injunction enjoining enforcement of the Department's determination. The Department cross-moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: A motion to dismiss the petition because it was unverified was denied as such defects were ignored unless objected to with due diligence, and the alleged defect was not raised until several weeks after the petition was served. Under New York, N.Y., Admin. Code § 496-(a), tow trucks must be licensed unless specifically exempted in New York, N.Y., Admin. Code § 495-(e), and repossessors were not included in any of the enumerated exemptions. The New York City Administrative Code failed to distinguish tow trucks which recovered vehicles from the scene of accidents from those engaged in repossession operations. The allegations concerning the need for stealth in repossession operations and the insignificant public safety risks attendant therewith were irrelevant. The company was engaged in unlawful towing and was not exempt from the licensing requirements. The licensing process was not onerous. The company failed to demonstrate inability to comply with the law and maintain a business. The regulations were not preempted by federal law.


OUTCOME: The motion for a preliminary injunction was denied, and any injunction currently in effect was to expire two weeks after the service of this decision, order and judgment with notice of entry.


HEADNOTES

Licenses - Tow Trucks - Repossessors

Pursuant to the plain language of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, tow trucks engaged in repossessing vehicles must be licensed just as any other tow trucks. The Code does not distinguish between tow trucks that recover vehicles from the scene of accidents and those engaged in repossession operations. Moreover, repossessors are not included in any of the enumerated exceptions to the licensing requirement.

 

1676 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Turza


DOCKET NO. 2002su24041, 2002su28770


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 432; 751 N.Y.S.2d 351; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1486



October 18, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Corrected December 17, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction certain domestic violence charges, after the court transferred to its Integrated Domestic Violence Part the misdemeanor and violation cases that had been pending in the Suffolk County District Court (New York).


OVERVIEW: New York's Chief Administrative Judge had established an Integrated Domestic Violence (IDV) Part within the supreme court in certain counties, to enable courts to better deal with family-related issues. Defendant argued that the misdemeanor charges against him should not have been transferred to the IDV Part, because N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 10.20, 10.30, and 210.05, read together, precluded such a transfer since he had not been charged by indictment. The court held that if it read the statutory provisions to preclude transfer to the IDV Part, it would have to find that they effectively violated the broad jurisdiction granted to the court in various provisions of N.Y. Const. art. VI. Rather than find the criminal procedure laws unconstitutional, it held that the limitations on transfer did not apply where IDV Parts had been established, and that it therefore had jurisdiction over all the domestic actions involving defendant, even the misdemeanor charges not commenced by indictment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Transfer to Other Court - Authority of Integrated Domestic Violence Part of Supreme Court to Transfer Misdemeanor and Violation Cases to Itself

Pursuant to state constitutional provisions granting the Supreme Court unlimited jurisdiction and the broad power to transfer cases to itself, the procedural provisions of the Criminal Procedure Law that limit a superior court's trial jurisdictions over misdemeanors and petty offenses should not be read as applying to an Integrated Domestic Violence (IDV) Part of the Supreme Court. An IDV Part is a response to the need for a single court to adjudicate all matters which might bring a family into the court system when the underlying issue is domestic violence. IDV Parts, with the authority to transfer cases pending in a local criminal court to themselves, have been created in accordance with the lawful exercise of the authority duly delegated by the State Constitution to the Chief Administrative Judge to manage the court system.

DeMatteo v. DeMatteo


Index: D-37432, RJI: 32-00-2215


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ONEIDA COUNTY


194 Misc. 2d 640; 749 N.Y.S.2d 671; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1324



October 9, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 5, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that plaintiff not smoke in her residence for twenty-four hours prior to minor child's scheduled presence and that neither party smoke in a motor vehicle when said child is present in that vehicle.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A 14-year-old child requested that he not be exposed to Environmental Tobacco Smoke during court ordered visitations with his mother.


OVERVIEW: The court took judicial notice that environmental tobacco smoke was a carcinogen, causing lung cancer in otherwise healthy non-smokers and that the children of smoking parents suffered a higher incidence of respiratory infections and smaller rates of increases in lung functions. N.Y. Pub. Health §1399 (repealed) declared ETS to be a significant health hazard wherever it might be encountered, while regulating exposure only in selected public places. The court concluded that §1399 (repealed) declared Environmental Tobacco Smoke (ETS) to be a public health risk based on 1989 medical literature. Justice and reason dictated that any party be allowed to produce new scientific knowledge acquired since 1989. Pending a hearing, the best interests of the child were served limiting his exposure to ETS. The child's mother was to be smoke-free around the child. The court ordered the recommendation of the law guardian that the mother not smoke in her residence for 24 hours prior to her son's scheduled presence. Smoking in a motor vehicle by either party was prohibited only when the boy was present in that vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court took judicial notice of specific facts and conclusions and ordered the mother not smoke in her residence for 24 hours prior to her son's scheduled presence. Smoking in a motor vehicle by either party was prohibited only when the son was present in the vehicle.


HEADNOTES

Evidence - Judicial Notice - Health Risks Associated with Exposure to Environmental Tobacco Smoke

In a visitation dispute involving the exposure of the parties' 14-year-old son to environmental tobacco smoke (ETS) during court-ordered visitations with plaintiff mother, the court will take judicial notice of specific health risks associated with ETS as recognized by the enactment of Public Health Law article 13-E in 1989. That statute, in regulating exposure to secondhand smoke in public places, declares ETS to be a public health risk based upon scientific studies showing that breathing secondhand smoke poses a significant health hazard for nonsmokers. However, since the judicially noticed legislative facts are based upon the state of medical literature as of 1989, plaintiff may contest the judicially noticed facts by producing any new scientific knowledge on ETS. Plaintiff will have the burden of proof to refute the legislative findings. Furthermore, the court cannot utilize judicial notice to render a final decision mandating an absolutely smoke-free environment in plaintiff's home. It cannot currently be scientifically determined when a room or home that is the site of smoking ceases to be a risk once smoking has stopped. Accordingly, since the best interests of the child are served by limiting his exposure to ETS, pending a hearing, defendant father will maintain a smoke-free house, and plaintiff will not smoke in her home 24 hours prior to scheduled visitations.

 

1678 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Rudgayzer & Gratt v. Enine, Inc.


Index No. 32527/01


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, KINGS COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 449; 749 N.Y.S.2d 855; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1322


July 2, 2002, Submitted  
September 30, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Corrected October 29, 2002.
Reversed by, Partial summary judgment granted by, Remanded by Rudgayzer & Gratt v. Enine, Inc., 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 420 (N.Y. App. Term, Apr. 14, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment denied; complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff law firm sued defendants, facsimile originator and the company which hired it to send facsimiles, under 47 U.S.C.S. § 227 of the Telephone Consumer Protection Act of 1991, for sending the firm an unsolicited facsimile. The firm moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The firm sought statutory and treble damages. The facsimile was commercial speech, and, as it concerned a lawful activity and was not misleading, was protected under U.S. Const. amend. I. The government interests advanced regarding 47 U.S.C.S. § 227 of the Telephone Consumer Protection Act's (TCPA) prohibition on unsolicited faxes were to prevent tying up fax lines and shifting advertising costs from advertisers to recipients. Given the current low cost and minimal time involved to receive a fax, banning junk faxes advertising property, goods, or services, but permitting faxes with other content, did not clearly advance the government's alleged interests. It was not practical or possible to know if a fax sent to a large workplace was unsolicited, so the TCPA did not advance the government interests asserted and was more extensive than necessary to serve that interest. A "no-call list" for fax numbers would have been a less intrusive way of achieving the government's objectives. N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 396-aa provided a less restrictive means of controlling unsolicited faxes, and was sufficient protection.


OUTCOME: The firm's summary judgment motion was denied, and the complaint was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Freedom of Speech - Constitutionality of Federal Telephone Consumer Protection Act of 1991 with Regard to Unsolicited Telefacsimile Advertising

Plaintiff law firm fails to state a claim under the Federal Telephone Consumer Protection Act of 1991 (TCPA) (47 USC § 227) based upon the receipt from defendant of a single unsolicited facsimile transmission of an advertisement. The TCPA proscribes the use of any "telephone facsimile machine, computer, or other device to send an unsolicited advertisement to a telephone facsimile machine" (47 USC § 227 [b] [1] [C]). However, fax advertisements qualify as constitutionally protected commercial speech as long as they concern lawful activity and are not misleading. Consequently, the TCPA as applied to faxes is unconstitutional. The statute is also more restrictive than is necessary to serve the governmental interest asserted. The establishment of a "no-fax" database would be less intrusive on First Amendment rights. Furthermore, the less restrictive New York statute concerning unsolicited faxes (General Business Law § 396-aa) is sufficient to protect businesses from unsolicited advertising faxes.

Weil v. Johnson

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1728; 2002 NY Slip Op 50513(U)

Lyles v. State


Claim No. 105774.


COURT OF CLAIMS OF NEW YORK


194 Misc. 2d 32; 752 N.Y.S.2d 523; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1581



September 26, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Lyles v. State, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13845 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Dec. 22, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion granted; claim dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant citizen sued defendant State for federal and state constitutional violations arising from traffic stops and searches of his vehicle. The State moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The citizen alleged state troopers searched his vehicle without authorization, damaging the vehicle and other personal property, handcuffed him, and otherwise violated his constitutional rights because of his race. No cause of action could be stated against the State under either 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 or 42 U.S.C.S. § 1981, and the court had no authority to imply a monetary cause of action against the State based on federal constitutional violations, absent congressional or state legislative approval. While an implied cause of action could, generally, be asserted against the State for violation of the equal protection and search and seizure clauses of N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 11, 12, such a cause of action was unavailable when adequate common law causes of action for false arrest, false imprisonment, assault and battery, intentional and negligent injury to property, and negligent infliction of emotional distress could have been stated. No common law causes of action were asserted, and, if they had been, they would have been foreclosed by the limitations provisions of Ct. Cl. Act § 10(3), (3-b), because the claims were filed nearly three years after their accrual.


OUTCOME: The State's motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

State - Claim against State - Federal and State Constitutional Claims - Availability of Common-Law Tort Remedy

The Court of Claims lacks subject matter jurisdiction over claimant's federal constitutional claims arising from an alleged illegal search and seizure of his person and automobile by state troopers. Absent congressional or state legislative approval, the Court of Claims cannot recognize a monetary cause of action against the State for deprivation of federal constitutional rights. Similarly, a state constitutional tort claim is not available to claimant, because claimant had an adequate common-law tort remedy but chose not to assert any common-law causes of action in the claim. The alleged wrongs could have been redressed by timely interposed monetary common-law tort claims. Recognition of an implied constitutional tort claim is therefore not necessary to ensure the full realization of claimant's rights under the State Constitution. Moreover, since the claim was filed three years after its accrual, any common-law claims would have been time-barred in any event (see Court of Claims Act § 10 [3], [3-b]).

 

1681 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


N.Y. State Crime Victims Bd. v. Majid


INDEX NO. 2643-02, RJI No. 01-02-069712


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 710; 749 N.Y.S.2d 837; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1428



September 24, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 13, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Statute held constitutional; motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Crime Victims Board sought an injunction under N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a(6) restraining defendant prison inmate from disbursing, encumbering, or assigning funds in the inmate's account (other than sums constituting child support, earned income, or that were exempt from enforcement or execution), until the inmate's victims' tort claims against him were finally determined. The inmate sought a declaration that § 632-a was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The inmate had been convicted of murdering one police officer and attempting to murder another. Learning that $ 15,062 was about to be paid into the inmate's prison account, the Board sought an injunction or attachment against the account to prevent the inmate from spending or transferring funds. The inmate argued that § 632-a (the "Son of Sam law") violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of the U.S. Constitution and the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the New York and U.S. Constitutions. The court disagreed. As § 632-a did not even create a civil remedy, much less a criminal punishment, there was no Ex Post Facto Clause violation. Since the inmate had both notice and the opportunity to be heard before injunctive relief was granted, there was no procedural due process violation. As the Board and the inmate had the same rights to apply for provisional remedies, there was no Equal Protection violation. The court found the victims and citizens of New York would be irreparably damaged if the inmate was allowed to spend the funds in his account before a court could determine whether he was required to pay that money over to his victims. No equities flowed in the inmate's favor.


OUTCOME: The injunction was granted, and the court entered a judgement declaring that the provision for injunctive relief in the "Son of Sam" law did not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause of the federal Constitution or the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the New York and federal Constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Crime Victims Board - Provisional Remedies

1. Executive Law § 632-a (6), which authorizes the State Crime Victims Board to utilize provisional remedies, violates neither the Ex Post Facto, Due Process or Equal Protection Clause. There is no ex post facto violation in legislation authorizing a governmental entity to seek already existing civil remedies, since the Ex Post Facto Clause pertains only to criminal punishments. There is no procedural due process violation, since defendant has had ample notice and opportunity to oppose the board's application for provisional remedies. There is no substantive due process violation, since there is a reasonable connection between permitting the board to obtain provisional remedies and the state's compelling interest in ensuring that the victims of crime are compensated by those who harm them. Moreover, inasmuch as provisional remedies are equally available to the board and defendant, there is no equal protection violation.

Injunctions - When Injunctive Relief Appropriate - Restraint of Prisoner's Funds

2. An injunction restraining the Department of Correctional Services from disbursing, distributing, encumbering or assigning any and all funds credited to the inmate account of defendant, except those sums which constitute child support and earned income, is granted where the victims of defendant's crimes have civil actions pending against him. Injunctive relief to preserve the assets of a potential judgment debtor is available if the action is directed to a specific fund which can be regarded as the "subject of the action." A criminal's bank account that is subject to the "Son of Sam" Law (Executive Law § 632-a) is considered to be different from the general assets of the ordinary defendant in a civil case. Accordingly, the victims of defendant's crimes and the citizens of this state will be irreparably damaged if the defendant is allowed to spend the funds in his inmate account before a court can determine whether he is required to pay that money over to his victims. Moreover, there are no equities flowing in favor of the defendant.

 

1682 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Chapman v. State


No. M-64954.


COURT OF CLAIMS OF NEW YORK


193 Misc. 2d 216; 748 N.Y.S.2d 465; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1295



September 18, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Amended October 22, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss granted in part and denied in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimant and his wife and children sued the defendant to recover for personal injuries allegedly sustained as the result of an assault and battery by two state troopers. Following a trial, the court dismissed the case due to lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The legislature resuscitated the action via 2000 N.Y. Laws 584 and this claim was filed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant had filed a statutorily revived suit against the State for assault and battery by state troopers, but the court of claims did not have jurisdiction over challenges to the constitutionality of statutes, even if such determination was necessary to resolve a claim for money damages against the State. Such challenges had to be brought by an action for declaratory judgment, which had to be brought in the supreme court. N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 3001. As such, the court did not take up the constitutional question put forth by the State challenging the claim's revival. The court did not accept the claimant's urging that the additional causes of action for the minor children were merely amendments of the pleadings. The court necessarily interpreted via 2000 N.Y. Laws 584 § 1 the legislation as authorizing only the revival of the previously filed claim brought by a claimant born on January 21, 1947. The court found the enabling legislation did not specifically authorize the revival of the previously filed derivative action by the claimant's wife. The claims by the wife and children were dismissed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was granted as to the claims by the mother and child but was denied as to the claimant.


HEADNOTES

State - Court of Claims - Lack of Subject Matter Jurisdiction to Determine Constitutionality of Private Bill Reviving Time-Barred Claim

1. The Court of Claims lacks subject matter jurisdiction to determine the constitutionality of a private bill that revived claimant's otherwise time-barred claim to recover damages for personal injuries allegedly sustained as the result of an assault and battery by two state troopers (see L 2000, ch 584). The question of whether the private bill is violative of the constitutional proscription against the payment of a claim that "would be barred by lapse of time" (NY Const, art III, § 19) must be brought in an action for declaratory judgment in Supreme Court.

State - Claim against State - Scope of Private Bill Reviving Time-Barred Claim

2. A private bill that revived claimant's otherwise time-barred claim to recover damages for personal injuries allegedly sustained as the result of an assault and battery by two state troopers (see L 2000, ch 584) does not encompass the derivative causes of action asserted on behalf of claimant's wife and minor children. The additional causes of action are not merely amendments of the pleadings that should be allowed. The enabling bill authorized only the revival of claimant's individual claim, without also specifically authorizing the revival of the previously filed derivative claims. Consequently, the bill, being in derogation of the State's sovereignty, must be strictly construed to exclude the derivative claims.

 

1683 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Adams


Indictment No. 2596/97


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 78; 747 N.Y.S.2d 909; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1274



September 18, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 16, 2002. As Amended October 31, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Application for Certificate of Relief of Civil Disabilities denied in part and granted in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant applied for a limited certificate of relief from disabilities (CRD), pursuant to N.Y. Correct. Law § 702, seeking permission to apply for hunting licenses and use long guns solely in governmentally recognized hunting areas outside of New York City, and solely during designated autumn hunting seasons. He expanded his application by asking the court to issue a CRD for employment purposes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with assault in the second degree, two counts of assault in the third degree, and two counts of criminal contempt in the first degree after a verbal and physical confrontation with his wife. He was convicted of criminal contempt, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 215.51(v), (vi), and sentenced to five years' probation and 500 hours of community service. The court also issued a court order of protection lasting five years. While defendant was still on probation, he applied for a CRD for the limited purpose of obtaining permission to apply for hunting licenses and to use long guns, but expanded his application by asking the court to issue a CRD for employment purposes. The trial court held that (1) state law did not require defendant to have a CRD before he possessed a hunting rifle; (2) because defendant was convicted of a felony, he was prohibited under 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1) from receiving any firearm or ammunition that had been transported in interstate commerce; (3) defendant was not eligible to possess a firearm under any exception to 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1); and (4) the court would not issue a CRD authorizing defendant to possess a rifle.


OUTCOME: The trial court granted defendant's application for a CRD for employment purposes, but denied the application to the extent that defendant requested permission to apply for a hunting license and to possess and use a rifle.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and Prisoners - Certificate of Relief from Disabilities--Domestic Violence Probationer - Federal Prohibition against Possessing Hunting Rifle

Federal law prohibits defendant, a domestic violence probationer who is still subject to an order of protection, from being granted a limited certificate of relief from disabilities (CRD) (Correction Law § 702) in order to permit him to apply for a hunting license and to use a hunting rifle during designated hunting seasons. Even though a hunting rifle is not a "firearm" that a felon or person subject to an order of protection is automatically prohibited from possessing under New York law (see CPL 530.14; Penal Law § 265.00 [3]; § 400.00 [1]), a hunting rifle is a "firearm" under federal law (18 USC § 921 [a] [3]). Defendant, as a convicted felon, is prohibited by federal law from possessing such a weapon that traveled in interstate commerce (18 USC § 922 [g] [1]). Moreover, since the CRD cannot relieve defendant of all firearm restraints, the CRD is legally insufficient to qualify for the exemption to the felon-in-possession of a weapon prohibition where the felon has had all core "civil rights" restored, including the right to possess all types of firearms (see 18 USC § 921 [a] [20]). Furthermore, a defendant who has been convicted of a domestic violence offense or who is subject to an order of protection is ineligible for a CRD that would restore the right to possess any type of weapon (see 18 USC § 922 [g] [8]).

 

1684 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Guzdek v. McCall


Index No.: 6621-99, RJI No.: 01-02-068901


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 759; 749 N.Y.S.2d 827; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1469



September 17, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Amended November 19, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment denied; defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a police labor union, its president, and other officers, filed a motion for summary judgment against defendants, the state comptroller and others, in their declaratory judgment action which sought to hold 1999 N.Y. Laws ch. 411 invalid and to have payments made into the pension accumulation fund as required by N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law §§ 23(b)(3)(b) and 323(b)(3)(b). Defendants filed a cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The union's members were participants in either of two state retirement funds. The comptroller had managed the funds' investments so well that the employers' normal contribution rate had decreased to zero. Pursuant to 1999 N.Y. Laws ch. 411, payment of the administrative expenses or costs was made from the fund at that point. Plaintiffs' contention that ch. 411 violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7 failed because there was no impairment of the comptroller's fiduciary discretion over the funds, nor were investment earnings diverted at the expense of the funds' members. The court held that 1999 N.Y. Laws ch. 411 was a proper exercise of the legislature's authority, noting that the comptroller never had any significant discretion with respect to funding of administrative expenses previously. The court held that ch. 411 fell within the limits of the legislature's reserved authority. The comptroller was relieved of his statutory obligation to collect employers' reimbursements of administration costs in certain circumstances, but this did not diminish or impair the benefit of his independent judgment. Accordingly, the court concluded that ch. 411 did not represent a radical change.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and granted defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service - Retirement and Pension Benefits - Employers' Contributions - Impairment of Constitutionally Protected Pension Benefits

Chapter 411 of the Laws of 1999 does not unlawfully infringe upon constitutionally protected pension benefits of public employees (NY Const, art V, § 7). Chapter 411 relieves local public employers of their statutory obligation to make annual administration contributions into the Common Retirement Fund (CRF) to reimburse the CRF for administrative expenses (see Retirement and Social Security Law § 23 [b] [3] [b]; § 323 [b] [3] [b]) if the State Comptroller determines that CRF assets and investment earnings in any given year are sufficient to fully fund basic retirement benefits without the "normal contribution" employers are required to make for that year. The statute relieves the Comptroller of the statutory obligation to collect employers' reimbursements of administration costs in certain circumstances, but does not interfere with the Comptroller's independent judgment or create an inappropriate level of risk in the management of the CRF. Plaintiffs failed to demonstrate that the use of investment earnings to pay administration contributions when such funds are available poses any risk to the security of employees' retirement benefits.

 

1685 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Snuszki v. Wright


Index No: 110189


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NIAGARA COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 490; 751 N.Y.S.2d 344; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1507



September 6, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Snuszki v. Wright, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12365 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Nov. 21, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff administrator filed an action against defendant inmate, pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a(3), seeking funds which the inmate received after he settled an action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The inmate filed a motion to dismiss the administrator's action, arguing, inter alia, that N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a(3) was unconstitutional. The New York State Office of the Attorney General requested that it be allowed to intervene.


OVERVIEW: An inmate was incarcerated after he was convicted of committing second-degree murder in 1988. While he was incarcerated, the inmate filed an action, pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, against employees of the New York State Department of Correctional Services, and he settled that action for $ 25,000 in May 2001. The person who was appointed to administer the murder victim's estate learned about the settlement and filed an action in October 2001, pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a(3), seeking the money the inmate received. The trial court held that § 632-a(3) did not deny the inmate access to the courts, did not violate the inmate's right to equal protection of the law and substantive due process of law, and was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The trial court denied the inmate's motion to dismiss the administrator's complaint and granted the administrator's request that the $ 25,000 being held in the inmate's account by the warden of the facility where the inmate was incarcerated be paid into the court until the New York State Crime Victims Board could take action within its mandate to protect the money.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Son of Sam Law

The Son of Sam Law, which allows the victim of a "specified crime" or the victim's estate, to bring an action to recover money damages from the person convicted of committing the crime within three years of the discovery of funds of the convicted person (Executive Law § 632-a [3]), is not unconstitutional as applied to the settlement proceeds of a federal civil rights action received in 2001 by defendant, a prison inmate incarcerated for having murdered plaintiffs' mother in 1988. The statute does not violate defendant's fundamental constitutional right to access to the courts. The statute does not prohibit defendant from pursuing legal relief against any alleged wrong that is committed against him in the future. Furthermore, the statute does not violate defendant's constitutional right to equal protection. The extension of time provided for in the statute, that allows crime victims to bring an action within three years of discovering any funds of a convicted person, is rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest in compensating victims of crime from the assets of the person who caused the harm. Lastly, the statute does not violate defendant's right to substantive due process. There is nothing arbitrary, conscience-shocking or oppressive in permitting crime victims to seek compensation from those who have caused them injury once they discover that the person who harmed them has acquired assets.

PEOPLE v. TAYLOR


Indictment No. 1845/2000, Indictment No. 1012/2001

1688 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


PEOPLE v. TAYLOR


Indictment No. 1845/2000, Indictment No. 1012/2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1196; 2002 NY Slip Op 50365(U)

Merchs. & Bus. Men's Mut. Ins. Co. v. A.P.O. Health Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2014; 228 N.Y.L.J. 41

1692 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Francis S. v. Rochester Psychiatric Ctr.


No. C # 1207


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 438; 748 N.Y.S.2d 642; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1244



August 28, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended October 8, 2002. As Amended December 11, 2002.
Related proceeding at People v. Francis S., 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 872 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., May 3, 2005)



DISPOSITION: The trial court explained the decisions made during trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner entered a plea of not responsible by reason of mental disease or defect to the crimes of attempted assault and criminal possession of a weapon and was adjudicated in the County Court (New York) to be suffering from a dangerous mental disorder. In accordance with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(16), petitioner requested a rehearing and review of that recommitment order via a jury trial in accordance with N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.35.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimed that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(14) was unconstitutional because he had been committed both criminally and civilly in state psychiatric facilities for a period longer than he could have been incarcerated if he had been convicted of his charged crimes. The reviewing court held that the application to have § 330.20(14) declared unconstitutional was denied. Petitioner's hypothetical maximum sentence did not provide the constitutional limit for his commitment. Further, the jury instruction sequence advocated by petitioner was the more logical, the more intelligible to the jury, and fairer to petitioner. The jury should have had the opportunity to determine the issue of mental illness and the need for retention before it determined the issue of dangerous mental disorder. Also, the trial court in its discretion could use the jury in an advisory capacity on the issue of dangerousness, as it did, but it did not indicate that to the jury. Therefore, the jury should have been instructed that the sequence of its findings should be mental illness, need for retention, and dangerous mental disorder.


OUTCOME: The instruction to jury was incorrect, but the exculpatory testimony by petitioner was admissible.


HEADNOTES

Mental Health - Person Acquitted of Crime by Reason of Insanity - Length of Confinement

1. CPL 330.20 (14) is not unconstitutional because it permits a defendant found not responsible by reason of mental disease or defect to be confined for a period longer than he could have been incarcerated if he had been convicted of the crimes charged. An insanity acquitee's hypothetical maximum sentence does not set the constitutional limit for his commitment. Moreover, an insanity acquitee, having been found not to be suffering from a dangerous mental disorder, could constitutionally later be found to have such a mental condition and, thereupon, criminally recommitted, since the criminal court retains supervisory authority over insanity acquitees.

Mental Health - Person Acquitted of Crime by Reason of Insanity - Jury Findings

2. In a proceeding to determine if an insanity acquitee's confinement should be continued, a jury should be instructed that it is to make two findings: mental illness, and the need for retention. Mental Hygiene Law § 9.35, which is incorporated by reference in CPL 330.20 (16), provides, in part, that "if the verdict of the jury ... be that such person is mentally ill and in need of retention the justice shall ... make an order authorizing continued retention."

Mental Health - Person Acquitted of Crime by Reason of Insanity - Sequence of Jury Findings

3. In a proceeding to determine if an insanity acquitee's confinement should be continued, a jury should first determine if the acquitee has a mental illness. If the jury determines that the acquitee suffers from a mental illness, it should then determine if he is in need of retention, or if he is mentally ill but not in need of retention. If the acquitee is determined to be both mentally ill and in need of retention, the jury should finally determine if he suffers from a dangerous mental disorder. It is more logical for a jury to have the opportunity to determine the issue of mental illness and the need for retention before determining the issue of dangerous mental disorder.

Mental Health - Person Acquitted of Crime by Reason of Insanity - Effect of Acquitee's Plea in Criminal Action

4. In a proceeding to determine the need for continued confinement of an insanity acquitee, the jury should be instructed that the act which resulted in the acquitee's plea of not responsible by reason of mental disease or defect established beyond a reasonable doubt that the acquitee committed the crimes charged. However, such an instruction does not preclude the acquitee from testifying that his involvement in the crime resulting in the plea was different from the allegations against him, and that despite the plea he did not suffer from a mental illness.

 

1693 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Garal Wholesalers Ltd. v. Miller Brewing Co.


INDEX No. 01-25517


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


193 Misc. 2d 630; 751 N.Y.S.2d 679; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1321



August 16, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended November 5, 2002. Counsel and Opinion Amended November 27, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss and strike Miller's first affirmative defense granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff beer wholesaler, suing defendant brewer for breach of contract and termination of its distributorship in violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 55-c, moved to dismiss and strike the brewer's first affirmative defense, in which it challenged the constitutionality of the statute claimed to have been violated.


OVERVIEW: The wholesaler's distribution contract with the brewer's predecessor explicitly acknowledged that N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 55-c governed the agreement. The predecessor sold the rights to certain labels to the brewer, which announced that it was terminating the wholesaler's contract to allow its own pre-existing distributors to take over the territory. In the meantime, § 55-c had been amended to narrowly circumscribe the circumstances and manner in which beer distributorships could be terminated, and the amendment also indicated that it was to have retrospective application. The court held that such retrospective application to the agreement the brewer inherited from the predecessor did not violate the Contracts Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. The amendments were primarily a clarification of pre-existing law in an area of heavy governmental regulation, and there was no reason to suppose that such changes were not within the reasonable expectations of the contracting parties.


OUTCOME: The court granted the wholesaler's motion.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Contract Clause - Retroactive Application of New Termination Procedures for Beer Distributorships

The retroactive application of recently enacted amendments to Alcoholic Beverage Control Law § 55-c (L 2001, ch 346, eff June 15, 2001) with regard to the new termination procedures for beer distributorships is not violative of the constitutional prohibition against impairing the obligations of contracts (US Const, art I, § 10 [1]) as applied to defendant's attempt to terminate plaintiff's beer distributorship before the legislation was signed into law on September 19, 2001. The amendments prohibit termination of a beer wholesaler's distributorship agreement except under very circumscribed occurrences. Defendant's August 31, 2001 termination letter fails to satisfy the new amendments as to the requisite notice, a reasonably detailed written national "consolidation policy," and the compensation required for the loss of distributorship rights. The remedial amendments did not impair any contract-based expectations. Defendant contracted in a "heavily regulated" area, and could not justifiably rely on the continuity of the statutory scheme where the parties' agreement expressly provides that their contractual rights were subject to alteration by state law with respect to "longer or otherwise differing termination notice periods, procedures, or justifications." Retroactive application of the statute would therefore not substantially alter the vested contract rights of the parties or impose unanticipated consequences.
