Johnson v. Ctr. for Animal Care & Control, Inc.


INDEX NO. 11980/2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


192 Misc. 2d 210; 745 N.Y.S.2d 890; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 845



July 10, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Corrected August 7, 2002.



DISPOSITION: The plaintiff's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff dog owner sought an order releasing his dogs in their original condition. The dogs were in the possession of defendant animal shelter, which argued that the dogs could not be released to the owner without first being neutered, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 17-804(a).


OVERVIEW: When the city marshal executed an order of eviction, he found the owner's two male Rottweiler puppies and turned them over to the animal shelter. When owner attempted to regain possession of his two dogs, he was informed that his dogs could not be released to him unless they were first neutered. The owner argued that the requirement to have his dogs neutered as a precondition of getting them returned to him violated his right to equal protection, where it created an unconstitutional distinction between his pedigreed dogs and registered show breed dogs. The shelter opposed the owner's motion, arguing that the provision met constitutional muster. The trial court found that the requirement to establish a genuine public need for the challenged ordinance did not lie with New York City, but rather it was whether the dog owner demonstrated that there was no rational connection between the regulation and the promotion of safety of persons and property. The trial court found that the owner could not meet the rational review standard, and the petition was denied.


OUTCOME: The dog owner's petition was denied.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Municipal Ordinance Requiring Sterilization of Homeless Animals in Shelters

A municipal ordinance which provides that shelters for homeless animals may not release a dog to anyone, including its owner, unless the dog has been sterilized (Administrative Code of City of NY § 17-804 [a]), constitutes a valid police regulation that is not violative of equal protection. The ordinance was enacted to address the serious problem of overpopulation of unwanted dogs and cats. Plaintiff, whose male dogs were turned over to defendant's shelter by the city marshal following the execution of an eviction order, has failed to demonstrate that the challenged ordinance is not rationally related to the promotion of public health and safety.

In re Retention of Judge


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


192 Misc. 2d 203; 745 N.Y.S.2d 885; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 848



July 9, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Corrected August 7, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Because the Rochester Psychiatric Center did not produce sufficient evidence, and sustain their burden of proof, by clear and convincing evidence, Mr. Judge should be released from confinement in that facility.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following an involuntary admission to a mental hospital pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.27, petitioner patient sought an order compelling respondent hospital to release him.


OVERVIEW: The patient was most recently admitted to the hospital, pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.27. The patient sought to be released, and the hospital sought an order authorizing an involuntary admission for a period of 60 days. At the hearing, the Attorney General for the State of New York, appeared on behalf of the hospital, and offered, and the trial court took judicial notice of, an order of a previous court authorizing the emergency admission of the patient to continue for a 15-day period. However, the trial court found that such order, under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.39, was based upon a lesser standard, of "reasonable cause" to believe that the patient was mentally ill, and in need of retention. That standard was insufficient to support a determination that the patient was in need of continued retention for a period of 60 days, following his involuntary admission. The appropriate standard to guarantee due process rights of the patient was that the hospital was required to prove by clear and convincing evidence that the patient was in need of hospitalization, a standard the hospital did not meet.


OUTCOME: The patient was ordered released.


HEADNOTES

Incapacitated and Mentally Disabled Persons - Involuntary Commitment - Burden of Proof at Patient-Initiated Retention Hearing

In a patient-initiated retention hearing conducted pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law § 9.31 following an involuntary psychiatric admission under Mental Hygiene Law § 9.27, the hospital has the burden of demonstrating by clear and convincing evidence that the patient is dangerous and in need of continued retention for a 60-day period. Placing the burden on the patient on the issue of dangerousness would contravene minimum due process standards and would thus subject the statutory scheme to constitutional infirmity. The statutory requirement that the patient initiate the request for a hearing is merely a procedural device for invoking the patient's statutory and constitutional right for a judicial determination of the patient's continued, involuntary confinement. Accordingly, since petitioner hospital failed to produce sufficient evidence to sustain its burden of proof by clear and convincing evidence following the patient's emergency involuntary admission, the patient is ordered released from confinement.

 

1699 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Haneiph


Docket No. 2001KN063974


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


191 Misc. 2d 738; 745 N.Y.S.2d 405; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 818



June 25, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the seventh degree, a class B misdemeanor. Defendant moved to dismiss the information and underlying complaint pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.30 on the ground that the People failed to answer ready for trial within the statutory period.


OVERVIEW: On August 11, 2001, defendant was arraigned. At arraignment, the People served and filed a supporting deposition as well as a field test. Defendant was released on his own recognizance, and the case was adjourned to October 1, 2001 for discovery by stipulation. Although the People alleged that they answered ready for trial at arraignment, the minutes of defendant's arraignment contained no such statement of readiness. On September 11, 2001, the governor issued an executive order in which he declared a disaster emergency in the state. On October 29, 2001, the People answered not ready for trial due to the policy restricting notification of officers to testify at trial in misdemeanors involving non-incarcerated defendants. On February 28, 2002, the People answered ready for trial. On March 18, 2002, defense counsel filed a motion to dismiss. The trial court found that the governor did not usurp the functions of the legislature in issuing the executive orders. The attack on the World Trade Center was the single largest attack upon the mainland United States in its entire history. Thereafter, the courts were closed anywhere from several days to a week.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Separation of Powers - Suspension of Laws by Governor during State Disaster Emergency - Executive Orders Suspending Operation of CPL 30.30 Following Attack on World Trade Center

Executive Orders issued by the Governor suspending certain speedy trial provisions of CPL 30.30 in response to the September 11, 2001 terrorist attack on the World Trade Center (see 9 NYCRR 5.113 et seq.) are not unconstitutional as violative of the principle of separation of powers. The Executive Orders were authorized by Executive Law § 29-a, which provides that the Governor may "temporarily suspend specific provisions of any statute ... during a state disaster emergency, if compliance with such provisions would prevent, hinder, or delay action necessary to cope with the disaster." In addition to suspending the general operation of CPL 30.30 until January 7, 2002, the Governor specifically suspended CPL 30.30 (4) (g) "so far as it may be interpreted to limit 'exceptional circumstances' in a way that would not include any delay attributable to the disaster emergency" ( 9 NYCRR 5.113.28). The attack on the World Trade Center clearly fits within the definition of an "exceptional circumstance." In view of the fact that the purpose of CPL 30.30 is to serve the narrow purpose of prosecutorial readiness, and given the severe restrictions upon the People's ability to be ready for trial in the weeks immediately following September 11, the Governor's Executive Orders were consistent with the intent of the Legislature and are therefore constitutional. Accordingly, since the People can be charged with only 78 days of delay in defendant's misdemeanor drug possession prosecution, which commenced with his arraignment on August 11, 2001, defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint on speedy trial grounds is denied.

1701 of 1817 DOCUMENTS



Silver v. Pataki


INDEX NO. 110553/1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


192 Misc. 2d 117; 744 N.Y.S.2d 821; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 706



June 17, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Opinion Corrected July 9, 2002.
Affirmed by Silver v. Pataki, 3 A.D.3d 101, 769 N.Y.S.2d 518, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13222 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 2003)



DISPOSITION: The Governor's motion for summary judgment was granted; the court declared that the provisions vetoed (other than Veto 494, which was moot) were unconstitutionally enacted by the legislature and thus were void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action seeking a judgment declaring that 55 line-item vetoes by defendant Governor of the State of New York (governor) interposed in 1998 in bills that did not appropriate monies violated N.Y. Const. art. IV § 7, plaintiff Speaker of the New York State Assembly (speaker) moved the court to determine his prior application for summary judgment, and the governor's cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the action.


OVERVIEW: The speaker challenged the constitutionality of the governor's line item vetoes. The governor moved to dismiss for lack of standing, and the interim court, on appeal, agreed. However, the appellate court overturned the decision, and remanded the matter back to the trial court. The speaker then sought a ruling on his previous motion for summary judgment which had not been ruled on by the trial court, and the governor cross moved for summary judgment. The trial court found that while it was clear that the case was moot in that any veto found to have been unconstitutionally interposed could not have resulted in the appropriation being revived and implemented in the manner directed by the legislature, courts had recognized exceptions to the mootness doctrine. None of the material vetoed constituted substantive provisions that would become part of the consolidated laws of the state. However, by inserting provisions that affected appropriations in bills that did not appropriate money, but which referred to bills that did appropriate money, the legislature impermissibly attempted to do indirectly that which could not have been done directly.


OUTCOME: The governor's cross motion for summary judgment was granted.



HEADNOTES

Courts - Academic and Moot Questions - Exception to Mootness Doctrine - Power of Legislature to Alter Governor's Budget Bills

1. An action by the Speaker of the New York State Assembly seeking a judgment declaring that certain line-item vetoes by the Governor interposed in 1998 violated section 7 of article IV of the State Constitution should not be dismissed on grounds of mootness, even though any veto found to have been unconstitutionally interposed cannot result in the appropriation being revived and implemented in the manner directed by the Legislature. This action qualifies for an exception to the mootness doctrine. The only aspect of the requested relief remaining is a declaration relating to the respective powers of the parties in the budgetary process. The issues raised are novel, of significant public importance, and may not be capable of review other than through litigation. A final determination of these issues will likely enable the parties to ascertain their respective powers in the budgetary process, and thus avoid future litigation which is probable absent a decision on the merits.

State - Budget - Power of Legislature to Alter Governor's Budget Bills

2. Legislative directions, segregations and limitations set forth in separate nonappropriation bills with respect to the spending of appropriated monies unconstitutionally altered the budget bills submitted by the Governor. Under the State Constitution, the "legislature may not alter an appropriation bill submitted by the governor except to strike out or reduce items therein, but it may add thereto items of appropriation provided that such additions are stated separately and distinctly from the original items of the bill and refer each to a single object or purpose" (NY Const, art VII, § 4). Budget bills submitted by the Governor may consist both of measures that appropriate money as well as programmatic bills amending substantive law to implement the appropriations. However, the contents of an appropriation bill are limited to provisions that relate "specifically to some particular appropriation in the bill" (NY Const, art VII, § 6). Such provisions may set forth the "when, how or where" the monies appropriated therein may be spent. Thus, being part of an item of appropriation, these provisions are subject to the "no alteration" restriction of section 4 of article VII. The Legislature may not constitutionally place alterations of the Governor's appropriation related legislation stating "when, how or where" monies may be expended in nonappropriation programmatic bills. By inserting these provisions that affect appropriations in bills that do not appropriate money, but which refer to bills that do appropriate money, the Legislature impermissibly attempted to do indirectly that which could not be done directly.

 

1702 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taylor


Indictment No. 1845/2000, Indictment No. 1012/2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


191 Misc. 2d 672; 743 N.Y.S.2d 253; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 568



May 22, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Corrected June 19, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss indictments and to release materials relating to jury selection denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with first degree murder and lesser crimes, regarding which the State sought the death penalty, moved to dismiss the indictments and for the disclosure of information regarding the selection of grand juries.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said Hispanics, "poor people," and "young people" were underrepresented in the grand jury selection process. He sought jury-selection information to prove his claim. Neither "poor people" nor "young people" were distinctive groups as contemplated by the requirement to select grand juries from a fair cross-section of the community. The sources from which jury lists were composed were not unreasonably stale or, if they were, such staleness did not cause an underrepresentation of Hispanics. Underrepresentation of Hispanics in those sources, including lists of voters, licensed drivers, taxpayers, and unemployment compensation, resulting from voluntary behavior patterns, unencouraged by state action, did not show their systematic exclusion. A lack of monitoring of jury lists did not show a systematic exclusion of any group. The methods by which grand juries were selected did not violate defendant's right to equal protection or his due process right to a grand jury drawn from a fair cross-section of the community, nor did he show that additional disclosure of jury-selection material was necessary to determine his claims.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motions to dismiss the indictments or for additional jury-selection materials were denied.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury - Selection of Jury - Fair Cross-Section of Community

Defendant has failed to demonstrate that the indictments in his death penalty case (see CPL 250.40) should be dismissed due to the underrepresentation in the Queens County grand jury pool of "poor people," Hispanics and young persons aged 18 to 34. Underrepresentation in the grand jury pool alone does not establish a constitutional violation. To make a prima facie showing requiring the People to respond, there must be a showing that the underrepresentation actually resulted from the selection procedures employed. A fair cross-section claim requires a showing that something inherent in the selection process resulted in the systematic exclusion of the underrepresented group. Defendant has failed to show that the method by which the grand juries were selected violated either his right to equal protection or his due process right to a grand jury drawn from a fair cross-section of the community. No showing has been made of any discriminatory intent, either presumed or actual, at any stage of the jury selection process. Any underrepresentation of Hispanics on the jury source lists is not attributable to the jury selection system, and does not establish a fair cross-section violation. Furthermore, defendant failed to demonstrate that additional disclosure of jury selection material is necessary to determine his claims.

 

1703 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


N.Y. County Lawyer's Ass'n v. State


Index No. 102987/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


192 Misc. 2d 424; 745 N.Y.S.2d 376; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 753



May 3, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] City of New York's cross motion to dismiss denied; NYCLA's motion for a preliminary injunction granted in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action stemming from defendant State's failure to increase the current compensation rates for assigned counsel, plaintiff county bar association moved for a preliminary injunction and declaratory judgment mandating a higher minimum hourly rate for court-assigned counsel.


OVERVIEW: The bar association sought declarations, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 3001 that: (1) the State was obligated to ensure that a sufficient number of qualified private attorneys were available and able to represent children and indigent adults in family and criminal proceedings in New York City; (2) the State's failure to raise the hourly rates of compensation paid to assigned counsel, the distinction between the rates paid for in and out-of-court work, and the ceilings on total per case compensation created a severe and unacceptably high risk that children and indigent adults would not receive meaningful and effective legal representation; and (3) the rate-setting was unconstitutional as currently applied to the representation of children and indigent adults. Specifically, the bar contended that the failure to increase the current compensation rates for assigned counsel rendered hollow the right to counsel. The trial court found that the bar association satisfied its burden of showing a likelihood that indigent litigants in the New York City family and criminal courts were being denied effective assistance of counsel as a result of the current compensation rates.


OUTCOME: The court issued a mandatory preliminary injunction directing payment of an interim rate of $ 90 an hour for in and out-of-court work, but declined to issue a declaratory judgment without a full trial on the merits.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client - Assignment of Counsel - Compensation

In view of the documented imminent danger of ineffective assistance of counsel to indigent litigants in New York City Family and Criminal Courts resulting from the inadequate statutory compensation rates paid to assigned counsel (see County Law § 722-b; Family Ct Act § 245 [b]; Judiciary Law § 35 [2]), plaintiff bar association is granted a mandatory preliminary injunction directing payment of an interim rate of $ 90 for in- and out-of-court work. The $ 90 hourly fee is consistent with the rates paid for assigned counsel work in the federal courts. Plaintiff has satisfied its heightened burden of showing extraordinary circumstances to warrant preliminary injunctive relief. Plaintiff's uncontroverted documentary evidence demonstrates the adverse effects and the depth of the crisis of the current compensation rates on juvenile delinquency cases, abuse and neglect proceedings, appeal backlogs, arraignment overload, individual assigned counsel case overload, uncertified panel counsel, and prolonged delays. The doctrine of separation of powers must yield on a showing that the State's failure to raise the current compensation rates adversely affects the judiciary's ability to function and presumptively subjects innocent indigent citizens to increased risks of adverse adjudications and convictions merely because of their poverty.

Cadiz-Jones v Zambretti


123772/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2043; 2002 NY Slip Op 30135(U); 227 N.Y.L.J. 81



April 9, 2002, Decided

1705 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. McFarlan


Ind. No. 3192/01


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


191 Misc. 2d 531; 744 N.Y.S.2d 287; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 390



April 4, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name, Counsel and Opinion Corrected May 21, 2002.
Later proceeding at People v. McFarlan, 2004 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 16481 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Dec. 21, 2004)
Subsequent appeal at People v. McFarlan, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11296 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Oct. 25, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for jostling and other crimes. He filed a motion to suppress evidence obtained in a search that he claimed was incident to an illegal arrest, and to suppress a lineup as the fruit of the illegal arrest and as being tainted and unduly suggestive.


OVERVIEW: A woman told police she saw two men pickpocketing on a bus. An officer produced a computer printout with the photos of suspects, and the woman identified defendant as one of the men. Later, the officer saw defendant bump into a person on a bus, and arrested him. The first woman picked him out of a lineup. At the suppression hearing, the State could not find the original computer printout of the photo array, so a second printout was generated. This, claimed defendant, was a Rosario violation. The court disagreed. Under the New York Electronic Signatures and Records Act (ESRA), N.Y. State Tech. Law §§ 101-109 (McKinney's art. 57-A), the court was obliged to consider the second printout as proof of the original computer record due to the manner in which the document was stored and retrieved. The court determined that ERSA applied to criminal as well as civil cases, as defendant's constitutional rights were not violated by its application. The court did not need to decide whether ERSA was preempted by a federal statute, since the result would have been the same under either. The officer had probable cause to arrest defendant, and the lineup was properly conducted.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to suppress was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Disclosure - Failure to Produce Rosario Material - Failure to Produce "Original" Computer-Generated Photo Array - Application of Electronic Signatures and Records Act

The failure of the People to produce the "original" computer-generated photo array from which defendant was identified does not constitute a Rosario violation or create a presumption that the photo identification was impermissibly suggestive. The People have demonstrated that the electronic record was appropriately retrieved and reproduced on paper, resulting in a second printout that is identical to the "original" printout. Under these circumstances, the second printout is admissible under New York's Electronic Signatures and Records Act (State Technology Law art I [ESRA]). ESRA governs the use and legal admissibility of records stored by electronic means in both civil and criminal proceedings. The statute permits the court to consider the second printout as proof of the "original" computer record due to the manner in which the document was stored and retrieved (see State Technology Law § 105; 9 NYCRR 540.5). Since both printouts were generated in the same format, there can be no prejudice from the fact that the defendant was selected from the first printout and that a second identical printout was later used at hearing or trial. Each is identical and conveys the full recoverable information. Furthermore, the result with regard to the photo array would be the same even if the Federal Electronic Signatures in Global and National Commerce Act (15 USC § 7001 et seq.) were applicable.

 

1706 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Clinton L.C. v. Lisa B.


Docket No. P-218-01M, Family File 09353


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, ULSTER COUNTY


191 Misc. 2d 211; 741 N.Y.S.2d 834; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 421



April 3, 2002, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant alleged he was the father of a minor child born out of wedlock. In a contested paternity proceeding, the claimant petitioned the trial court for the appointment of assigned counsel to represent him in the proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The minor child's presumptive father filed a paternity petition two years after the child was born, alleging himself to be the minor's father. That petition had been dismissed due to the fact that he was in a rehabilitation center. Subsequently, the presumptive father had appeared for several neglect and custody proceedings involving the minor and the minor's mother. The claimant, in requesting appointed counsel, submitted the required financial statements, and met the economic requirements for assignment of counsel. The trial court found that the claimant, in seeking to establish his paternity, was seeking to establish paternal rights which, once established, were to be protected by assigned counsel. Further, the trial court found that establishing the entitlement to parental rights was no less constitutionally protected than defending the diminution of those rights.


OUTCOME: The petition for assigned counsel was granted.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock - Paternity Proceeding - Assigned Counsel

Pursuant to Family Court Act § 262 (b), a putative father is constitutionally entitled to assigned counsel as a petitioner in a paternity proceeding where he is seeking to establish his paternity of the subject child and the assistance of counsel is necessary to enable him to prosecute the action. To deny indigent petitioners access to assigned counsel overlooks the situation where the respondent has no funds from which to reimburse the petitioner and the issues presented are complex. In seeking to establish his paternity, petitioner is seeking to establish parental rights which, once established, are to be protected by assigned counsel. Establishing the entitlement to parental rights should be no less constitutionally protected than defending against their diminution, especially when there are legal challenges to be addressed before blood tests can even be ordered.

 

1707 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Louhal Props. v. Strada


MOTION SEQ. NO. 001, INDEX NO.: 01-006581


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


191 Misc. 2d 746; 743 N.Y.S.2d 810; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 508



March 28, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Case Name and Opinion Corrected May 29, 2002. Counsel Corrected July 2, 2002.
Affirmed by, Remitted by Louhal Props. v. Strada, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9017 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Aug. 25, 2003)



DISPOSITION: The motion of 7-Eleven for summary judgment was granted; village was enjoined from enforcing the zoning ordinance or the legislation; village was ordered to proceed with processing 7-Eleven's site development application.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff businesses filed an action against defendants, the Board of Trustees of the Village of Westbury (New York), the Village of Westbury (New York) Planning Board, and village officials, seeking an order declaring that village ordinances which regulated the hours businesses could operate were invalid. A business that had applied for permission to operate a convenience store filed a motion for a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: A business had an interest in real property that was zoned for business use sought permission to operate a convenience store that would be open 24 hours a day. At the time the application was submitted, the village where the property was located had not passed any ordinance regulating the hours during which businesses could operate. In November 2000, the village board of trustees enacted Village of Westbury, N.Y., Code § 248.251.1, which prohibited businesses located within 100 feet of property zoned for residential use from operating between the hours of 11:00 p.m. and 6:00 a.m. Six months later, the village board of trustees enacted Village of Westbury, N.Y., Code § 128-1 to provide criminal penalties for violating Village of Westbury, N.Y., Code § 248.251.1. The court held that (1) to the extent the ordinances prohibited the sale of alcoholic beverages during hours when sale was permitted by the New York State Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, state law preempted them; (2) Village of Westbury, N.Y., Code § 248.251.1 was an invalid exercise of zoning power; and (3) Village of Westbury, N.Y., Code § 128-1 was an unreasonable regulatory interference with private business.


OUTCOME: The court treated the business's motion for a preliminary injunction as a motion for summary judgment and for permanent injunctive relief, granted the motion, enjoined the village from enforcing the ordinances, and ordered the village to process the business's site development application.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Invalid Restriction on Operation of 24-Hour Businesses

1. A village zoning ordinance that prohibits a business located within 100 feet of residentially zoned property from operating overnight constitutes an invalid exercise of the zoning power. The zoning ordinance does not bear a sufficient relationship to the physical use of land (see Village Law § 7-700), and thus may not be upheld. Respondent failed to adequately substantiate its claim with respect to the adverse impact of 24-hour uses on neighboring properties. Respondent failed to submit any prior complaints from residents living adjacent to 24-hour businesses regarding problems specifically created by overnight operations. Absent substantial evidence showing the external impact of the land use in question, a restriction on hours of operation constitutes an impermissible attempt to regulate the details of the operation of a business rather than a valid regulation of land use.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Invalid Exercise of Local Police Power to Regulate 24-Hour Business Operations

2. A general village ordinance that prohibits a business located within 100 feet of residentially zoned property from operating overnight constitutes an invalid exercise of the village's police power to regulate business operations. In order to justify regulatory interference with the conduct of a private business, there must be some "real or substantial evil" that the regulation is designed to cure, and a reasonable relation between such menace and the remedy proposed. Petitioner has shown that the ordinance will interfere with its customary business operations, while respondent has failed to establish the existence of a "substantial evil." Respondent Village failed to submit any prior complaints from residents living adjacent to 24-hour businesses regarding problems specifically created by overnight operations. In any event, even if the harm were substantiated by complaints based on actual experience, the ordinance would still be invalid since it is overly broad and does not bear a reasonable relationship to the harm confronted. The goals asserted by the Village could be promoted through less onerous and more effective means, such as the adoption and/or vigorous enforcement of laws specifically addressed to the levels of light and noise generated in proximity to residential property.

PEOPLE v. TAYLOR


Indictment No. 1845/2000, Indictment No. 1012/2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY, PART J-25


2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 171; 2002 NY Slip Op 50096(U)

1709 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Upshaw


Docket No. 2001NY76577


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 704; 741 N.Y.S.2d 664; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 203



March 19, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's request to dismiss both counts in the accusatory instrument denied; defendant's request for a bill of particulars and for discovery granted; defendant's motion for a Sandoval ruling was referred to the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with inciting to riot and disorderly conduct after he and his companions allegedly confronted a crowd with praise for the World Trade Center Bombing, and threatened crowd members. He filed a motion to dismiss, arguing that the accusatory instrument was facially insufficient.


OVERVIEW: The complaint alleged that defendant and his companions shouted at a crowd of about 50 people in praise of the terrorist attack on the World Trade Center and the resulting deaths; vehemently expressed their shared disappointment that the carnage had not been greater; and accosted people in the crowd, yelling in their faces. The court held that the complaint made out the elements of the crime of inciting to riot. He and his accomplices used extremely inflammatory language calculated to cause unrest in the crowd; his conduct could not be considered an expression of a political nature, intended to spur debate, that would be protected by the First Amendment. Further, it was alleged that he threatened crowd members. The elements of disorderly conduct were also made out by the complaint. Defendant's words and deeds as alleged in the complaint demonstrated his intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm, or recklessly create a risk thereof by engaging in tumultuous or threatening behavior.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Inciting to Riot - Statements Praising Terrorist Attack on World Trade Center

1. Defendant may properly be charged with inciting to riot ( Penal Law § 240.08) where, within days of the September 11, 2001 terrorist assault on the World Trade Center, defendant and several alleged accomplices shouted at a gathering crowd of people near Times Square in praise of the terrorist attack and the resulting deaths of police officers, firefighters and civilians. Defendant's words that "it's good that the World Trade Center was bombed," "more cops and firemen should have died," "more bombs should have dropped and more people should have been killed," and his additional conduct in shouting the threat "we've got something for your asses" directly into the faces of onlookers, were plainly intended to incite the crowd to violence, and not simply to express a point of view. Under the circumstances, defendant's conduct went well beyond protected speech and constituted a very real threat of violence.

Crimes - Disorderly Conduct - Statements Praising Terrorist Attack on World Trade Center

2. Defendant may properly be charged with disorderly conduct ( Penal Law § 240.20) where, within days of the September 11, 2001 terrorist assault on the World Trade Center, defendant and several alleged accomplices shouted at a gathering crowd of people near Times Square in praise of the terrorist attack and the resulting deaths of police officers, firefighters and civilians. Defendant's words, coupled with his conduct in shouting the threat "we've got something for your asses" directly into the faces of onlookers, demonstrate his "intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm, or recklessly creating a risk thereof" by engaging in "tumultuous or threatening behavior."

1713 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Levandowski


Index No. 203586


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, RENSSELAER COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 738; 738 N.Y.S.2d 552; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 64



February 20, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to vacate removal denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to vacate the instant court's previous removal, to itself from the Hoosick, New York, Town Court, of an action prosecuting her for endangering the welfare of a child. She argued that the removal was beyond the court's powers and deprived her of her right to a jury drawn from a fair cross-section of her community.


OVERVIEW: The chief judge of New York's high court approved a plan, and delegated its administration to the chief administrator, to integrate courts in Rensselaer County to permit coordinated action in related cases. Under this plan, the instant court removed to itself a criminal action commenced against defendant in the town court. When defendant challenged its action, the court held, first, that it had the power under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 19 to remove actions, even to itself, except where such removal was explicitly prohibited, and that the integrated plan under which it acted was within the N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 28 powers of the chief judge and the chief administrator. Finally, since the action was properly in the instant court, there was no violation of N.Y. Jud. Law § 500's fair cross-section requirement in drawing from the countywide pool that the instant court customarily drew from.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Transfer to Other Court - Power of Supreme Court to Remove Pending Local Criminal Court Action to Itself

1. Supreme Court has the constitutional authority to remove a pending local criminal court action to itself without the need for specific statutory authorization. NY Constitution, article VI, § 19 (a) authorizes Supreme Court, "as may be provided by law," to "transfer to itself any action or proceeding originated or pending in another court within the judicial department ... upon a finding that such a transfer will promote the administration of justice." The phrase "as may be provided by law" does not require implementing legislation before Supreme Court can remove a lower court case to itself. Rather, the Constitution grants Supreme Court full authority to remove cases to itself, subject to the regulatory power of the Legislature to adopt legislation governing the procedure for doing so.

Jury - Right to Jury Trial - Effect of Removal of Pending Local Criminal Court Action to Supreme Court

2. Supreme Court's removal of a criminal action pending in a local criminal court to itself as authorized by NY Constitution, article VI, § 19 (a) does not violate defendant's constitutional and statutory rights to be judged by a jury of her peers selected at random from a fair cross-section of the community. Defendant's right to jury trial does not require that the jury be drawn only from the local community where the charges were filed. Rather, Judiciary Law § 500 only requires that a jury panel be selected from "the community in the county or other governmental subdivision wherein the court convenes." A jury panel randomly selected from a county-wide pool represents a fair cross-section of the community in the county wherein the court will "convene" within the meaning of Judiciary Law § 500. Furthermore, defendant has failed to demonstrate that a jury panel randomly selected from a county-wide pool, rather than from the local community, would result in an unfair trial.

2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 577; 2002 NY Slip Op 50129(U)

1717 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Pataki v. N.Y. State Assembly


INDEX NO. 4864-01


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 716; 738 N.Y.S.2d 512; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 51



January 17, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transferred by Pataki v. N.Y. State Assembly, 98 N.Y.2d 644, 771 N.E.2d 832, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 1019, 744 N.Y.S.2d 759 (2002)
Affirmed by Pataki v. N.Y. State Assembly, 2004 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4746 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, Apr. 22, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff Governor awarded judgment; cross-motions of the defendants denied; implementation of decision, order and judgment stayed pending determination of the next appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff governor commenced an action against defendants, state senate, assembly, and comptroller, seeking a judgment declaring certain budget bills unconstitutional. The comptroller's motion to dismiss was granted. Intervention was denied. The parties moved and cross-moved for summary judgment, and agreed that the issues raised in the instant action presented questions of law, amenable to summary judgment resolution.


OVERVIEW: The two critical issues in the instant action were: (1) what proposals could properly be included by the governor in an appropriation bill; and (2) whether the legislature could strike out what it found to be extraneous, non-appropriation measures from the governor's proposed budget. After discussing the history of the state budgeting process, the court found that the legislature could not alter the governor's appropriation bills, except to strike out or reduce specific terms of appropriation or to add new appropriations, under N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 4. This did not mean that ultimate control of public spending would not still reside with the legislature. If the legislature decided that the policy proposals advanced by the governor with respect to how to spend the state's money, as contained in an appropriation bill or bills, were not what the law should be, the remedy was in the legislature's hands. The legislature could simply fail to enact the governor's appropriation bills. The court found that the enactment of the 37 single-purpose bills, in the instant action, was an unconstitutional attempt by the legislature to discard the governor's executive budget and write its own.


OUTCOME: The governor was awarded judgment declaring that the action of the state assembly and state senate, in enacting 46 budget bills, was unconstitutional. The cross motions for summary judgment, by the assembly and senate, were denied. Implementation of the decision, order, and judgment of the court was stayed pending the determination of the next court to which an appeal would be taken.


HEADNOTES

State - Budget - Power of Legislature to Delete Nonappropriation Measures from Governor's Budget

The New York State Assembly and Senate acted unconstitutionally by passing altered and amended versions of the nine appropriation bills submitted by the Governor in order to correct alleged constitutional deficiencies. Under the constitutional budgetary scheme, the Governor is solely responsible for formulating state fiscal policy. The ultimate approval of that policy is left to the Legislature, subject to severe restrictions upon how it can alter the Governor's appropriation and spending plans. The Governor has the option of submitting the proposed budget and "proposed legislation" to implement the budget to the Legislature in either a single bill or in multiple bills (NY Const, art VII, § 3). The appropriations and "proposed legislation" need not be set forth in separate bills. Once the proposed budget is submitted, the Legislature may not alter the Governor's appropriation bills except to strike out or reduce specific items of appropriation or add new appropriations. Consequently, the Legislature lacks the constitutional authority to strike out what it finds to be extraneous, nonappropriation measures. Furthermore, the Legislature's enactment of 37 single-purpose budget bills was an unconstitutional attempt to discard the Governor's budget and write one of its own.

PIERRE-LYS v. NEW YORK CITY TAXI & LIMOUSINE COMM'N


Index No. SCNY7048/00


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, SMALL CLAIMS TERM, NEW YORK COUNTY


2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 82; 2002 NY Slip Op 50068(U)

1719 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taylor


Indictment No. 1845/2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 124; 738 N.Y.S.2d 497; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3



January 7, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to strike denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved for an order dismissing all but one of the counts of an indictment which charged defendant with, among other things, first-degree murder committed in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(viii).


OVERVIEW: The indictment charged defendant with murder in the first degree and lesser crimes in connection with a robbery and shooting. In his motion, defendant argued that the multiple killings charged were allegedly the product of such separate and distinct criminal acts committed in the course of a single criminal transaction. Each act was alleged to have been committed with an independent specific intent to cause the death of the individual victim. The trial court found that because the killings resulted from successive and distinguishable impulses, each able to support a separate charge, the conduct alleged in the first 20 counts did not constitute a single continuing offense, and therefore need not to have been charged in a single count. Further, the trial court found that not only was it unnecessary to charge the killings in a single count as defendant proposed, but it would have been unwise. Finally, the trial court found that the statutes under which defendant was charged were not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Indictment - Multiplicitous Counts - First Degree Murder

1. An indictment charging defendant with 20 counts of first degree murder, for allegedly causing the death of five individuals as part of the same criminal transaction, is not multiplicitous. Each count named two individuals, one as a principal deceased, i.e., the person intentionally killed and the other a secondary deceased, i.e., the additional person intentionally killed as part of the same criminal transaction. Counts of an indictment are multiplicitous when they separately charge the same crime. The sequential killing of more than two persons, committed as part of the same criminal transaction in violation of Penal Law § 125.27 (1) (a) (viii), does not constitute a single continuing offense over time. A criminal transaction may involve conduct establishing more than one offense. The definition of first degree murder indicates that the crime will be committed through the commission, as part of the same criminal transaction, of two or more separate and distinct offenses, each constituting an intentional murder. Here, the multiple killings charged were allegedly the product of such separate and distinct criminal acts committed in the course of a single criminal transaction. Each act is alleged to have been committed with an independent specific intent to cause the death of the individual victim. Because the killing resulted from successive and distinguishable impulses, each able to support a separate charge, the conduct alleged in the first 20 counts does not constitute a single continuing offense, and therefore need not be charged in a single count.

Crimes - Murder - Felony Murder - Robbery

2. The count of an indictment charging defendant with first degree murder for allegedly causing the death of a person during the commission of a robbery will not be dismissed on the ground that the robbery element of that charge is unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Penal Law § 160.00 clearly defines robbery. The statute proscribes intentional killing by a defendant while in the course of committing and in furtherance of robbery (Penal Law § 125.27 [1] [a] [vii]). The count tracks this language. Terms defined by statute are not unconstitutionally vague because they are used in a newly enacted provision and have not yet received a definitive definition from the state's highest court.

Crimes - Murder - Felony Murder - Robbery

3. The counts of an indictment charging defendant with first degree and second degree felony murder, for allegedly intentionally killing a person while in the course of committing and in furtherance of a robbery, will not be dismissed on the ground that the element "in the course of and in furtherance of" is vague and overbroad. Statutory vagueness involves analyzing whether the statute is sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that the conduct is forbidden and provides explicit standards for application. A conviction for felony murder requires a showing that the killing was committed in the attempted execution of the unlawful end and in furtherance of the unlawful purpose. Defendant stands accused of having forcibly stolen money at gunpoint in a restaurant and shooting the employees to ensure no surviving witnesses. The felony murder statutes, including the elements "in the course of and in furtherance of," raise no danger of arbitrary or discriminatory application and are sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that the charged conduct was forbidden.

Crimes - Murder - Felony Murder

4. Penal Law § 125.27 (1) (a) (vii) is not underinclusive to the extent that the statute includes certain felonies and not others as predicates for first degree felony murder. A statute is presumed constitutional and will not be struck down as unconstitutional unless the invalidity is demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant has failed to make the requisite showing.

 

1720 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Neville


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, VILLAGE OF VALLEY STREAM, NASSAU COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 432; 737 N.Y.S.2d 251; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 9



January 4, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Statute prohibiting cellphone usage while operating a motor vehicle held constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On preliminary consideration of a citation charging defendant with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1225-C, which prohibited hand-held cell phone use while driving, the court, sua sponte, considered whether the new statute violated the New York or United States Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Before hearing a case charging defendant with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1225-C, a new law prohibiting use of hand-held cell phone use, the court considered the constitutionality of the statute, on its own motion, bearing in mind the presumption of constitutionality. First, because the law clearly defined the prohibited conduct, and provided for an initial period of non-enforcement, the court held that it was not vague or overbroad. Second, it held that it did not unreasonably infringe privacy rights beyond what was necessary for the state to exercise its police power to protect public safety, because drivers could use headsets or pull off the road during calls. Finally, the numerous exclusions for persons using cell phones in emergency situations did not violate equal protection, because they bore a reasonable relation to the legislative purpose of promoting public safety.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the statute was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Vagueness - Statute Prohibiting Use of Mobile Telephones while Driving

1. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c, which prohibits use of hand-held mobile telephones while operating a motor vehicle, is not unconstitutionally vague. A statute is unconstitutional if it is so vague or overly broad to the point where a reasonable person of ordinary intelligence would be unable to ascertain what conduct is prohibited. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c (2) (a) provides that "no person shall operate a motor vehicle upon a public highway using a mobile telephone to engage in a call while such vehicle is in motion." The statute distinguishes between the prohibited "mobile telephones" and the permitted "hands-free mobile telephone" where the operator can maintain "both hands" on the steering device. ( Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c [1] [e].) This language is clear and indisputable to the ordinary citizen.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Statute Prohibiting Use of Mobile Telephones while Driving

2. The State did not exceed its authority and impede privacy rights under the United States and New York Constitutions when enacting Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c, which prohibits use of hand-held mobile telephones while operating a motor vehicle. Police powers concern balancing the regulating authority of the State and the rights of the individuals whose freedoms may be curtailed. A law prohibiting the use of hand-held cell phones satisfies the State's interest in protecting the health, safety and welfare of its citizens and proper use of its police power. The legislative intent sets forth the need to protect citizens from motor vehicle accidents and serious physical injuries that result from the use of hand-held cell phones. The only impediment placed upon the public is to refrain from using a hand-held cell phone while the car is in motion. The citizen can speak after the car has stopped or may operate a "hands-free" cell phone. Accordingly, the State's regulation is reasonable in its intentions and is a valid use of the Legislature's police authority.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Statute Prohibiting Use of Mobile Telephones while Driving

3. Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c, which prohibits use of hand-held mobile telephones while operating a motor vehicle, does not violate the Due Process or Equal Protection Clauses (US Const 14th Amend) due to the fact that the statute exempts calls to an emergency response operator, hospital, ambulance, fire department, health clinic, medical doctor's office, or police department, and calls made from "authorized emergency vehicles" which include ambulances, plus police and fire department ( Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1225-c [3] [a], [b]). A legislative body may exempt certain situations and professions so long as the exclusions bear a reasonable relation to the desired legislative purpose. The law and its exclusions are not based on race, sex, age or national origins. Therefore, no strict scrutiny test requiring or compelling governmental interest is necessary. It is logical that a legislature would exempt those vehicles and professionals who need to make emergency calls as part of their employment, and extend this exemption to the ordinary citizen for these same reasons. These calls are short in duration, thus limiting the long, distracting calls that are likely to result in an accident.

Matter of William Kollmer Contr., Ltd. v Town of Huntington


00-30862


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1391; 2001 NY Slip Op 30080(U)

1724 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ellis


Indictment No. 01-017


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, CATTARAUGUS COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 98; 737 N.Y.S.2d 232; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 892



December 13, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by People v. Ellis, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10073 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Oct. 2, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to suppress the blood alcohol results granted; motion to suppress the statements made by defendant denied; motion to suppress identification testimony denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, indicted for vehicular manslaughter, DWI, and related offenses, moved to suppress statements she made to the police, a photographic identification of her, and evidence of the alcohol content of her blood.


OVERVIEW: The court held that Miranda warnings were not required before defendant made her statements to police because she was not under arrest, and subsequent questioning ended at defendant's request. Although showing one photograph for an identification was suggestive, the witness had an independent source for her identification. The blood test would not be suppressed under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194, the State asserted that defendant consented to the blood test. However, defendant was told, incorrectly, that if she did not give blood, her license or privilege to operate a vehicle would be revoked and her refusal to give blood could be used against her at a trial. Further, although she had not been arrested, the warnings given to her clearly implied, at least twice, that she had in fact been arrested. Under the circumstances, defendant's purported consent to give blood was simply a submission to authority. The State failed to meet its burden of demonstrating the legality of the police conduct in the first instance. Because of that failure, the burden never shifted to defendant to overcome the State's proof and the results of the blood test were suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress the blood alcohol results. The motion to suppress the statements made by defendant was denied. The motion to suppress the identification testimony was also denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Confession - Investigatory Questioning Following Fatal Automobile Accident

1. In defendant's prosecution for vehicular manslaughter and related offenses arising from a fatal automobile accident in which the vehicle left the road and plunged into the water, Miranda warnings were not required when the police questioned defendant at the hospital emergency room immediately after the accident in order to find out what had occurred with respect to the accident and to ascertain if anyone else was injured. The police officer responding to the scene pulled defendant out of the water and noticed that she had the odor of alcohol about her. The questioning at the hospital was brief, investigatory and ended at defendant's request when she had a visitor. Accordingly, under these circumstances, defendant's statement that she had been driving the vehicle at the time of the accident should not be suppressed.

Crimes - Identification of Defendant - Identification from Single Photograph

2. In defendant's prosecution for vehicular manslaughter and related offenses, the bartender who served defendant a drink prior to the accident and who identified defendant from a single photograph shown to her by the police may identify defendant at trial. Although showing a person a single photograph is suggestive, the bartender spent several minutes talking to defendant in the bar, and thus had an independent source for her in-court identification of defendant.

Motor Vehicles - Chemical Tests - Consent to Give Blood

3. In defendant's prosecution for vehicular manslaughter and related offenses, the blood alcohol test results from blood drawn at the hospital emergency room in the absence of a court order at a time that defendant was not yet under arrest are inadmissible where the People failed to establish that defendant validly consented to have her blood drawn. Since the criteria for applying the implied consent statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1194) are not present in this case, and defendant had not otherwise been given an alco-sensor test, the People had the burden of demonstrating by clear and convincing evidence that defendant knowingly and voluntarily consented to give blood. Defendant suffered serious injuries in the accident in which her mother and her friend were killed. Shortly after arriving at the hospital, where she was "very upset" and crying, she was incorrectly informed that if she did not give blood, her license or privilege to operate a vehicle would be revoked and her refusal to give blood could be used against her at a trial. Further, although she had not been arrested, the warnings given to her clearly implied, at least twice, that she had in fact been arrested. Under these circumstances, defendant's purported consent to give blood was simply a "submission to authority." Since the law and the consequences were misrepresented to defendant, the People failed to meet their burden of demonstrating the legality of the police conduct in the first instance.

 

1725 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Comm. for Environmentally Sound Dev. v. City of New York


Index No. 111815-01


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


190 Misc. 2d 359; 737 N.Y.S.2d 792; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 840



December 10, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name of this Case and Opinion Text has been Corrected by the Court February 12, 2002. As Corrected March 12, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Motions to dismiss granted in part, denied in part and held in abeyance in part pending an evidentiary hearing; motion for summary judgment held in abeyance..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, petitioners, a community environmental group and individual community members, sought to enjoin further construction of a city development project. Respondents consisted of city and state governments and officials (city respondents), city agencies and authorities (authority respondents), and the project's developer.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners claimed the authority respondents violated their duty under the New York State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA), N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 8-101 et seq., to take a "hard look" at the project to determine if alleged changes in the project's size required further environmental review. The court found the authority respondents sold the project's land, effectively ending their role, and the responsibility was with the city. Having found a viable claim against the city, the court ordered a factual hearing as to whether the claim was barred by the statute of limitations. The second cause of action alleged that the buildings department's approval of the project plans and issuance of a work permit exceeded its legal authority, violated the project covenant, and ran afoul of SEQRA. However, petitioners failed to exhaust administrative remedies, and the cause was dismissed. The third claim alleged an imminent change in federal regulations on carbon monoxide standards constituted a change in circumstances requiring further SEQRA review of the project. However, the regulations had not yet been enacted, and the claim was not ripe for judicial review.


OUTCOME: The authority respondents' motion to dismiss was granted in whole, the city respondents' and the developer's motions to dismiss were granted in part, denied in part, and held in abeyance in part pending a factual hearing, and the city respondents' motion for summary judgment was held in abeyance.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation - Environmental Quality Review - Continuing Responsibility of Lead Agency to Examine Project Modifications after Proposed Action is Taken

The City of New York, as the successor lead environmental review agency on a major commercial development project on property formerly owned by the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, has the continuing obligation under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) to insure that the project size limitations imposed by the 1997 final supplemental environmental impact statement (FSEIS) and the covenant executed by the project developer upon the sale of the property are followed by the developer. When the Authority respondents sold the property to the developer on July 31, 2000, they lost all decision-making authority over the project and effectively relinquished their status as lead agency to the City, which, by virtue of the covenant, is now specifically entrusted with the exclusive authority to enforce the limitations contained in the 1997 FSEIS. A lead agency's responsibility under SEQRA to examine changes to a project does not end simply because the agency has issued its statement of findings and has taken the proposed action, especially where, as here, the construction is still ongoing. The only way to fully implement the policies underlying SEQRA is to hold that a lead agency has a continuing duty to ensure that conditions derived through the SEQRA process are followed by the project developer after the proposed action has been taken (see 6 NYCRR 617.6 [b] [6] [i] [a]; 617.9 [a] [7]).

1727 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


County of Oneida v. Estate of Kennedy


Index No. 01-0197, RJI No. 32-01-0492


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ONEIDA COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 689; 734 N.Y.S.2d 402; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 464



November 15, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended January 15, 2002.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, city and county, sought judgment providing for the demolition of a burned, unsalvageable building, compensation for the costs thereof, and an injunction restraining the expenditure of fire insurance proceeds. Defendant estate sought a holding that it did not owe the cost of demolition. The estate and its individual defendants also sought a dismissal of the complaint and enforcement of an alleged contract to demolish the property.


OVERVIEW: The court found that a contract implied in law between the city and the estate by which the city would demolish the structure at no cost to the estate was void because the contract bestowed an unconstitutional gift of services upon the estate. The city could not demolish private property and not charge the owner for the cost of its services because a failure to charge was an unconstitutional gift in violation of N.Y. Const. art. 8, § 1. The estate did not dispute that the property should have been demolished, nor did it oppose the demolition. The court found that no contract was formed between the parties. While the estate did make a prima facie case for a contract implied in law, the same was not enforceable because it violated the New York State Constitution.


OUTCOME: Judgment was granted to the city and county. The estate owed the cost of demolition. An inquest to establish the amount owing was set. The estate was enjoined from expending $ 15,000 of the insurance proceeds pending the inquest.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Gift of Public Funds - Demolition of Private Property at Taxpayers' Expense - Municipality Required to Seek Reimbursement for Demolition of Public Nuisance

1. Defendant, the owner of a fire-damaged, unsalvageable building demolished by plaintiff municipalities as a public nuisance, is liable for plaintiffs' demolition costs. There are no enforceable contracts which would release defendant from the obligation to reimburse plaintiffs for the cost of demolition. The contract implied in law claimed by defendant by which plaintiffs allegedly agreed to demolish defendant's structure at no cost to defendant would be void and unenforceable as violative of the constitutional prohibition against a municipality bestowing a gift of public funds or services for the benefit of a private party (NY Const, art VIII, § 1). If a municipality remediates a nuisance or violation of law as a service for a private property owner, the constitutional gifts provision mandates that the municipality make all reasonable efforts to obtain reimbursement for the cost of the remediation. Moreover, constitutional requirements cannot be satisfied by the imposition of a special assessment charge against the property (see, General City Law § 20 [35]) where the property is not of sufficient value to reimburse for the cost of demolition. Plaintiffs' customs and practices of allowing substantially deteriorated privately owned property to be foreclosed for unpaid taxes, and then demolishing the property without seeking full restitution, is unconstitutional under the gifts provision.

Constitutional Law - Gift of Public Funds - Demolition of Private Property at Taxpayers' Expense - Selective Enforcement

2. Defendant, the owner of a fire-damaged, unsalvageable building demolished by plaintiff municipalities as a public nuisance, is liable for plaintiffs' demolition costs. Defendant may not assert a defense of "selective enforcement" based upon plaintiffs' long-standing policy of not seeking restitution from property owners for demolition costs and allowing property not in code compliance to be taken for unpaid real estate taxes. While plaintiffs' code enforcement has previously been inadequate, defendant cannot demonstrate that plaintiffs' decision to now seek reimbursement was predicated upon "invidious discrimination" against a constitutionally protected group. Plaintiffs have a duty to enforce their housing and sanitary codes and to promptly abate public nuisances which may affect the public health (see, Public Health Law § 1304). Plaintiffs violated the constitutional prohibition against a municipality bestowing a gift of public funds or services for the benefit of a private party (NY Const, art VIII, § 1) by delaying the enforcement of the codes and allowing nuisances to exist until taking a property for taxes and then demolishing damaged structures at taxpayers' expense. Under these circumstances, defendant cannot be deemed to be a member of a class suffering "invidious discrimination."

1734 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Mark N. v. Runaway Homeless Youth Shelter


Docket No. V-416-94


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, CHAUTAUQUA COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 245; 733 N.Y.S.2d 566; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 331



September 21, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 5, 2001.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father filed a writ of habeas corpus for the return of his daughter who was staying in a shelter run by respondent safehouse, claiming that N.Y. Exec. Law § 532-b was unconstitutional as applied.


OVERVIEW: The father sought the return of his daughter from the safehouse after she ran away. The safehouse refused to return her prior to the expiration of the 30-day period set forth in § 532-b. The father, therefore, brought a writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the intent of § 532-b was to reunite families. The safehouse acted in place of the state to protect the daughter's interests by offering her a safe place to reside and other needed assistance. However, the father's interests in the immediate return of his daughter also had to be considered. By not providing a balancing of the interests, § 532-b placed the safehouse in a position that was superior to the father. He could file a writ of habeas corpus prior to the expiration of the 30-day period.


OUTCOME: The statute was unconstitutional as applied. The father was entitled to a hearing to determine the best interests of the daughter.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Runaway and Homeless Youth Act of 1978 - Right of Parent to File Writ of Habeas Corpus during First 30 Days of Runaway's Stay in Safehouse

The Runaway and Homeless Youth Act of 1978, which provides that a runaway youth "may" remain in an approved Safehouse for a period not to exceed 30 days from the date of admission ( Executive Law § 532-b [2]), does not prohibit the parent of a runaway child residing in such a Safehouse from filing a writ of habeas corpus seeking return of the child prior to the expiration of the 30-day period. Preventing a parent from applying to the court for a period of 30 days does not promote the purpose of the Act, which is to reunite families while also providing "appropriate services to help runaway youth cope with their problems." In the interests of reuniting a family, a parent must be able to address the issues that drove the family apart. A prohibition, even for a period of 30 days, is an infringement of the parent's due process rights. Consequently, Executive Law § 532-b would be unconstitutional as applied if construed to preclude the parent from filing a writ of habeas corpus for the first 30 days. In balancing both the child's and parent's due process rights, a hearing is required to determine whether the best interests of the child will be served by remaining in the Safehouse or by the return of the child. The child will have the burden of showing by a preponderance of the evidence that his or her best interests would be served by not returning home immediately.

1736 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Anonymous v. Peters


21404


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 203; 730 N.Y.S.2d 689; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 317



September 12, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 30, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A report of suspected child abuse was filed with the Nassau Department of Social Services (New York) with respect to petitioner father's children. The father sought to have his name removed from the New York State Central Registry. The application for expungement was denied. The matter was referred to the Bureau of Special Hearings. The father commenced the instant proceeding by order to show cause and secured a stay of the fair hearing.


OVERVIEW: A father sought declaration that sections of the social services law, as applied to him, were violative of procedural due process on the grounds that the statutes and regulations failed to mandate a hearing prior to submission of a name to the registry and failed to mandate a time in which a hearing had to be conducted. The supreme court rejected the registrar's argument that the father lacked standing because the father sufficiently demonstrated that his inclusion in the registry satisfied the "stigma plus" standard which invoked the protections of procedural due process by showing that inclusion could impact his future employment or adoption opportunities. The court held that the challenged statutes were constitutional and reasonable because the inconvenience experienced by the father was far outweighed by the strong need to protect children from abuse. The imperative of due process was satisfied where a report was substantiated by a fair preponderance of the evidence and was not disseminated when the report was not relevant to the inquiring agency. The failure to set forth a time in which a hearing had to occur could be remedied by a proceeding to compel a hearing.


OUTCOME: The petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Constitutional Challenge to Entry of Name in Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment

1. Petitioner, whose name was placed on the New York State Central Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment pursuant to Social Services Law § 422 based solely upon the initial "indicated" finding of child abuse involving his four children, has standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statutory scheme as being violative of due process by permitting respondents to enter a name in the Register prior to conducting a fair hearing, and failing to provide a time limit in which a hearing must be conducted after a request to expunge. Although mere stigma alone is insufficient to invoke procedural due process protection, given the nature and severity of an "indicated" child abuse determination, petitioner has sufficiently demonstrated that his inclusion in the Register satisfies the "stigma plus" standard that invokes the protections of procedural due process. The reality of the impact of the inclusion of one's name in a register of child abusers is sufficient to satisfy the "stigma plus" test.

Social Services - Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment - Procedural Due Process Requirements

2. Petitioner, whose name was placed on the New York State Central Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment pursuant to Social Services Law § 422 based solely upon the initial "indicated" finding of child abuse involving his four children, has failed to demonstrate that the statutory scheme is violative of due process because it does not require a hearing prior to his name being entered into the Register. The statutory scheme provides for the investigation of an initial report of abuse by the local child protective agency. That investigation results in a determination either that the report was unfounded, in which case the matter is closed, or a determination that the report was "indicated," based on "credible evidence," in which case the report is kept on file in the Register. Procedural due process only requires that a report be substantiated by a fair preponderance of the evidence at a hearing, or that the time to move for expungement have expired prior to the dissemination of the information to providers or licensing agencies. Consequently, the use of a lower "credible evidence" standard during the investigation stage is not violative of due process. The interest of children to be shielded from abuse must take precedence, especially since a party's rights are otherwise amply protected in the statutory scheme before a report of abuse can be disseminated. Furthermore, the fact that the statutory scheme and its regulations set no specific time limit for the holding of an expungement hearing does not constitute a constitutional infirmity.

BOTA v. MUNICIPAL CIV. SERV. COMM'N OF NEW ROCHELLE
