1738 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. P.S.


21396


JUSTICE COURT OF NEW YORK, TOWN OF EASTCHESTER, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 71; 731 N.Y.S.2d 341; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 309



September 5, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 3, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a mother, was charged with aggravated harassment in the second degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30-1, and moved to dismiss the charge pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.40 and pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.40.


OVERVIEW: A mother moved to dismiss harassment charges under N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30-1 alleging that under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.40 the accusatory instrument failed to make a prima facie case for harassment. She claimed that the instrument lacked facts showing the necessary intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm the victim because she did not speak to the victim, did not ask to speak to the victim, and did not know that the victim would be eavesdropping on the threatening call taken by a third person. She alleged that the charges should be dismissed in the interest of justice. The motion was denied because the mother called the place of employment of the victim and left a threatening message. The language and elements of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30-1 were satisfied by the communication intentionally made to one individual which caused alarm to the victim. Nor did applying N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30-1 to the mother offend due process, as the threatened harm was specific in time, place, and short duration. The mother established no grounds for dismissal in the interests of justice.


OUTCOME: The defendant's motions to dismiss were denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Threatening Telephone Call Directed at Third Party

1. An accusatory instrument charging defendant with aggravated harassment ( Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) for making an annoying and threatening phone call to a hospital nurse directed at complainant, the program director of children services in the hospital where defendant's five-year-old son was a patient, is sufficient on its face even though defendant did not actually communicate the threat directly to complainant. The accusatory instrument alleges that there was verbally aggressive behavior by defendant toward complainant over a period of four weeks, followed by six telephone calls by defendant to complainant within the two hours preceding the threats conveyed via the agitated telephone message left with the nurse. Under these circumstances, the statute encompasses complainant as an intended victim. The elements of the crime are satisfied by a communication intentionally made to one individual which causes alarm to a third person. The statute does not require that the subject of the harassing communication be the same person to whom the communication is made. Moreover, whether complainant overheard the phone conversation or was advised of it by the direct recipient is of no consequence, since the nature of the communication was such that defendant's threat was reasonably intended by her to be passed on to complainant.

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Constitutionality of Statute - Threatening Telephone Call Directed at Third Party

2. Defendant may lawfully be charged with aggravated harassment ( Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) for making an annoying and threatening phone call to a hospital nurse directed at complainant, the program director of children services in the hospital where defendant's five-year-old son was a patient. The harassment statute, as applied to defendant, is not violative of defendant's rights to freedom of speech. The accusatory instrument alleges that there was verbally aggressive behavior by defendant toward complainant over a period of four weeks, followed by six telephone calls by defendant to complainant within the two hours preceding the threats conveyed via the agitated telephone message left with the nurse. Under these circumstances, a jury reasonably could find that defendant's communications escalated in intensity and specificity to the point that they instilled fear of impending or imminent, if not immediate, danger of severe harm in the mind of the alleged victim. Furthermore, the statute, as applied, is not so vague and overbroad as to violate defendant's due process rights. The very nature of the alleged threat in issue, and its communication to the institution where the victim is employed, removes the possibility that defendant could have been unaware of the criminal nature of her conduct.

People v. Amadeo


Indictment No. 3523/2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, CRIMINAL TERM, QUEENS COUNTY


2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 406; 2001 NY Slip Op 40190U



August 1, 2001 Decided


NOTICE:  [*1] THIS OPINION IS UNCORRECTED AND WILL NOT BE PUBLISHED IN THE OFFICIAL REPORTS.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion denied.

 

1742 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cullinan


Docket No. 2000NY051819


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 699; 729 N.Y.S.2d 385; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 247



July 30, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss granted at arraignment. CPL § 140.45.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss an information charging him with playing loud music on public transportation, in violation of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 21, § 1050.7(e), arguing that the provision was unconstitutionally vague and that the information was facially insufficient.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was cited for violation of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 21, § 1050.7(e), after he allegedly played loud music on public transportation. He challenged the provision as unconstitutionally vague. The court held that the regulation described the offense with all the specificity required. The court also held, however, that the information charging the violation had not described defendant's alleged violation with any specificity at all. The regulation identified particular levels of noise as illegal, but defendant's noise level had not been measured at all. Therefore, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.45 required dismissal of the charge at the arraignment.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the information at arraignment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Regulation - Vagueness - Excessive Noise

1. 21 NYCRR 1050.7 (e), which states that "No person on or in any [transit] facility or conveyance shall ... (e) create any sound through the use of any sound production device, except as authorized by section 1050.6(c) of this Part," but permits "use of radios and other devices listened to solely by headphones or earphones and inaudible to others," is not unconstitutionally vague. The regulation incorporates by reference the provisions of another regulation scientifically defining "excessive noise." That regulation specifies maximum sound levels permitted on subway trains and sets forth the manner in which sound is to be measured. Accordingly, the regulations in question provide adequate notice to the public of the forbidden conduct and contain objective standards for law enforcement personnel.

Crimes - Information - Legal Sufficiency - Disorderly Conduct - Excessive Noise

2. An information charging defendant with disorderly conduct in violation of a regulation prohibiting the playing of radios on subways is facially insufficient. The regulation prohibits noise in transit facilities which exceeds certain levels as measured in a specified way. Under this provision, an accusatory instrument is not facially sufficient if it merely states that a radio is audible. Because the information does not allege that the sound emitted by defendant's radio was measured and that it exceeded the permissible levels, the accusatory instrument fails to state every element of the offense and is thus jurisdictionally defective. Moreover, the failure to measure the radio's sound made it impossible for the People to draft and file a facially sufficient information.

 

1743 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


In re New York Urban Dev. Corp.


Index Number: 406492/94, Index Number: 95/403586, Index Number: 405058/94, Index Number: 406497/94 (498000/94)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 303; 731 N.Y.S.2d 921; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 352



July 24, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended November 5, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Court fixed rate of pre-judgment interest at 9% per annum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Condemnor and claimant both filed motions to re-settle the judgment to be entered as it related to interest on condemnation awards made on properties taken in prior vesting actions. Claimant sought pre-judgment interest at the nine percent permitted by the statute, while condemnor urged interest at six percent.


OVERVIEW: Condemnor asked the court to consider the "virtual risk free" nature of the condemnation award as warranting a lower rate. The court noted various appellate and trial courts had sustained the nine percent rate for relevant interest periods, essentially determining that the various objectants to the nine percent rate failed to persuade that the presumptively reasonable statutory rate was "unreasonably high." The court observed it did not appear that under the facts, there could be any further arguments advanced for a lower rate that would be any more persuasive than those raised and rejected by both the appellate and trial courts. Accordingly, the court opined that the nine percent rate had sufficiently and significantly withstood challenge both at the trial and appellate level to warrant its preclusive application to the interest periods at issue without further examination as to its propriety. Further, the court advised a finding of a lesser rate for the judgments than that fixed by the court for condemnation awards for relevant interest periods would be unconstitutionally discriminatory, and therefore unreasonable and palpably improper.


OUTCOME: The court fixed the rate of pre-judgment interest to be paid at nine percent per annum until the date of payment.


HEADNOTES

Interest - Prejudgment Interest - Condemnation Award - Propriety of Statutory 9% Rate

The statutory maximum 9% prejudgment interest rate ( CPLR 5004) is the appropriate rate to be applied to the condemnation awards on properties taken in 1994 and 1995. To ensure that a condemnee obtains just compensation, the state is constitutionally required to pay prejudgment interest for delay in making payment and deprivation of use of the property. The 9% rate has sufficiently and significantly withstood judicial challenge to warrant its preclusive application herein without further examination as to its propriety. Uncertainty as to the judgment rate would turn entry of judgment, presently a clerk-administered function, into a postverdict litigated matter further burdening the trial court. Furthermore, any differentiation of rates for condemnation awards covering the same interest periods would be unconstitutionally discriminatory, and therefore "unreasonable and palpably improper."

 

1744 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Humane Soc'y of N.Y. v. City of New York


Index No. 121839/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 735; 729 N.Y.S.2d 360; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 263



July 4, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion for discovery denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a declaratory judgment action by plaintiff humane society and others challenging defendant New York City Board of Health's amendment to New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 42, § 161.01, designating ferrets as prohibited wild animals, an individual plaintiff moved for leave to depose public officials and to discover documents outside the official record.


OVERVIEW: New York City's Board of Health amended New York City, N.Y., R. tit. 42, § 161.01, which listed wild animals that could not be kept as pets in the city, to include ferrets. An individual plaintiff joined the humane society's challenge to the rule. Because of his previous activities as an advocate of ferrets as pets, the individual plaintiff believed the board's action was motivated in part by individual members' animosity toward him. He therefore sought to depose several board members and other officials and to discover written materials, not part of the agency record, that might indicate improper motives. The court held that since the legislature had empowered the board to make all health-related legislative decisions relating to New York City, the board's processes were protected by the speech and debate clause of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 11. Therefore, the materials sought were all privileged.


OUTCOME: The court denied the individual plaintiff's discovery motion.


HEADNOTES

Disclosure - Protective Order - Information Protected by Common-Law Legislative Immunity Privilege - Adoption of Rule Prohibiting Wild Animals

Plaintiff's request for depositions from members of the New York City Board of Health and other public officials in connection with his challenge to the validity of the 1999 amendment to the New York City Health Code, which established a list of prohibited wild animals that includes ferrets (24 RCNY 161.01), is barred by the common-law legislative immunity privilege applicable to government officials in the executive branch when engaged in legislative activities. The common-law privilege applies to the adoption of legislative-type rules by an agency such as the Board of Health. The privilege ordinarily precludes disclosure as to the officials' deliberations and motivations in passing a rule or regulation. The applicability of the privilege may not be avoided based on plaintiff's unsupported allegations that the members of the Board of Health acted from bias against him or against pet ferrets. Nor may the privilege be avoided based on plaintiff's claim that he only seeks to inquire into what the officials' "actual conduct was prior to making their decision." Plaintiff's request for depositions is prohibited by the privilege regardless of how plaintiff seeks to characterize the nature of the inquiry. Furthermore, plaintiff's request for documents outside the official record which may have been considered and rejected by the Board of Health in connection with its adoption of the challenged rule is likewise barred by the legislative immunity privilege in the absence of any objective evidence tending to show that the impetus for the rule was illicit bias or prejudice.

 

1745 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Zwack


21360, INDEX NO. 201009, INDICTMENT NO. 1 and 2


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, RENSSELAER COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 761; 729 N.Y.S.2d 846; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 277



June 25, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Movants, media sources, filed a motion seeking permission to be allowed to conduct audiovisual and still photographic coverage of the underlying criminal trial and subsequent proceedings. Both requests were based upon the argument that N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 was unconstitutional, and assuming, arguendo, that it was not, that it did not apply to still photography.


OVERVIEW: The court held that N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 by its express terms did not prohibit still photographic coverage of in-court proceedings. The prohibition only related to televising, broadcasting, or taking of motion pictures, and not still photography. Therefore, the request to conduct still photographic coverage of all trial and related proceedings was granted. However, the court held that it lacked authority to entertain the motion arguing that the ban on audio-visual coverage was unconstitutional. N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 imposed an absolute, generalized ban on audio-visual coverage of trial court proceedings, no matter what the circumstances of the case or the assessment of the proceeding judge. Where a movant had no constitutional or statutory right to broadcast, the court was without authority to permit it to intervene. Rather than moving in county court for an order permitting audiovisual coverage, the movant should have commenced a declaratory judgment action in the supreme court challenging the constitutionality of the statute.


OUTCOME: The request to conduct still photographic coverage of the trial and related proceedings was granted, but a request to provide audio-visual coverage was denied.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Audiovisual Coverage of Judicial Proceedings - Still Photography Permitted

1. An application by the media to conduct still photographic coverage of all trial and related proceedings in defendant's criminal prosecution is granted. Civil Rights Law § 52, which bans audiovisual coverage of certain proceedings, does not prohibit still photographic coverage of in-court proceedings.

Courts - Audiovisual Coverage of Judicial Proceedings - Authority of County Court to Entertain Motion to Conduct Audiovisual Coverage

2. County Court does not have the authority to entertain a motion by the media to conduct audiovisual coverage of defendant's criminal trial. The movant has no constitutional or statutory right to broadcast. It should instead have commenced a declaratory judgment action in Supreme Court challenging the constitutionality of Civil Rights Law § 52. However, the denial of movant's request relates only to subpoenaed testimony of witnesses, and to no other portion of the trial.

 

1746 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Webster v. Ryan


FF #: 1448D


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


189 Misc. 2d 86; 729 N.Y.S.2d 315; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 264



June 21, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 11, 2001.



DISPOSITION: The Court will conduct a standing hearing in this case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon remand from the Appellate Division (New York), the family court returned custody of the child to respondent father, and rejected the claims of petitioner foster mother seeking visitation and custody rights to the child. However, the family court reserved the question of whether the child had an independent constitutional right to seek visitation with the foster mother and allowed the parties to file briefs on the issue.


OVERVIEW: The family court answered the question of whether the child had an independent constitutional right to seek visitation with the foster mother in the affirmative. It held, generally, that a child had a constitutional right to maintain contact with a person with whom he had developed a parent-like relationship. Accompanying that right, was a right to the equal protection of the laws. This required that a child have the due process necessary to claim his right. This claim could be given constitutional protection, while at the same time giving due recognition, respect, and protection to a parent's constitutional right to the custody, care, and control of his or her child. The family court then outlined a procedure by which the fundamental right of the father to direct the care, custody, and guardianship of his child could be balanced with the fundamental rights of the child. The procedure would consist of a standing hearing to determine if the child had a parent-like relationship with the foster mother, with whom contact was desired; and upon a finding of standing, a subsequent hearing would be held to determine whether continued contact was in the child's best interest.


OUTCOME: The family court determined that it would conduct a standing hearing in the case.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation - Visitation Rights of Person with Parent-Like Relationship

1. A child has a constitutional right to maintain contact, over the objection of a parent, with a person with whom the child has developed a parent-like relationship. The historical development of family law in America and the expansion of individual constitutional rights by the United States Supreme Court and the New York State Court of Appeals support this holding. A child has certain enumerated and unenumerated constitutionally protected rights which are protected coextensively by the United States and New York Constitutions. The child's unenumerated rights include a fundamental right to maintain contact with a person with whom the child has developed a parent-like relationship. The child also has an enumerated First Amendment freedom of association right under the New York and United States Constitutions to maintain personal relations with a nonbiologically related person. These rights are liberty interests protected by the Due Process Clauses of the United States and New York Constitutions. Furthermore, equal protection of the laws entitles the child to be similarly situated with other children who have a statutory procedure to enforce constitutionally or statutorily protected association rights. A child having such a right must be provided a process to enforce that right against unwarranted restrictions by the state or the parent. The fundamental right of the parent to direct the care, custody and guardianship of his or her child must be balanced with the fundamental rights of the child.

Parent and Child - Visitation - Visitation Rights of Person with Parent-Like Relationship

2. In determining whether a child has a constitutional right to maintain contact, over the objection of a parent, with a person with whom the child has developed a parent-like relationship, the child initially must allege facts which, if proven, would establish that the child has developed a parent-like relationship with another person and that his or her custodial parent has refused contact or arbitrarily restricted established contact. The court will then conduct a standing hearing. Upon a finding of standing, the court will conduct a hearing to determine if continued contact is in the child's best interest and to what extent such contact is appropriate. In determining what is in the child's best interest, the court will take into account the circumstances usually considered when making such determinations and also consider: how the relationship was formed and whether the parent objected or consented to any aspect of the relationship's formation and continuation; the length, nature and quality of the relationship; and whether any court-ordered contact would significantly interfere with the parent-child relationship or with the parent's rightful authority over the child. It will be presumed that the parent's decision to restrict or terminate contact is in the child's best interest. The child will carry the burden of proving all relevant issues by a preponderance of the evidence. The court must give significant weight to the parent's determination as to what is in the child's best interest, including the decision to allow a certain level of contact.

 

1747 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Diaz


Ind. No. 7262-00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 341; 727 N.Y.S.2d 298; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 204



June 15, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A grand jury indicted the defendant for assault in the third degree as a felony, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 120.00(1) and the Hate Crimes Act of 2000, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 485.00-.10. The defendant filed a pretrial motion challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 485.05, .10.


OVERVIEW: The defendant attacked a stranger while the stranger was returning home from a bar frequented by homosexual men. The victim told police that the defendant repeatedly cursed him and called him a "faggot" and a "degenerate" during the attack, and the defendant told police that he attacked the victim because he was angry about comments he heard on a radio talk show about homosexual men molesting children and had decided to vent his anger by attacking a person he thought was homosexual. The defendant was charged with a felony because he had selected his victim based on his belief that the victim was homosexual. The trial court held that (1) there was a presumption that the Hate Crimes Act of 2000, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 485.00-.10, was valid and the defendant had the burden of demonstrating that the statute was unconstitutional; (2) N.Y. Penal Law §§ 485.05(1)(a), 485.05(1)(b), 485.10 did not increase punishment for thought alone, but for behavior impelled by thought; (3) the Hate Crimes Act of 2000 did not lend itself to arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement; and (4) the Hate Crimes Act of 2000 was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The trial court found that the Hate Crimes Act was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Hate Crimes - Constitutionality of Hate Crimes Act

The Hate Crimes Act of 2000 (Penal Law §§ 485.00-485.10), which enhances the penalties for certain crimes committed because of the offender's bias or prejudice, is not unconstitutionally vague. The language of the Act is clear and can reasonably be understood by all citizens to prohibit certain kinds of attacks made against others who have been selected by the offender because of a perception as to the victim's special vulnerability, appearance, or background. The notions embodied in the Act have long been used in the sentencing process to enhance penalties for criminal behavior. The Act simply provides that such enhancement may take place in every case where such victim selection has occurred by permitting the prosecution to submit such behavior to the trier of fact as an additional element of the underlying offense. Moreover, the language of the Act does not lend itself to arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. The Act provides objective criteria which must be observed throughout the accusatory process, and does not provide for enforcement according to the subjective conclusions of the complainant, police or prosecutor.

 

1748 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Yellow Book of Ny L.P. v. Dimilia


INDEX NO. 11639/00


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, NASSAU COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 489; 729 N.Y.S.2d 286; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 202



May 31, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a collection action by plaintiff Yellow Pages publisher, the publisher moved for reargument after defendant advertiser moved for vacation of a default judgment entered against her. The court had previously deferred action pending hearings on factual issues raised in the pleadings; the publisher argued that hearings were not required.


OVERVIEW: Defendant legally unsophisticated house cleaner contracted to advertise in the publisher's Yellow Pages. She never paid the bill, and the publisher obtained a default judgment against her, which the advertiser sought to vacate. The publisher cited two unpublished decisions in cases in which it had been successful in support of its argument that a hearing on the issues raised by the advertiser was not required. The court held that under the circumstances, policy considerations precluded reliance on unpublished cases even as persuasive; furthermore, their relevance was not clear. The advertiser raised important factual issues relating to service of process, clarity of a purported contractual agreement conferring jurisdiction, and whether the advertiser agreed to personal liability on the contract or signed on behalf of a corporate business.


OUTCOME: The court granted reargument but adhered to its earlier determination to conduct hearings on the three contested factual issues.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Stare Decisis - Effect of Unpublished Summary Decision of Appellate Court - Grounds to Vacate Default Judgment

Defendant is entitled to a factual hearing on her motion to vacate a default judgment entered against her for an unpaid fee for advertising published by plaintiff. Factual issues exist as to whether service was properly effectuated on a member of defendant's household, whether defendant has a meritorious defense because it is unclear from the single signature line utilized on plaintiff's form contract whether defendant was binding herself personally on behalf of a sole proprietorship or was merely acting on behalf of corporate party, and whether the language in the parties' contract providing that the contract is "deemed transacted in Nassau County" is sufficient to confer personal jurisdiction over defendant. Furthermore, on plaintiff's motion for reargument, an unpublished summary decision of the Appellate Term involving a similar claim for unpaid advertising fees in which it was determined that the agreement therein "clearly and unambiguously made the defendant individually liable" is not entitled to be given controlling stare decisis effect in the absence of a recitation of any of the adjudicated facts. Likewise, a second unpublished summary decision of the Appellate Term in which it was determined that the same jurisdictional clause "was sufficient to establish the basis for personal jurisdiction over defendant" cannot be considered binding precedent in the absence of the specific legal grounds upon which the decision was based.

 

1749 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Amadeo


Indictment No. 3523/2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 187; 727 N.Y.S.2d 290; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 163



May 22, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with attempted murder in the second degree as a hate crime and related offenses. Defendant asked the court to permit him to file a motion challenging the constitutionality of the hate crimes legislation, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 485.00. Defendant filed an order to show cause as directed by the court and the People responded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought leave of the court to file an untimely motion challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 485.00. The court held that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 255.20 required that all pretrial motions be included in the same set of motion papers and that the motion was required to be served or filed within 45 days of arraignment. Defendant relied on the second of the two exceptions contained in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 255.20, that the untimely motion should be heard and decided for good cause shown and in the interest of justice. The court held that it was the duty of the court to protect the fundamental rights of all citizens. The court found there were adverse consequences which preclusion of the motion might have had on the defendant's fundamental rights. If defendant could have mounted a legitimate challenge to the constitutionality of the statute, preclusion of that challenge based upon the possible oversights or carelessness of his counsel might have resulted in reversal of any conviction on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The court exercised its interest of justice discretion to allow defendant's counsel to file the motion.


OUTCOME: Defendant's counsel was allowed to file the motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Pretrial Motions - Permission to File Late Motion

Defendant's motion for leave to file a motion challenging the constitutionality of the recently enacted "Hate Crimes" legislation after the 45-day deadline for filing pretrial motions contained in CPL 255.20 has passed is granted in the interest of justice. CPL 255.20 requires that all pretrial motions be included in the same set of motion papers and served or filed within 45 days of arraignment. A defendant who has failed to act in a timely fashion may still be entitled to relief if the consequences of his or her tardiness would affect the accuracy and fairness of the truth-finding process of the trial. The constitutionality of the Hate Crimes legislation remains an open question for the courts to consider, and a preclusion of that challenge based on the oversight or carelessness of defendant's counsel in not making a timely challenge could possibly result in the reversal of any conviction on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.

 

1750 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Kellogg v. Travis


Index No. 111547/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 164; 728 N.Y.S.2d 645; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 148



May 18, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants parole board moved to dismiss plaintiff parolee's declaratory action to determine if N.Y. Executive Law § 995(7) was an unconstitutional ex post facto law and violated the double jeopardy clause. He claimed that taking blood for DNA testing was an illegal search.


OVERVIEW: Parolee was told he had to give a blood sample for a DNA data bank or else his parole would be violated. The law that required the sample was passed after parolee was convicted. He allowed the sample to be taken under protest. He then filed a declaratory action to have the law declared unconstitutional. The court held that the law was not an ex post facto law since it did not change the definition of any criminal conduct and was not punitive. In addition, parolee was not prosecuted nor punished a second time for the same crimes. Thus, there was no double jeopardy issue. Taking of the blood sample was not so invasive as to constitute an unreasonable search and seizure. The sample was to be used in a statewide data bank and not in an investigation of parolee for a particular crime. Parolee failed to prove that the law was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The law was declared constitutional, and the motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - DNA Identification Index - Constitutionality of Executive Law § 995 

1. The amendment to Executive Law § 995 (7), which expanded the definition of designated offenders who must provide a sample for DNA testing for inclusion in a computerized state DNA identification index to include offenders who committed their offenses prior to the date the law became effective but whose sentences had not been completed before that date, is not an unconstitutional ex post facto law. Although the amendment is applicable to plaintiff regarding events that occurred before the enactment of the amendment and did disadvantage him, the amendment did not change the definition of any criminal conduct and is not punitive in nature. The intent of the amendment was not to punish persons such as plaintiff for past behavior but to expand the definition of persons who must submit tissue samples to the state DNA data bank pursuant to Executive Law § 995-c so as to assist law enforcement officials in accurately solving crimes committed by persons already convicted of serious crimes and thus promoting public safety.

Crimes - DNA Identification Index - Constitutionality of Executive Law § 995 

2. The amendment to Executive Law § 995 (7), which expanded the definition of designated offenders who must provide a sample for DNA testing for inclusion in a computerized state DNA identification index to include plaintiff and other offenders who committed their offenses prior to the date the law became effective but whose sentences had not been completed before that date, does not unconstitutionally place defendant in double jeopardy. Plaintiff was neither prosecuted nor punished a second time for the crimes for which he was convicted, and the statute is not punitive.

Crimes - DNA Identification Index - Taking of DNA Sample Not Unconstitutional Search and Seizure

3. The taking of a blood sample from plaintiff, a "designated offender" required to provide a sample for DNA testing for inclusion in a computerized state DNA identification index as that term is defined in Executive Law § 995 (7), without probable cause or a warrant was not so invasive as to be deemed an unconstitutional search and seizure. The sample was not being taken for the purpose of investigating whether plaintiff committed a particular crime. Moreover, the statute does not even require that a designated offender give a blood sample; other tissue samples collected in a less obtrusive manner can be utilized.

PEOPLE v. DEF-89, JOHN F. OWENS

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 458; 2001 NY Slip Op 40041U

1752 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


First Baptist Church of Crown Heights Ctr. for Nursing & Rehab., Inc. v. Novello


Index No. 42185/00, Index No. 42187/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 330; 728 N.Y.S.2d 881; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 207



April 24, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitions granted to the extent that moratorium issued by the Department is vacated and respondents are directed to continue to process Petitioners' applications in conformity with applicable statutes, regulations and the Department's own practices, patterns, and procedures.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nursing home builders sought mandamus relief to compel respondent Department of Health of the State of New York to continue processing their pending applications for the construction and establishment of nursing homes.


OVERVIEW: The builders were in the process of complying with contingencies on their applications to build nursing homes, when the department issued a moratorium on all such applications. The builders filed motions in the nature of mandamus for orders that the department had an affirmative duty to continue processing their applications. They claimed the moratorium constituted a rule made in violation of the state administrative procedures act. The court held that absent a revised public needs determination, the current determination continued in effect. The moratorium violated the builders' due process rights. In addition, it was in the form of a rule, which required a public needs determination pursuant to the administrative procedures act. The board was without authority to make an across-the-board determination of indefinite duration without authorizing legislation.


OUTCOME: The petitions were granted to the extent that the department's moratorium was vacated and it was directed to continue to process the applications.


HEADNOTES

Health - Nursing Homes - Moratorium on Nursing Home Applications

The moratorium issued by the New York State Department of Health (Department) on the further processing of nursing home applications that have been approved by the Public Health Council, but not yet approved by the Department to begin construction, is vacated, and respondent Commissioner is directed to continue processing petitioners' applications. The moratorium--which has no end date--denies petitioners due process since it has the practical effect of imperiling their projects without providing them with any redress or mechanism of appeal. While, initially, compelling the Department to process the applications is contingent upon its discretionary finding as to the public need for construction of the proposed nursing homes, such a finding has been made and is in effect. Respondent is not being compelled to make any particular determination but, rather, to follow the procedures mandated by law relative to the processing of petitioners' applications (see, Public Health Law § 206 [1] [a]). Moreover, the moratorium is a rule, and respondent failed to comply with the rule-making requirements of the State Administrative Procedure Act before imposing the moratorium.

 

1753 of 1817 DOCUMENTS



Patrolmen's Benevolent Ass'n v. N.Y. State Pub. Empl. Rels. Bd.


INDEX NO. 7663-00, (RJI No. 01-00-064679)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 146; 728 N.Y.S.2d 875; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 143



April 16, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, public employees' union and others, filed suit seeking a declaration that 1998 N.Y. Laws ch. 641 did not violate the Home Rule provision of the state constitution, N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2, and moved for summary judgment. Defendants, the City of New York (city) and state and city agencies, filed a cross-motion seeking a declaration that Chapter 641 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In 1996, the New York State Legislature enacted 1996 N.Y. Laws ch. 13 over the governor's veto. That statute transferred impasse jurisdiction over collective bargaining disputes between the City of New York and the Patrolmen's Benevolent Association (PBA) from the Board of Collective Bargaining of the City of New York (BCB) to the New York State Public Relations Board (PERB). However, a court ruled that Chapter 13 was unconstitutional because it applied only to New York City and, as such, violated N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2. In response to that ruling, the legislature enacted 1998 N.Y. Laws ch. 641. Chapter 641 extended PERB's impasse jurisdiction to the entire state, and removed restrictions on that jurisdiction with respect to collective bargaining negotiations with the New York City police. The court held that Chapter 641 did not violate N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2 because it was a general law that applied to all local governments and that the legislature may act by general law in areas of general state concern, even though the enactment may touch upon the property, affairs, or government of municipalities and circumscribe local authority in that area.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and declared that N.Y. Laws ch. 641 did not violate the Home Rule provision of state constitution.



HEADNOTES

Civil Service - Public Employees' Fair Employment Act - Impasse Jurisdiction of Public Employment Relations Board Regarding Police and Firefighters' Unions - No Home Rule Violation

1. Chapter 641 of the Laws of 1998, which, in order to overcome the Home Rule constitutional defects in a prior statute (L 1996, ch 13) that transferred impasse jurisdiction of collective bargaining disputes between the City of New York and its police force from the City's Board of Collective Bargaining to the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB), extends PERB's impasse jurisdiction to all organized fire and police departments within the State (Civil Service Law § 212 [3]), is not violative of the Home Rule provisions of the State Constitution. Unlike the prior "special" law, chapter 641 is a "general" law (NY Const, art IX, § 3 [d] [1]) designed to make PERB's collective bargaining dispute procedures available to all police and firefighters' unions across the State. Moreover, the fact that chapter 641 contains a grandfather clause permitting existing local boards to retain impasse jurisdiction at the option of the collective bargaining agent does not constitute a Home Rule violation. By its terms and its effect, chapter 641 treats all local governments alike in meeting the Legislature's purpose of prohibiting local governments from denying access to PERB's impasse resolution procedures to police and firefighters' unions.

Civil Service - Public Employees' Fair Employment Act - Impasse Jurisdiction of Public Employment Relations Board Regarding Police and Firefighters' Union - Jurisdiction of Scope of Bargaining and Impasse Resolution Issues

2. Chapter 641 of the Laws of 1998, which extends the impasse jurisdiction of the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB) to all organized fire and police departments within the State (Civil Service Law § 212 [3]), must be construed to place jurisdiction of scope of bargaining issues, including when the scope of bargaining dispute is pursued through the vehicle of an improper practice charge, with PERB and not with a local body such as New York City's Board of Collective Bargaining (BCB). In order to determine if an impasse exists with respect to the conditions of employment, PERB must have the power to determine what the "conditions of employment" are in order to meet its statutory mandate with regard to impasse resolution. Consequently, under the statutory scheme, PERB has jurisdiction of scope of bargaining and impasse resolution issues arising out of collective negotiations between the City of New York and its police union, with the BCB retaining jurisdiction of improper practice complaints not related to scope of bargaining or impasse resolution. Cumbersome and time-consuming processes that would have two separate agencies on two different levels of government attempting to separately resolve the intertwined issues of scope of bargaining and impasse resolution would violate the legislative policy underlying the Public Employees' Fair Employment Act.

1755 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Davis


Docket No. V-1105-2000


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, OTSEGO COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 81; 725 N.Y.S.2d 812; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 140



March 19, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff petitioned the court for visitation with her granddaughter. During the last visit between plaintiff and her granddaughter, the child, who was lactose intolerant, broke out in hives. Subsequently defendant mother refused visitation for plaintiff except in defendant's home. As a result, plaintiff filed a petition for custody of the child.


OVERVIEW: The court held that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, which provided that grandparents had standing to petition for visitation where either parent was deceased or where circumstances showed that conditions existed which equity would see fit to intervene, did not violate the Troxel doctrine. The Troxel doctrine held that a similar statute unconstitutionally violated the substantive due process rights of parents to the care, custody, and control of their children. The court held that the Troxel doctrine explicitly contemplated that a state could permit grandparent visitation in extraordinary circumstances, and that extraordinary circumstances existed in this case. Testimony indicated that plaintiff had been a loving and devoted grandmother. The court found no evidence that plaintiff had fed the child ice cream, which defendant claimed caused the attack of hives. While defendant was not unfit, there was ample evidence that she found parenting difficult. The Department of Social Services had opened a preventive case against defendant, and had investigated several abuse or neglect charges against defendant.


OUTCOME: Plaintiff was awarded visitation with the child, with conditions.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 72 

1. Domestic Relations Law § 72, which allows grandparents to apply to court for visitation where either or both of the grandchild's parents are dead or in any circumstances that warrant the equitable intervention of the court, is constitutional. A statute should be construed in a manner which would remove doubt of its constitutionality. Here, courts can remove doubt as to the constitutionality of section 72 by requiring that special weight be accorded the preference of parents. This preference must be respected absent extraordinary circumstances.

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents

2. Petitioner paternal grandmother is granted visitation rights with her two-year-old granddaughter, notwithstanding objections by the biological mother. Domestic Relations Law § 72 grants grandparents standing to petition for visitation where "circumstances show that conditions exist which equity would see fit to intervene." If the grandparent makes that hurdle, the court, in resolving the visitation dispute, "may make such directions as the best interest of the child may require" (id.). The biological father is incarcerated and the grandmother and her family have a strong and long-standing relationship with the child from birth. Thus, a significantly reduced role for the grandmother in the child's life would be seriously detrimental to the child. In addition, any visitation concerns of the mother, who finds parenting difficult, appear pretextual and are easily addressed by an appropriate order.

People v. Owens


DA-3 & DEF-59, Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with Ind. # 414/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 494; 725 N.Y.S.2d 810; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 64



March 16, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] People's motion entitled DA-3 and defendant's cross-motion denied. Defendant's motion entitled DEF-59 granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a death penalty case, the People sought an order requiring defendant before trial to file a notice of intention to offer psychiatric evidence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §400.27(13) (2001). Defendant opposed the motion and cross-moved for an order declaring that statute unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant acknowledged that he did not intend to offer psychiatric evidence during the guilt phase of trial, and had not filed a notice of intent to proffer psychiatric evidence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §400.27(13). The court entered an order requiring defendant to file the notice after the scheduled deadline to empanel a jury, but before the commencement of opening statements. Section 400.27(13) did not mandate notice before resumption of individual voir dire. Defendant merely had to file the notice before the presentation of evidence at the guilt phase.


OUTCOME: People's motion was denied. Defendant was required to serve and file the notice after empanelling of the jury, but before the parties' opening statements.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Sentence - Notice of Intent to Offer Psychiatric Evidence at Capital Murder Trial

Defendant's notice of intention to offer psychiatric evidence pursuant to CPL 400.27 (13) at the penalty phase of his capital murder trial must be filed and served before the presentation of evidence at the guilt phase. It may be served after the empanelment of the jury, but before the parties' opening statements. Such requirement sufficiently complies with the mandate of CPL 400.27 (13) without unfairly prejudicing the defense.

PEOPLE v. DEF-25, JOHN F. OWENS

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 460; 2001 NY Slip Op 40116U

1758 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Owens


Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with Ind. # 414/99, DEF-27


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 380; 723 N.Y.S.2d 621; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 52



March 6, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion challenging the constitutionality of the guilty plea provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 220 and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§250.40, 400.27, as they had been interpreted by prior New York case law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first degree murder, second degree murder, and first degree rape. Plaintiff filed a notice of intent to seek the death penalty, notice, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §250.40. Defendant had not sought to enter a guilty plea, either before or after the notice was filed. Nonetheless, defendant filed the motion, alleging that the guilty plea provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 220 and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§250.40, 400.27, as interpreted by the New York Court of Appeals, were unconstitutional. Defendant requested that the notice be stricken and that he be allowed to enter a guilty plea if he so chose, or that he be allowed to offer argument at sentencing that the law prohibited him from entering a guilty plea. The court of appeals had held, in two earlier decisions, that art. 220 prohibited a guilty plea while a notice was pending, and that plaintiff's statutory authority to file a notice within 120 days of arraignment overrode a defendant's unconditional right to plead guilty to an indictment. The trial court denied defendant's constitutional challenges, stating that it was bound by the earlier decisions of the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied in its entirety, because prior New York case law had excised the unconstitutional guilty plea provisions of the New York death penalty statute, and the resulting death penalty statute, like all statutes, carried a strong presumption of constitutionality.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Constitutionality of Plea Bargain Provisions of Death Penalty Statute - Ripeness of Issue

In a capital murder prosecution where defendant has not yet made a request to enter a guilty plea, defendant's motion challenging the constitutionality of the guilty plea provisions of the death penalty statute is denied, as the issue is not ripe for adjudication. Even if he now sought to enter a guilty plea, he has not proven beyond a reasonable doubt that the current death penalty scheme is unconstitutional.

 

1759 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fatal


INDICTMENT NO. 9261/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 334; 723 N.Y.S.2d 609; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 41



February 21, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss Count 5 of the indictment as it applies to Darryl Williams and Sean Fatal denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss count five of an indictment which charged them both with gang assault in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §120.07.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were alleged to have gotten into a car after an argument with rival gang members, and individually and simultaneously fired shots from their car while being driven by a fourth. Defendants moved to dismiss one count of the indictment, arguing that the wording of the statute allowed charges to be brought without an ascertainment of whether a defendant was actually in a gang. The trial court found that the word "gang" as used in the gang assault statute, N.Y. Penal Law §120.07, had no associational implications but was limited to the requirement that a minimum of three persons be actually present and participating in the assault, and directed that the jury would be so instructed. Further, the court found that there was no doubt that defendants' alleged conduct met the elements of the crime of gang assault, specifically the requirement that at least three persons be "actually present" as participants in the crime as required by the statute. As such, defendants' motion was denied.


OUTCOME: Defendants' motion to dismiss was denied as it pertained to count five of the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Gang Assault - Facial Constitutionality

1. Penal Law § 120.07, gang assault in the first degree, is not facially unconstitutional on the ground that the wording and title of the statute allow the prosecution to denominate an individual charged under its provisions as being a gang member without proof or without a hearing to measure the probative value of such evidence against its potential for undue prejudice in violation of the due process rights of the accused. A person is guilty under the statute "when, with intent to cause serious physical injury to another person and when aided by two or more other persons actually present, he causes serious physical injury to such person or to a third person." The primary concern motivating the enactment of the statute is to impose a harsher penalty for an assault committed by multiple assailants. The word "gang" does not appear in the statutory description of the crime. There is no element requiring that any of the participants in the offense be affiliated with an organization or even with each other prior to the commission of the assault. The only requirement under Penal Law § 120.07, other than the intentional infliction of injury, is that there be at least three persons actually present to assist in the commission of the assault.

Crimes - Gang Assault - Unconstitutional Vagueness - Element of Aid by Two or More Persons Actually Present

2. Penal Law § 120.07, which provides that a person is guilty of gang assault in the first degree "when, with intent to cause serious physical injury to another person and when aided by two or more other persons actually present, he causes serious physical injury to such person or to a third person," is not unconstitutional as applied because the element requiring that two or more persons be "actually present" for the commission of the assault is unconstitutionally vague. The aggravating element, "aided by another person actually present," means that the participants in the assault must be physically present, willing and able to render immediate aid to one another, and must, by their presence, pose an additional risk of violence. There is no vagueness in this element of the crime of gang assault. Thus, in a prosecution of three defendants in which the evidence indicates that the defendants, accompanied by a fourth person, were together in an automobile and individually and simultaneously fired shots from the car window allegedly causing death to one victim and serious physical injury to another, defendants' alleged conduct meets the elements of the crime of gang assault, specifically the requirement that at least three persons be "actually present" as participants in the crime.

 

1760 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Singh


Docket # 20 00KN037586


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 465; 722 N.Y.S.2d 368; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 40



February 20, 2001, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with two counts of aggravated harassment in the second degree (N.Y. Penal Law §240.30(1), (2)) and one count of harassment in the second degree (N.Y. Penal Law §240.26(1)). Defendant moved to dismiss the purported information on grounds of facial insufficiency pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§100.45, 170.30, 170.35.


OVERVIEW: Defendant telephoned a government agency, spoke with an employee, and threatened to start killing judges and some lawyers so that people would start to listen to defendant. There was no allegation that the recipient of the phone call was personally threatened by defendant, that defendant knew the name of the person who answered the phone, or that defendant identified any particular individual by name. The court dismissed the charge of harassment in the second degree because § 240.26 required that the recipient of the threat and the target of the threat be one and the same individual. Defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of aggravated harassment under § 240.30(1)) was denied because there was no statutory requirement that the target of the threat be specified or identified. The defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of aggravated harassment under § 240.30(2) was denied because when non-hearsay allegations set forth that a caller has threatened criminal behavior, that communication was inherently illegitimate for the purposes of facial sufficiency.


OUTCOME: Harassment charge was dismissed because the recipient of the threat and the target of the threat were not the same person. Aggravated harassment charges were sustained because there was no statutory requirement that the target be specified, and non-hearsay allegations setting forth illegitimate communications were sufficient for the purposes of facial sufficiency.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Target of Threat Need Not be Specified

1. The target of a threat need not be specified or identified in the accusatory instrument in order to establish a prima facie case of aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]). The statute provides that a person is guilty of such crime "when, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another person, he or she ... [c]ommunicates, or causes a communication to be initiated by mechanical or electronic means or otherwise, with a person, anonymously, or otherwise ... in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm." The statute's use of the words "another person" in the opening paragraph and the use of the words "a person" referred to in subdivision (1) evinces a legislative intent that the subject of the communication need not be the same person to whom the communication is made. The statutory language does not set forth that the target of the threat must be identified or specified. Furthermore, there is nothing in the statute which indicates that the threatening communication must be directed at anyone in particular. Thus, an information that sets forth in sum and substance that defendant made a telephone call which was received by the complainant, and that during the conversation the defendant made threats targeted to third persons causing the complainant to be annoyed and alarmed, is facially sufficient with respect to a charge of aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]).

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Legitimate Communication

2. With respect to a charge of aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [2]), nonhearsay allegations that a defendant placed a phone call that threatened criminal behavior will suffice as prima facie evidence that defendant made a phone call with no purpose of legitimate communication. Accordingly, an information that sets forth that defendant placed a phone call to the Child Abuse Reports Registry during which he told the complainant that he "will start killing judges and some lawyers so that people will start to listen to the defendant, and that if [the complainant] doesn't take [the defendant's] report, that [the defendant] would start killing some people too, and that [the defendant] understands what the kids at Columbine High School were thinking before they did what they did," is facially sufficient with respect to a charge of aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [2]).

Index No. 1091TSN99, Motion Calendar Nos. 16 & 70
