People v. Bimonte


Docket No. 99Q057899


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 677; 726 N.Y.S.2d 830; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 67



February 2, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 7, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury of two counts of an attempt to commit the crime of possessing a sexual performance by a child, N.Y. Penal Law §110/263.16. Defendant moved for an order setting aside the verdict pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §330.30.


OVERVIEW: Defendant tried to have developed two photos of his daughters, age five and seven at the time, in which their genitals were fully exposed. In his motion to set aside the verdict, defendant claimed that N.Y. Penal Law §110/263.16 was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad as applied to his case. The court found that each situation must be analyzed on a case by case basis and used the six Dost factors to find that the jury had constitutionally applied § 263.16 to defendant. The court further found there was no impermissibly expansive construction to the proscription on lewd exhibition of the genitals in this case.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion for an order setting aside the jury's verdict was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Possessing Sexual Performance by Child - Constitutionality of Statute as Applied to Defendant - Taking Nude Photographs of Minor Daughters

 Penal Law § 263.16, which proscribes possessing a sexual performance by a child, is not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who was convicted after a jury trial of attempting to commit the crime because of nude photographs he took of his two minor daughters, ages five and seven, containing a "lewd exhibition of the genitals." There was no impermissibly "expansive construction" of the statutory proscription to cover an innocent family photo or artistic depiction of a nude child. Rather, the jury constitutionally applied the statute to defendant. In each photograph, the focal point is the genitals of defendant's daughters. The setting in both photographs is sexually suggestive, in places or poses generally associated with sexual activity that are foreign to most children in the course of normal, benign activity. Moreover, the conduct depicted in each photograph shows a sexual coyness, at a minimum, and even suggests a willingness to engage in sexual activity. Furthermore, it can be said that the conduct depicted in each photograph is intended or designed to elicit a sexual response in the viewer. Accordingly, defendant's motion to set aside the jury's verdict pursuant to CPL 330.30 is denied.

 

1763 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tiffany


DOCKET NUMBER 2000NY093939


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 917; 721 N.Y.S.2d 741; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 23



January 30, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss the count charging harassment in the second degree denied, motion to compel a bill of particulars and for discovery granted to the extent of the response to the motion and the Voluntary Disclosure Form furnished by the People, motion to suppress statement evidence or, in the alternative, for a hearing, denied as moot, defendant's motion to preclude the use at trial of any prior criminal history or prior uncharged criminal or immoral acts referred to the trial court for decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a criminal prosecution for harassment, defendant filed motions to dismiss, to compel a bill of particulars and discovery, to suppress statement evidence or, in the alternative, for a Huntley/Dunaway hearing, and to preclude the use of prior criminal history/bad acts at trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with aggravated harassment in the second degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30(1), and harassment in the second degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.26(3). Defendant moved, inter alia, to dismiss both counts as facially insufficient. Specifically, defendant sought dismissal of the count charging aggravated harassment on the ground that the statements alleged in the complaint did not constitute genuine threats. In addition, defendant sought dismissal of the count charging harassment on the ground that the complaint lacked factual allegations demonstrating a specific intent to harass. The court denied the motion to dismiss the count charging aggravated harassment because the court found the factual allegations provided reasonable cause to infer defendant acted with intent to harass when he communicated threats of physical violence by telephone. Additionally, the court denied the motion to dismiss the count charging harassment because there were specific threats to kill the complainant and put a bullet through her head. Thus, the court concluded it could infer from the threats that defendant intended to harass, annoy, or alarm the complainant.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions to dismiss the counts charging harassment, and aggravated harassment, in the second degree. The court granted the motion to compel a bill of particulars and for discovery. The court denied as moot the motion to suppress. Finally, the court referred for decision on the motion to preclude the use at trial of any prior criminal history or prior uncharged criminal or immoral acts.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Threatening Telephone Calls - Intent to Harass

1. In a prosecution for aggravated harassment in the second degree, the factual allegations provide reasonable cause to infer that defendant acted with intent to harass, annoy or alarm the complainant when he communicated by telephone threats of physical violence which were likely to cause annoyance or alarm. The complaint alleges that more than one threatening phone call was made each day for a period of over two months. The sheer number of calls standing alone provides a reasonable basis to infer that defendant intended to communicate with the complainant in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm. Moreover, the content of those communications includes unequivocal and specific threats of harm, which further evidence an intent to cause annoyance or alarm and which, despite the absence of a specific time and place for fulfillment of the threats, were statements likely to cause annoyance or alarm.

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Threatening Telephone Calls - Constitutionality

2. Penal Law § 240.30 (1), aggravated harassment in the second degree, is not constitutionally overbroad. While crude and vulgar outbursts which are not intended to, and do not, create a public disturbance may be protected from criminal prosecution as free speech, threats of physical violence, such as those communicated by defendant over the telephone, which are likely to cause alarm or annoyance to the recipient, enjoy no such constitutional protection. Constitutional free speech protections have never been thought to give absolute protection to every individual to speak whenever he pleases, or to use any form of address in any circumstances that he chooses; a person's right to free expression may be curtailed upon a showing that substantial privacy interests are being invaded in an essentially intolerable manner.

Crimes - Harassment - Threatening Telephone Calls - Communication Lacking Any Legitimate Purpose

3. Defendant's motion to dismiss a charge of harassment in the second degree is denied where the charge arose out of threatening telephone calls defendant made to the complainant concerning custody and/or visitation with his child. The intent to harass, annoy or alarm may be inferred from the surrounding circumstances, and defendant made specific, unconditional threats to kill the complainant and to put a bullet through her head. Thus, the court may infer from the threats alleged in the complaint that the defendant intended to harass, annoy or alarm the complainant. While a telephone call to one's spouse to discuss the issue of child custody may well have a legitimate purpose, any attempt to resolve that issue by the making of threats of violence is not insulated from criminal sanction.

 

1764 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aboaf


Docket No. 2000NY043646, 2000NY043696, 2000NY043571, 2000NY043658, 2000NY043648, 2000NY043649, 2000NY043659, 2000NY043606, 2000NY043647, 2000NY043671, 2000NY043516, 2000NY043645, 2000NY043702, 2000NY043672


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 173; 721 N.Y.S.2d 725; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 20



January 23, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 9, 2001.
Subsequent appeal at, Sub nomine at People v. Bull, 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1280 (N.Y. App. Term, Aug. 11, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss the informations against them based upon N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(4) on the grounds that they violated their right to free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(4) which prohibited being masked or disguised in a public place while congregating with others who were also masked or disguised. Defendants moved to dismiss on the ground that, on its face and as applied to them, § 240.35(4) violated their rights under U. S. Const. amend. I. The court held there was no basis to conclude that if defendants uncovered their faces they would be subjected to harassment. The court held the statute was not unconstitutionally vague on its face in regard to its references to masquerade party or like entertainment because ordinary people did not have to guess at its meaning. The court held the statute was not overbroad because it could prohibit masks which had expressive content because defendants had not contended they wore their masks as symbolic expressions. Moreover, the statute was capable of a reasonable limiting construction.


OUTCOME: Defendants' motions for dismissal were denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Loitering - Loitering while Masked or Disguised - Constitutionality of Penal Law § 240.35 (4) 

1. Penal Law § 240.35 (4), which prohibits being masked or disguised in a public place while congregating with others who are also masked or disguised, does not unconstitutionally impede defendants' right to free association guaranteed by the First Amendment. The key to a successful First Amendment challenge to a disclosure requirement is undisputed evidence that establishes, as a matter of law, the requisite nexus between compelled disclosure of the identities of individuals and resulting recriminations from either Government officials or private parties. Defendants, who describe themselves as anarchists and were arrested while participating in a May Day demonstration wearing bandanas covering their faces and shouting slogans like "take back the streets" and "police state," have offered no evidence of a pattern of harassment against them. The general knowledge that throughout history political dissidents often paid a heavy price for their unpopular beliefs is not a substitute for the necessary evidentiary showing. Accordingly, there is no basis to conclude that if defendants are forced to uncover their faces they will be subject to harassment that will abridge their First Amendment right to associate for the promotion of their beliefs and ideas.

Crimes - Loitering - Loitering while Masked or Disguised - Constitutionality of Penal Law § 240.35 (4) 

2. Penal Law § 240.35 (4), which prohibits being masked or disguised in a public place while congregating with others who are also masked or disguised, is not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. Although the statute contains an exemption for individuals attending a "masquerade party or like entertainment" if it is held in a city that has applicable regulations and a permit is obtained, it does not allow city officials unbridled discretion to decide whether an event is a masquerade party or like entertainment, and, therefore, lawful. While the statute does not define "masquerade party or like entertainment," the applicable test is whether the provision is so vague that people of common intelligence must necessarily guess at its meaning. "Masquerade" is defined as a social gathering of persons wearing masks and often fanciful costumes, and "like entertainment" refers to social gatherings, dances and performances that involve masks or costumes. These categories are specific and not drawn along content-related lines, thereby preventing an administrator from exercising unbridled discretion.

Crimes - Loitering - Loitering while Masked or Disguised - Constitutionality of Penal Law § 240.35 (4) 

3. Penal Law § 240.35 (4), which prohibits being masked or disguised in a public place while congregating with others who are also masked or disguised, is not unconstitutionally overbroad despite the fact that it prohibits masks which have expressive content, such as a gas mask worn to protest environmental degradation, as well as masks which provide anonymity as a corollary to the right to freedom of association. Prohibiting the gathering of masked people in public is a proper exercise of the State's police power to prevent and detect crime. An antimask law furthers the State's legitimate interest in protecting its citizens from intimidation, violence, and actual and implied threats, and these State interests are implicated even when masks are worn for anonymity or as symbolic expression, since any mask conceals the face of the wearer. Although it is possible that the statute could be impermissibly applied to the wearing of masks for symbolic expression in nonexempt activities, these offensive applications are not so numerous as to produce a chilling effect on protected activity. Moreover, the statute is capable of a reasonable limiting construction. The original antimask law was enacted to prohibit wearing masks in order to prevent identification during lawless activity, and construing the law so as to prohibit the wearing of masks for no legitimate purpose is consistent with this purpose.

 

1765 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


National Union Fire Ins. Co. of Pittsburgh, Pa. v. Erazo


Index Number 1488 TSN 99


CIVIL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 194; 721 N.Y.S.2d 720; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 30



January 19, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss based on the statute of limitations held in abeyance pending the submission of further papers and/or documents consistent with this decision; motion restored to the Special Term Part I calendar for February 7, 2000 at 9:30 a.m. for the submission of said papers and/or documents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurance company sought restitution from defendant embezzler under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 213-b for payments it made to an insured pursuant to a policy covering losses arising out of employee infidelity. Plaintiff alleged that it took a full assignment of all the rights of action held by the insured including those under defendant's restitution agreement. Defendant moved to dismiss claiming the action was time-barred.


OVERVIEW: Defendant embezzled $ 100,000 from his former employer, which was insured by plaintiff for such losses. Defendant pled guilty to grand larceny and was sentenced to five years probation. As a condition of probation, defendant was ordered to pay restitution to plaintiff's insured in the amount of $ 100,000. Defendant paid $ 50,000 immediately upon sentencing. Plaintiff sought payment for the remaining amount less the insured's deductible. The court held that plaintiff qualified as a "crime victim" within the meaning of § 213-b because the statute was intended to be broadly construed and a similar restitution provision in the New York Penal Law had been interpreted to include insurance companies in the definition of "crime victims." Therefore, plaintiff could rely on the seven year statute of limitations of § 213-b, rather than the three year statute of limitations ordinarily applicable to claims sounding in conversion. However, neither plaintiff nor defendant offered proof of when the crime actually occurred so the court was unable to determine the date plaintiff's cause of action accrued.


OUTCOME: Motion was held in abeyance pending submission of proof as to when the crime occurred. Plaintiff was entitled to the benefit of the seven year statute of limitations to collect restitution because it qualified as a "crime victim." The statute was intended to be interpreted broadly and insurers were "crime victims" under a similar statute.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions - What Statute Governs - Action by Victim of Criminal Offense - Insurance Company as Crime Victim

Plaintiff, an insurance company that paid a claim made by an insured for a loss the insured sustained when defendant stole money from it, is a "crime victim" within the meaning of CPLR 213-b. Accordingly, in its action to recover from defendant the money it paid out to its insured, plaintiff is entitled to rely on the seven-year Statute of Limitations of CPLR 213-b, rather than the three-year Statute of Limitations normally applicable to claims sounding in conversion. Although the statute does not define the term "crime victim," its legislative history indicates a strong intent that the statute be broadly construed to compensate victims of crime. Moreover, the provisions of the Penal Law that provide for restitution to crime victims have consistently been applied to insurance companies.

 

1766 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


N.Y. County Lawyers' Ass'n v. Pataki


Index No. 102987/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


188 Misc. 2d 776; 727 N.Y.S.2d 851; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 205



January 16, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 3, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Motions denied in part and granted in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, State and governor, filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. §§ 3211(a)(3), (7), in an action brought by plaintiff lawyers' association seeking declaratory and injunctive relief, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. §§ 3001, 6301, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: The association commenced an action against defendants challenging the compensation levels and limits for assigned private counsel. Defendants moved to dismiss the complaint, contending that: (1) the association lacked standing to raise the claims asserted; (2) the complaint failed to state a justiciable case against the governor; and (3) there was no cognizable claim of tortious interference with contract. The court determined the association had direct standing as it has suffered an injury in fact different from the public at large. In addition, the court found the association had organizational standing to represent the rights of its members and third-party standing to represent the rights of its members' clients and potential clients. However, the court found the allegations in the complaint did not draw a sufficient connection between the governor and the alleged unconstitutional conduct of the State to conclude the governor was a real party in interest. Finally, the court concluded the association's assumed duties did not create a contract within the meaning of tortious interference, and in the absence of a contractual relationship, there could be no breach.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the first eight causes of action; however, the court dismissed the ninth cause of action for tortious interference with contract. Also, the court granted defendants' motion to strike the governor as a named defendant.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Third-Party Standing - Lawyers' Association Challenge to Monetary Cap Provision for Assigned Private Counsel

1. Plaintiff New York County Lawyers' Association (NYCLA) has third-party standing to challenge the monetary cap provisions of County Law § 722-b and Judiciary Law § 35 (3) for assigned private counsel. To maintain third-party standing a litigant must have direct standing and then the court must consider the relationship of the litigant to the person whose right it seeks to assert, the ability of the rightholder to vindicate his or her rights and the risk that the third-party rights will be adversely affected absent the litigation. NYCLA satisfies the test for direct standing as it has suffered an injury in fact within the zone of interests protected by the statute, different from the public at large. Third-party standing is also satisfied. The third-party indigent clients' rights to assigned counsel are inextricably intertwined with NYCLA activities. Thus, plaintiff is an effective proponent of the clients' rights. Moreover, the third parties would not be able to assert their own rights because of the magnitude of the litigation and the lack of resources available to them. Furthermore, the substantial empirical evidence provides strong indication that the third parties' rights and interests will be protected by this litigation.

Parties - Standing - Organizational Standing - Lawyers' Association Challenge to Monetary Cap Provision for Assigned Private Counsel

2. Plaintiff New York County Lawyers' Association (NYCLA) has organizational standing to challenge the monetary cap provisions of County Law § 722-b and Judiciary Law § 35 (3) for assigned private counsel. To maintain organizational standing the organization must have direct standing, some or all of its members must have direct standing to sue, the interests it asserts must be germane to its purposes, and neither the asserted claim nor the appropriate relief may require participation of the individual members. NYCLA has direct standing as it has suffered an injury in fact within the zone of interests protected by the statute, different from the public at large. NYCLA's members fall within the zone of interests of the statutes and related court rules, and thus have direct standing to sue. In addition, the NYCLA members' injury is different from the public at large because they are an advocate class enlisted to defend the indigents. Moreover, the interests NYCLA seeks to protect are germane to its organizational purpose and the panel members' participation is not necessary.

Parties - Proper Parties - Governor - Lawyers' Association Challenge to Monetary Cap Provision for Assigned Private Counsel

3. The Governor is not a proper party to a lawsuit commenced by the New York County Lawyers' Association challenging the monetary cap provisions of County Law § 722-b and Judiciary Law § 35 (3) for assigned private counsel. Plaintiff's complaint failed to draw a sufficient nexus between the Governor and the statute alleged to be unconstitutional to conclude that the Governor, independent of the State, is a real party in interest.

Attorney and Client - Compensation - Lawyers' Association Challenge to Monetary Cap Provision for Assigned Private Counsel

4. The State's motion to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action claims asserted by the New York County Lawyers' Association (NYCLA) challenging the monetary cap provisions of County Law § 722-b and Judiciary Law § 35 (3) for assigned private counsel is denied. NYCLA claims that failure to provide sufficient compensation to private counsel has resulted in deficiencies in the assigned counsel system and a risk that indigents will be denied their rights to effective assistance of counsel and due process of law. In support of these claims plaintiff has submitted empirical evidence detailing the current crisis in the assigned counsel program, official statistics, reports from independent groups, and other material which outlines the shortage of active panel attorneys and how they are overburdened. The submissions also show the adverse effects on juvenile delinquency cases, abuse and neglect proceedings, appellate backlogs, arraignment overload, the problem of uncertified panel counsel, prolonged delays, and how it relates to the current 18-B compensation rates.

Torts - Interference with Contractual Relations - Lawyers' Association Challenge to Monetary Cap Provision for Assigned Private Counsel

5. A tortious interference with contract claim against the State by the New York County Lawyers' Association (NYCLA) in its challenge of the monetary cap provisions of County Law § 722-b and Judiciary Law § 35 (3) for assigned private counsel is dismissed. A prima facie case for tortious interference requires the existence of a contract, defendant's awareness of the contract, defendant's intentional inducement of the contract's breach, actual breach, and damages. There is no contractual relationship between the City of New York and NYCLA, of which the State would be considered an interfering third party, as the relationship is not one rooted in a pecuniary interest. A binding contract requires mutuality of consideration. There was no bargained-for exchange between the parties here. NYCLA's assumed duties, codified in the court rules, do not create a contract within the meaning of tortious interference, and in the absence of a contractual relationship there can be no breach.

 

1767 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ocasio


21-062


CITY COURT OF NEW YORK, ROCHESTER


186 Misc. 2d 822; 720 N.Y.S.2d 908; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 12



January 11, 2001, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accusatory instrument charging loitering (Penal Law § 240.35 [2]) and loitering in the first degree (Penal Law § 240.36) dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with loitering under N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2) and loitering in the first degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 240.36. Defendant filed a motion seeking various forms of relief, including dismissal of the charges on grounds that the accusatory instrument was insufficient to support the charges.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conduct occurred on private property, specifically on the driveway in the rear yard of a private residence. This area clearly was not a place to which the public or a substantial group of persons had access. Further it was not any of the specific places enumerated in § 240.35(2). Moreover, to charge defendant with loitering did not further the general rationale of the loitering statutes which was to prevent minor offenses in places frequented by the public for the protection of the public. The conduct did not occur in a public place. Therefore, the charge of loitering in violation of § 240.35(2) had to be dismissed because the statute did not apply to defendant's alleged conduct. The allegations of the accusatory instrument were insufficient to show that defendant was loitering for the purpose of using or possessing drugs. The fact that four baggies were found on the ground where five men were standing did not establish defendant's purpose in being on that property was to use or possess drugs. In addition, a driveway in the back yard of a private residence was not "in any place" within the meaning of § 240.36.


OUTCOME: The accusatory instrument charging loitering and loitering in the first degree was dismissed. The driveway in the backyard of a residence was not a "public place" or "any place" within the meaning of the loitering statutes in issue.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Loitering - Public Place - Driveway in Rear Yard of Private Residence is Not Public Place

1. In a prosecution wherein it is alleged that defendant was gambling with dice on a driveway in the rear yard of a private residence, the charge of loitering in a public place for the purposes of gambling in violation of Penal Law § 240.35 (2) is dismissed on the ground that the driveway is not a public place within the meaning of the statute. The driveway is not a place to which the public or a substantial group of persons has access, and it is not any of the specific places enumerated in Penal Law § 240.00 (1), which defines the term "public place" for purposes of Penal Law article 240. Moreover, to charge defendant with loitering for his alleged conduct under these circumstances does not further the general rationale of the loitering statutes, which is to prevent minor offenses in places frequented by the public for the protection of the public.

Crimes - Loitering - Loitering in Public Place for Purposes of Gambling - Unconstitutionality of Statute 

2. Penal Law § 240.35 (2), which provides that a person is guilty of loitering when he or she loiters or remains in a public place for the purpose of gambling with cards, dice or other gambling paraphernalia, is unconstitutional. Gambling is illegal only if a person advances or profits from the gambling activity when acting other than as a player. Therefore, a person who gambles with dice as a casual player does not commit a crime, and a person who loiters for that purpose cannot engage in criminal behavior.

Crimes - Loitering - Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument

3. In a loitering prosecution wherein it is alleged that defendant and four other men were gambling with dice on the driveway in the rear yard of a private residence and four baggies containing cocaine were found on the ground in the area where the men were standing, the charge of first degree loitering in violation of Penal Law § 240.36 is dismissed on the ground that the allegations of the accusatory instrument are insufficient to show that defendant was loitering for the purposes of using or possessing a controlled substance. The mere presence of the baggies in the area cannot create a presumption that illicit drug use or possession was the reason why defendant was present. The accusatory instrument contains no allegation of use or possession of illicit drugs by defendant or any of the codefendants; the only conduct observed was gambling with dice.

Crimes - Loitering - "Any Place" - Driveway in Rear Yard of Private Residence is Not Any Place

4. In a prosecution wherein it is alleged that four baggies containing cocaine were found on the ground in the immediate vicinity of defendant and four other men as they stood in the driveway in the rear yard of a private residence, the charge of first degree loitering in violation of Penal Law § 240.36, which prohibits loitering or remaining in "any place" for the purposes of unlawfully using or possessing a controlled substance, is dismissed on the ground that the driveway is not "any place" within the meaning of the statute. The actions forming the basis of the charge took place on private property, albeit not inside the residence. To accept an expansive view of the term "any place" and apply the statute to private property does not serve either of the fundamental purposes of loitering statutes, which are to insulate members of the public from injury to their person or property and materially assist the police in maintaining the peace. It would, however, invite questionable intrusions into personal privacy and make the law an instrument of harassment.

 

1768 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Campaign for Fiscal Equity v. State


Index No.: 111070/93


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 1; 719 N.Y.S.2d 475; 2001 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1



January 9, 2001, Decided


NOTICE:

[EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 2 OF 3. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 2, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Settle Judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs filed suit alleging the State's funding mechanisms (1) caused New York City public school students to receive less than the sound basic education required by the New York State Constitution Education Article, N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1, and (2) had an adverse and disparate impact upon minority public school students in violation of implementing regulations promulgated under 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000d of Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, students, parents, and organizations concerned with education issues, challenged New York State's funding of New York City's public schools. Plaintiffs alleged (1) the State had failed to assure New York City's public schools received adequate funding to afford their students the sound basic education guaranteed by the Education Article of the New York State Constitution, N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1, and (2) the State's funding mechanisms had an adverse and disparate impact on the city's minority public school students in violation of specific implementing regulations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000d of Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964. The court found the education provided New York City students was so deficient that it fell below the constitutional floor set by the Education Article of the New York State Constitution and the State's actions were a substantial cause of this constitutional violation. With respect to the claim under Title VI's implementing regulations, the court found that the state school funding system had an adverse and disparate impact on minority public school children and this disparate impact was not adequately justified by any educational reason.


OUTCOME: It was determined that the New York Legislature had to take steps to reform the state educational system. Ordered that the State put in place reforms of school financing and governance designed to redress constitutional and regulatory violations. The court retained jurisdiction for as long as necessary to ensure that the constitutional and statutory/regulatory violations were corrected.


HEADNOTES

Schools - State Aid to School Districts - Constitutionality of State's Public School Financing System

1. New York State's public school financing system violates the Education Article of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art XI, § 1), since New York City public school students are not provided the opportunity to receive a sound basic education, and there is a causal link between this failure and the State's system for funding public schools. A sound basic education consists of the foundational skills that students need to become productive citizens capable of civil engagement and sustaining competitive employment. A sound basic education is gauged by the resources afforded students (inputs) and by students' performance (outputs). Regarding inputs, defendants have failed to provide minimally adequate teaching of reasonably up-to-date basic curricula by sufficient personnel adequately trained to teach those subjects. Also, defendants have failed to provide minimally adequate physical facilities and classrooms. Furthermore, defendants have failed to provide adequate instrumentalities of learning. Regarding outputs, the City public school students' graduation/dropout rates and performance on standardized tests demonstrate that they are not receiving a minimally adequate education. Increased funding can provide the City with better teachers, better school buildings and better instrumentalities of learning. Thus, there is a causal link between the current funding system and the students' poor performance.

Schools - State Aid to School Districts - Discrimination Cause of Action under Title VI of Civil Rights Act

2. New York State's public school financing system has an adverse and disparate impact on minority New York City public school children which is not adequately justified by any reason related to education, and thus violates title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 (42 USC § 2000d). 42 USC § 2000d-1 authorizes Federal agencies to promulgate rules and regulations to effectuate its provisions. Plaintiffs' claim arises from 34 CFR 100.3 (b) (2), which provides that recipients of Federal funding may not utilize criteria or methods of administration which have the effect of subjecting individuals to discrimination, or the effect of defeating or substantially impairing accomplishment of the objectives of the program. To prevail on this claim, plaintiffs must prove that a facially neutral practice has had an adverse and disparate impact upon a protected class of people. If plaintiffs meet that burden, then defendants must demonstrate that the challenged practice is justified by educational necessity. From 1994-1995 to 1999-2000, New York City has consistently received less total State aid than its percentage share of enrolled students. Seventy-three percent of the State's minority public school students are enrolled in the City's public schools; minority students make up approximately 84% of the City's public school enrollment; and the City receives less funding per capita, on average, than districts in the rest of the State. Money is a crucial determinant of educational quality, and receipt of less educational funding by minority students is an adverse disparate impact within the purview of the regulations. Thus, plaintiffs have proved that the State's school funding mechanism caused the alleged disparate impact. Defendants' justifications for the adverse disparate racial impact caused by the distribution of State aid are unpersuasive.

PEOPLE v. OWENS
