1770 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Livio


Index No. 17099/98 & 17100/98


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, NASSAU COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 302; 725 N.Y.S.2d 785; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 580



December 22, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 1, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a telemarketer, was accused of two counts of aggravated harassment in the second degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §240.30(1). Defendant sought an order dismissing the accusatory instrument against him as being insufficient on its face, on grounds that the allegations of offenses charged did not constitute a violation of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant made two separate telemarketing calls to each complainant, and when his services were declined, he called each one a "dumb nigger" and hung up. Defendant claimed his comments constituted protected speech under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court agreed. The expression of controversial and unpopular views was precisely what the state and federal constitutions protected. The mere fact that defendant, in anger and frustration, used colorful language in registering displeasure with complainants' refusal to purchase his services did not render the communication criminal within the ambit of the N.Y. Penal Law §240.30(1). Defendant's statement, under the circumstances alleged, did not constitute fighting words or obscenity, and therefore was protected by the principle of freedom of speech guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the accusatory instruments. Under the circumstances alleged, defendant's words were not used with the intent to provoke a reasonable person into an immediate and violent breach of the peace, as required for defendant to be convicted under the aggravated harassment statute. His speech was therefore protected by the federal and state constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Protected Speech

In a prosecution for aggravated harassment in violation of Penal Law § 240.30 (1) where it is alleged that defendant telemarketer called complainants one time each for the purpose of selling home improvement items to them, and, when such services were declined, called both a "dumb nigger," defendant's motion to dismiss the information for insufficiency is granted. The words "dumb nigger," though reprehensible, improper and uncalled for, do not rise to the level of aggravated harassment as defined by Penal Law § 240.30 (1). They are not legally obscene or "fighting words," and constitute protected speech under the Federal and State Constitutions. Moreover, criminal liability may be imposed for making a single telephone call only if the call is placed with the requisite intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm, and there is a lack of legitimate purpose for the call in the first instance. Here, the calls were not initiated for the purpose of harassment, annoyance, threats or alarm, but rather for the legitimate purpose of soliciting business.

 

1771 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Webb


Indictment No.: 5157/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 835; 721 N.Y.S.2d 475; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 566



December 21, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to strike the death penalty notice filed against him, challenging the constitutionality of the N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.20(1)(f) standard for death-qualifying jurors. In the alternative, he sought orders limiting death-qualifying voir dire.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, charged with a capital crime in which the State had filed a death penalty notice, challenged New York's procedure, set forth at N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.20(1)(f), for death-qualifying jurors as violating his rights to an impartial jury, to due process, and to a jury composed of a cross-section of the community. He argued that the provision permitting a challenge for cause only where a juror's views would actually preclude him from rendering an impartial verdict or properly exercising discretion in determining a sentence could only be applied constitutionally to the prosecution. The court declined to modify previous rulings that the statute was constitutional. People with reservations about the death penalty did not constitute a constitutionally cognizable group, because they did not necessarily have much in common. New York's scheme was crafted to meet federal constitutional requirements, and therefore properly limited juries in the exercise of their discretion.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the death penalty notice, because it found nothing in defendant's arguments to persuade it to abandon previous determinations that the statute was constitutional. The court did issue an order permitting defendant to further question jurors who expressed reservations about death penalty and attempt to rehabilitate them.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Jurors - Selection of Jury - Capital Murder - Jurors with Religious or Conscientious Qualms about Capital Punishment Not Cognizable Group

1. Jurors with religious or conscientious qualms about capital punishment do not constitute a cognizable and distinct group warranting protection from discrimination. A cognizable group is one that is a recognizable, distinct class, singled out for different treatment under the laws, as written or applied. The group must be defined by a limiting factor, by a common thread of attitude or experience, and by a community of interests that cannot be represented if the group is excluded from the jury selection process. There are no such characteristics among individuals expressing qualms about capital punishment. A shared attitude of conscientious objection to the death penalty is insufficient alone to create the kernel of a cognizable group. No common limiting factor has been shown to unify such individuals into a group, nor has any societal discrimination against such a group been demonstrated. Moreover, their exclusion from jury service in a capital case is not arbitrary, but is designed to further the State's interest in obtaining a single, impartial jury.

Crimes - Jurors - Selection of Jury - Capital Murder - Single Standard of Review When Deciding Challenges for Cause

2. There is a single standard of review for both the People's and defendant's challenges for cause pursuant to CPL 270.20 (1) (f) when selecting a jury in a capital murder case.

 

1772 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Westbury Wholesale Produce Co. v. Maine Maid Inn, L.L.C.


INDEX NOS. NCC841/00, NCC842/00, NCC843/00, NCC844/00


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, FIRST DISTRICT, NASSAU COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 911; 721 N.Y.S.2d 714; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 578



December 18, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion denied in its entirety, except to the extent that a joint trial of these actions directed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action to recover costs under a delivery contract, defendant moved to consolidate four separate Commercial Claims actions, and then dismiss them as in excess of the Commercial Claims jurisdictional ceiling.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a restaurant and catering facility. The defendant was alleged to have failed and refused to pay for 15 days worth of deliveries made by the plaintiff to the defendant's facility, with each delivery invoiced separately. The total amount due and owing is between $ 10,000-15,000. Plaintiff filed claims in the Commercial Claims Division of the District Court, assembling the invoices into four groups, the total of each was just under the statutory limit for Small and Commercial Claims. Defendant moved to consolidate the claims and dismiss for want of jurisdiction. Before the court, the issue was whether the monetary jurisdictional limit for Small and Commercial Claims was applicable to the total sought in a plaintiff's complaint, or whether it was applicable only to individual causes of action. The trial court found that under current caselaw decisions and statutory interpretation, the Commercial Claims jurisdiction of the District Court was determined whether or not the total sought, in the aggregate, exceeded the jurisdictional limit. As such, defendant's motions were denied.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion was denied in its entirety because current caselaw suggested that even in the aggregate, as long as an individual claim met the jurisdictional limits of the District Court, the court could hear any claim.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Jurisdiction - Monetary Limit of District Court - Separate Causes of Action Joined in Single Complaint

The $ 3,000 monetary jurisdictional limit for small and commercial claims brought in the Nassau County District Court (UDCA 1801, 1801-A) is applicable only to individual causes of action and should not be applied to the total sought in the complaint, even where the separate claims are consolidated for joint trial pursuant to CPLR 602. UDCA 211 allows the court to exercise jurisdiction where a single complaint contains separately stated and distinct causes of action and the aggregate demand exceeds the court's monetary jurisdiction so long as the demand of each individual separately stated cause of action does not exceed the monetary jurisdictional limit (see also, CCA 211; UCCA 211; UJCA 211). Consequently, the District Court has jurisdiction over plaintiff's four separate claims to recover monetary damages for 15 days' worth of deliveries of fresh produce to defendant's restaurant and catering facility where the total amount due and owing is between $ 10,000 and $ 15,000 but where each separate claim is under the court's monetary limit for commercial claims.

 

1773 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Green v. Giuliani


Index NO. 401259/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 138; 721 N.Y.S.2d 461; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 561



November 21, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, public advocate of the City of New York, applied, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Charter § 1109, for a summary judicial inquiry into the actions taken and public statements made by respondent, New York City's mayor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, public advocate of the City of New York, applied, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Charter § 1109, for a summary judicial inquiry into the actions taken and public statements made by respondent, New York City's mayor, following certain statements made by respondent mayor concerning a decedent's juvenile and criminal record which had been sealed by the court. The decedent was shot and killed by a New York City police officer. Petitioner alleged that the public disclosure of the decedent's juvenile and criminal record was unauthorized. On that basis, petitioner sought an inquiry into how respondent obtained the information which he made public, whether the information was from sealed records, and whether its release was made without regard to the statutory protection of such records. Respondent contended that New York City, N.Y., Charter § 1109 improperly violated the separation of powers doctrine. The trial court found that the legislative intent clearly authorized such inquiries. As such, petitioner's application was granted.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's application was granted, and it was further ordered that respondent's challenge to the application was denied because most of the questions raised by petitioner public advocate remained unanswered.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Summary Inquiry Powers of Public Advocate

1. New York City Charter § 1109, which permits the Public Advocate of New York City to seek from the courts an order to conduct a summary inquiry into alleged violations of duty in relation to the property, government or affairs of New York City, does not improperly assign a public trust role to a Supreme Court Justice in violation of New York Constitution, article VI, § 20, and thus is facially constitutional. The function and purpose of a Supreme Court Justice in presiding over a summary inquiry resulting in a report by the Public Advocate is a judicial function comparable to that of a Justice presiding over a Grand Jury investigation and report pursuant to CPL 190.85. The function is also consistent with the variety of traditionally permissible judicial roles.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Summary Inquiry by Public Advocate

2. New York City Charter § 1109, which permits the Public Advocate of New York City to seek from the courts an order to conduct a summary inquiry into alleged violations of duty in relation to the property, government or affairs of New York City, is not unconstitutional as applied to the Public Advocate's inquiry into actions taken and public statements made by the Mayor which allegedly involved information obtained from court-sealed records of an individual shot by a New York City police officer. The summary inquiry is sought with respect to allegations of specific misconduct in relation to specific statutory provisions. Access to sealed records is carefully regulated by statute. The Public Advocate seeks to discover answers to specific questions regarding the sources of the statement and how the sources were obtained. These questions are focused on the facts underlying the Public Advocate's allegation that in publicizing the victim's criminal and juvenile records, the Mayor disregarded specific provisions and requirements of the CPL and Family Court Act governing access to, and dissemination of, the content of such records.

Public Officers - Public Advocate - Inquiry into Mayor's Activities in Obtaining Information from Sealed Records

3. The Public Advocate's request, pursuant to New York City Charter § 1109, for an inquiry into actions taken and public statements made by the Mayor, which allegedly involved information obtained from court-sealed records of an individual shot by a New York City police officer, is not beyond the scope of the statute. Although inquiries into financial corruption may have been a primary reason for enacting the summary inquiry provision, the language of the current section 1109 broadly applies to all forms of official misconduct and encompasses the unauthorized release of the contents of sealed court records. The sealing requirements which are the focus of the Public Advocate's petition are an important part of the juvenile and criminal justice systems. Regardless of whether the confidentiality of these records continues after the death of their subject, the unsealing of the records is not self-executing. The ability and willingness of the Mayor, in concert with executive agencies serving under his aegis, to bypass the sealing safeguards, to ignore the procedures for judicial unsealing, and to disseminate information contained in sealed records at will, present a question as to the functioning of municipal government of sufficient importance to warrant a summary inquiry.

 

1774 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Paynter v. State


Index No. 98/10280


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


187 Misc. 2d 227; 720 N.Y.S.2d 712; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 569



November 14, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs were disadvantaged and minority students in defendants, city school district (RCSD) and state. Defendants included suburban districts. Plaintiffs' proposed class action claimed violations of N.Y. Const. art. XI , §1, education article; N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, equal protection; 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983; and regulations implementing § 1983. Plaintiffs asked the court to order a state remedial plan. Defendants moved to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs presented statistics to support their claim that the combination of poverty and racial isolation made it was impossible for students to receive a sound basic education as required in N.Y. Const. art. XI , § 1, and sought a declaration that the inferior education provided to minority students in defendant RCSD violated N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and its regulations. The statistics included a high concentration of minority, poor, and low scores. Section 1 required defendant state to offer all children the opportunity of a sound basic education, and did not require substantially equal facilities. Problematic was where the inequalities existing in cities were the product of demographic, economic, and political factors intrinsic to the cities themselves, and could not be attributed to legislative action or inaction. The court gave plaintiffs time to try to meet their difficult burden to overcome defendant state's legitimate nondiscriminatory reasons, to show that N.Y. Educ. Law §3202 and N.Y. Unconsol. Laws § 6265(5), related to low-income housing concentrations in cities, and other specific acts, violated § 11 and § 1983 and its regulations.


OUTCOME: Court granted defendant RCSD's and defendant suburban district's motion, because, although they were necessary parties, no remedies were sought. Court granted defendant state's motion to dismiss education article claims because no constitutional claim of failure to provide for the maintenance and support of a system of free public education was made by merely claiming failed academic results, but not defendant state's remaining claims.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Education Article - Providing Opportunity for Sound Basic Education - Failed Academic Results

1. Plaintiffs' allegations that the combination of poverty and racial isolation in the Rochester City School District (RCSD), caused by the State's role in fostering and failing to address racial and economic isolation between the RCSD and other districts in Monroe County, has resulted in poor academic outcomes of the children in RCSD, fail to state a violation of the Education Article of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art XI, § 1), which requires the State to offer all children the opportunity of a sound basic education. The failed academic results of the RCSD alone vis-a-vis the achievement of the other Monroe County schools are not sufficient to establish the predicate for a constitutional violation.

Schools - Students - Sufficiency of Discrimination Cause of Action under Implementing Regulations of Title VI of Civil Rights Act

2. Defendant's motion to dismiss plaintiffs' cause of action alleging that the State's failure to address racial and economic isolation of minority students in the Rochester City School District violates the implementing regulations (34 CFR 100.3 [b] [3]) under title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 (42 USC § 2000d) is denied. A validly stated cause of action under the title VI regulations includes whether a challenged practice has a sufficiently adverse racial impact and, if so, whether the practice is nevertheless justified. Plaintiffs have presumptively established that the combined effect of Education Law § 3202 and McKinney's Unconsolidated Laws of NY § 6265 (5) (New York State Urban Development Corporation Act § 15; L 1968, ch 174, § 1, as amended), which cause plaintiffs to remain in the Rochester City School Districts with a high poverty concentration, has an adverse discriminatory impact on African-American and Latino students, and thus the State. The State has presumptively defended the challenged statutes by way of legitimate nondiscriminatory reasons. Should section 6265 (5) prove to be discriminatory and not justified or necessary, the articulation of less discriminatory alternative remedies may be deferred. However, should the plaintiffs fail in their proof of discrimination, they will have to set forth their less discriminatory alternatives.

 

1775 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Fitzpatrick v. Youngs


No's: V-166-00, V-167-00


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, JEFFERSON COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 344; 717 N.Y.S.2d 503; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 487



November 2, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion to dismiss the Petition be and the same hereby is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent mother filed a motion for dismissal of petitioner paternal grandfather's petition for an order directing visitation with his minor grandchildren pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §72.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner paternal grandfather filed a petition for an order directing visitation with his minor grandchildren pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §72. Respondent mother of the children opposed the petition. Respondent filed a motion for dismissal contending that the statute was unconstitutional and violated the parental due process right in the visitation context. The motion for dismissal was denied because the New York statute had standing issues and relationship safeguards to protect the parental primacy right while promoting the best interests of the child.


OUTCOME: Respondent mother's motion for dismissal of petitioner grandfather's action for an order directing visitation was denied because the New York statute did not violate respondent's due process rights in that it had standing issues and relationship safeguards to protect the parental primacy right while promoting the best interests of the child.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Preservation of Parental Authority

In a proceeding in which the paternal grandparent of a child whose father died seeks visitation with that child, the motion of the child's natural mother to dismiss the petition is denied. Although the United States Supreme Court has held that a statute granting visitation rights to a broad category of nonparents violated a parent's substantive due process right to make decisions about the care, custody and control of the child (see, Troxel v Granville, 530 US 57), Domestic Relations Law § 72, which prescribes the circumstances under which nonparental visitation shall be permitted, is far more circumspect and involves a two-step process: equitable circumstances must exist or one of the parents must be deceased, and nonparental visitation, limited to the grandparents, must be in the child's best interests. While parents have a fundamental right to autonomy in child rearing, that right is neither unfettered nor absolute: in an appropriate case, the best interests of the child may take precedence. The fact that a grandparent action may be the mechanism triggering the assertion of the child's best interests does not automatically give rise to a declaration of infirmity of the parent's constitutional rights. Although the presumption of parental decision making as being appropriate in the first instance is not statutorily present in New York, nonetheless, by imposing the burden of proof upon the petitioning grandparents, the parental decision has been given some presumptive weight.

 

1776 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Dorfman v. Berman


Index No.: 6355-00, RJI No.:01 00 064124


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 415; 718 N.Y.S.2d 142; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 500



November 1, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Relief requested denied. Cross motion to dismiss denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff instituted a declaratory judgment action in the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) seeking to have N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 42(1) declared unconstitutional and in violation of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 1. Defendant filed a cross motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: After the district attorney submitted his resignation, plaintiff instituted a declaratory judgment action seeking to have N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 42(1) declared unconstitutional and in violation of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 1. Plaintiff further argued that by requiring the vacancy to be filled at the next general election, he was deprived of his right to participate in a primary election. However, the fact that the election was to be held without the possibility of a primary was the result of the operation of N.Y. Elec. Law § 6-116. In providing that the party nomination of a candidate was to be made by a county committee of the party, N.Y. Elec. Law § 6-116, not N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 42(1), precluded a primary election in the circumstances at issue. In so doing, the legislature opted to continue the pattern of filling vacancies at the next general election even in circumstances where it occurred too late to allow for a primary election, and this was interpreted as a reasonable application.


OUTCOME: Relief requested denied. Cross motion to dismiss denied as moot. Election law provisions precluded a primary election in the circumstances at issue. The legislative determination to fill vacancies in offices at the next general election, even in circumstances which would then preclude a primary, was certainly reasonable.


HEADNOTES

Elections - Primary Elections - Absence of Primary Election When Filling Certain Vacancies

1. Public Officers Law § 42 (1), which provides that a vacancy occurring before September 20 in an office authorized to be filled at a general election shall be filled at the next general election, does not violate plaintiff's right to participate in a primary election (see, NY Const, art I, § 1). Section 42 does not provide that a primary election will not be held where a vacancy occurs before September 20; rather, that section only provides that the vacancy be filled at the next general election. That the election is held without the possibility of a primary is the result of the operation of Election Law § 6-116, which provides that the party nomination of a candidate is to be made by a county committee of the party. Accordingly, Election Law § 6-116, not Public Officers Law § 42 (1), precludes a primary election where an elective public office holder resigns his office effective September 19.

Elections - Primary Elections - Absence of Primary Election When Filling Certain Vacancies

2. The scheme chosen by the Legislature for filling a vacancy in an elective public office which occurs after seven days before the last day for circulating designating petitions, or after the holding of the meeting or convention to nominate or designate candidates for such office, is reasonable even though it precludes holding a primary election. Where a District Attorney resigns his office one day before the date on which Public Officers Law § 42 (1) calls for such vacancy to be filled at the next general election, the vacancy could only be filled by an appointed officer through December 31 following the general election. If the office was not filled at the general election, the office would again be vacant after December 31. The scheme devised by the Legislature with respect to vacancies in the office of District Attorney includes limited appointed terms and special elections, all the while pointing to have the vacancy permanently filled in the next general election. In enacting Public Officers Law § 42 and Election Law § 6-116 the Legislature opted to continue the pattern of filling vacancies at the next general election, even in circumstances where it occurred too late to allow for a primary election. Given the prudent constitutional limitation on appointments to fill vacancies and the obligation to fill vacancies, the legislative determination to fill vacancies in offices at the next general election, even in circumstances which would then preclude a primary, is reasonable.

Elections - Primary Elections - Absence of Primary Election When Filling Certain Vacancies - Length of Term

3. In filling a vacancy created by the resignation of a District Attorney on the last day that the law requires such vacancy to be filled at the next general election, i.e., too late for a primary contest to be held, the term of the successful candidate at that election is not limited to the one-year balance of the unexpired term of the former District Attorney, since such a limitation is nothing more than an attempt to impose a judgment that forgoing a primary election for one year represents the extent of a reasonable limitation on the Legislature's authority to determine the manner in which such vacancies are filled. Moreover, such a limitation contravenes New York Constitution, article XIII, § 13 (a), which provides for a District Attorney to be chosen by the electors once in every three or four years as directed by the Legislature, and which has been interpreted to provide for election to a full term when filling a vacancy in that office.

 

1777 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Hertz v. Hertz


Index No.: 21586/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 222; 717 N.Y.S.2d 497; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 464



October 26, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents moved for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(3) and (a)(7) dismissing the verified petition herein on the grounds that the statute upon which the petition was premised, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, violated the U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The underlying proceeding was brought by petitioner/grandfather, wherein he sought an order of the court, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, to obtain visitation with his minor grandchildren. Petitioner was the grandfather of fifteen minor grandchildren. All of the respondent parents submitted affirmations wherein they vigorously opposed the granting of visitation to petitioner. The New York statute allowed only grandparents to petition for visitation rights, compared with the Washington statute struck down by the U.S. Supreme Court, which allowed "any person" to seek visitation. The court herein was of the opinion that the investiture of visitation rights in a party would seem to intrude and actually intrude more severely upon parental autonomy, than does setting a schedule of visitation. That there were factual differences between the cases did not alter the opinion that the U.S. Supreme Court's decision controlled in this case.


OUTCOME: The petition was dismissed; the statute was deemed unconstitutional, in that it violated the respondents' due process rights, specifically, their fundamental rights to make decisions concerning the care, custody and control of their children.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 72 

 Domestic Relations Law § 72, which allows grandparents to apply to court for visitation where either or both of the grandchild's parents are dead or in any circumstances which warrant the equitable intervention of the court, is unconstitutional in that it interferes with parents' fundamental right to make decisions concerning the care, custody, and control of their children in violation of the due process guarantees of the Fourteenth Amendment. Domestic Relations Law § 72 contains no requirement that a court accord a parent's decision any presumption of validity and permits the court to impose its own best interests standard, ultimately allowing the court to ignore the wishes of a parent and substitute its own judgment for that of the parent.

 

1778 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cabassa


Indictment No. 11646/91


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 200; 717 N.Y.S.2d 487; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 457



October 24, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Cabassa, 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7814 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, July 12, 2001)
Later proceeding at People v. Cabassa, 302 A.D.2d 252, 754 N.Y.S.2d 537, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1469 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed motions under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§440.10(1)(h), 440.20 for a new trial and to vacate his sentence after his conviction for sale of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty in federal court to a drug crime, but this conviction was reversed because of a failure to suppress evidence obtained in violation of his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights. In defendant's state trial for selling cocaine, the court ruled that defendant's federal conviction, although on appeal, could be used for impeachment if he testified. He chose not to testify, and was convicted. The court considered his federal conviction, but not the underlying facts of the case, in imposing sentence. After the federal conviction was reversed, defendant moved for a new trial under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §440.10(1)(h), and vacation of sentence under § 440.20. A new trial was denied. Use of the unconstitutionally obtained conviction to impeach would have been error, and under New York law, that the defendant did not testify did not preclude him from raising this issue. The error was harmless, however, as evidence of guilt was overwhelming. The sentence, imposed after the court considered the subsequently reversed conviction, was vacated as a violation of defendant's due process rights. On resentencing, the facts underlying the federal case, if reliable, could be considered.


OUTCOME: Motion for a new trial denied, because giving the State leave to use a conviction (which was later reversed) to impeach defendant if he testified was harmless error; motion to vacate sentence granted, because use of the same conviction in sentencing was reversible error.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Sentence - Motion to Set Aside Sentence - Sentence Taking into Consideration Prior Conviction Voided on Fourth Amendment Grounds

1. A sentence which takes into consideration a prior conviction that has been voided on Fourth Amendment grounds is founded upon misinformation of a constitutional magnitude and cannot stand.

Crimes - Defendant as Witness - Impeachment - Prior Conviction Voided on Fourth Amendment Grounds

2. The use of a prior conviction that has been voided on Fourth Amendment grounds to impeach a defendant's general credibility may deprive the defendant of due process of law if its use influenced the outcome of the case.

Crimes - Defendant as Witness - Impeachment - Prior Conviction Voided on Fourth Amendment Grounds - Applicability of Harmless Error Standard to Nontestifying Defendant

3. In determining defendant's motion for a new trial pursuant to CPL 440.10 (1) (h) on the ground that the trial court's Sandoval ruling permitting the People to impeach him with a prior Federal felony conviction if he chose to testify violated his due process rights because the Federal conviction was subsequently voided on Fourth Amendment grounds, the court must review the constitutional violation under a harmless error standard. Defendant is not entitled to an automatic reversal just because he decided not take the stand after the Sandoval ruling was made.

Crimes - Defendant as Witness - Impeachment - Prior Conviction Voided on Fourth Amendment Grounds - Erroneous Sandoval Ruling as Harmless Error

4. The motion by defendant, convicted of criminal sale of a controlled substance, for a new trial pursuant to CPL 440.10 (1) (h) on the ground that the trial court's Sandoval ruling permitting the People to impeach him with a prior Federal felony conviction if he chose to testify violated his due process rights because the Federal conviction was subsequently voided on Fourth Amendment grounds is denied, as there is no reasonable possibility that the erroneous Sandoval ruling contributed to defendant's conviction. The tape recording of the negotiations between defendant and an undercover officer, the testimony of another officer who was present, and the testimony of the chemist who analyzed the controlled substance demonstrated defendant's knowing sale of just over two ounces of cocaine.

Crimes - Sentence - Motion to Set Aside Sentence - Sentence Taking into Consideration Prior Conviction Voided on Fourth Amendment Grounds

5. In a prosecution for criminal sale of a controlled substance in which the sentencing court took into consideration defendant's prior Federal felony conviction, which was subsequently voided on Fourth Amendment grounds, in determining what sentence should be imposed, and it cannot be said that defendant's sentence was not enhanced based upon the fact of the conviction, defendant's motion pursuant to CPL 440.20 to set aside his sentence is granted to the extent that he will be resentenced. Once a conviction is voided on any constitutional ground, fundamental fairness precludes a sentence from standing where a court has considered the existence of the voided conviction to enhance punishment, whether under a recidivist statute or for purposes of increasing punishment within a statutory range. Where materially untrue assumptions or misinformation are presented to a court at sentencing, due process is violated and resentencing is required.

 

1779 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Park Hope Nursing Home v. Eckelberger


Index # 00/1789


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 617; 713 N.Y.S.2d 918; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 381



September 21, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed a motion for a default judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3215 because defendant failed to submit an answer to plaintiff's complaint alleging that defendant breached his agreement with the nursing home to pay over to plaintiff his Social Security Administration income and requesting specific performance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was admitted to plaintiff's nursing home. Defendant signed an agreement authorizing assignment of Medicaid payments to plaintiff. Although Social Security payments were received by defendant, he failed to make any contributions to plaintiff for his own care. As a result, plaintiff filed suit against defendant. Defendant failed to submit an answer to the complaint, and plaintiff made a motion for a default judgment. Plaintiff sought a court order directing that defendant turn over future Social Security payments. The court concluded that any order granting plaintiff's application would have constituted a violation of 42 U.S.C.S. § 407(a) because any court order would have become a legal process of the court, which was prohibited by § 407(a). Accordingly, the court declined to grant a default judgment in plaintiff's first cause of action. However, § 407(a) did not relieve defendant of his obligation to make payment for the uncovered costs of his care and treatment, but only protected Social Security benefits from the claims of creditors. Thus, plaintiff was to submit the exact amount owed before the court entered a default judgment on plaintiff's second cause of action.


OUTCOME: Default judgment was denied on plaintiff's first cause of action because Social Security Act (Act) protected Social Security benefits from the claims of creditors. However, the court would have entered default judgment on plaintiff's second cause of action, when amount owed was determined, because Act did not relieve defendant of his obligation to make payments for uncovered costs of his care and treatment.


HEADNOTES

Health - Nursing Homes - No Right to Resident's Future Social Security Payments

A nursing home is not entitled to specific performance of an agreement requiring a resident who requires 24-hour skilled care to turn over his Social Security payments in satisfaction of the charges he is incurring, since 42 USC § 407 (a) prohibits such payments from being subject to "execution, levy ... or other legal process." Although the pending case involves private contractual rights, not a State statute or regulation, the express language of the statute prevents any transfer or assignment of future payments, whether by actions at law or in equity, and without regard to the nature of the creditor. Any order granting plaintiff's application would constitute a violation of the statute, since plaintiff, by commencing this action, has invoked the jurisdiction of the State courts, and the current motion requests judicial relief. If the application were granted, an order, which would become a "legal process" of the court, would be issued directing defendant to turn over future Social Security benefits. Moreover, it is not altogether clear that there was an agreement by defendant to include Social Security benefits as part of his personal financial resources, which were to be contributed for his care.

 

1780 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burke


Docket # 2000KN006065


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 278; 715 N.Y.S.2d 603; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 445



September 21, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected January 16, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion for an order precluding the People from reducing the charges or in the alternative granting the Defendant a jury trial on the reduced charges is denied without a hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to preclude the People from reducing his charges from class A misdemeanors to class B misdemeanors or, in the alternative, to grant defendant a jury trial on the reduced charges, claiming the reduction on the eve of trial denied his U.S. Const. amend. VI right to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arraigned on a felony complaint and charged with two counts of assault, menacing, weapon possession and harassment. The felony assault charge was dismissed. Later, the People further reduced the class A misdemeanors to class B misdemeanors. Defendant moved to preclude the People from reducing the charges or to grant defendant a jury trial on the reduced charges, claiming the reduction of charges on the eve of trial denied his U.S. Const. amend. VI right to a jury trial. The court denied the motion. There was no abuse of prosecutorial privilege as the reduction of charges was raised after the two-week adjournment of trial. The reduction of assault to attempted assault was not so inherently different as to alter the defense strategy. There was no showing of vindictiveness as ample time was left for defendant to prepare for trial. Defendant was not entitled to a jury trial where the maximum length of incarceration was less than six months.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied because the reduction of the charges did not deprive defendant of his constitutional right to a jury trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Right to Jury Trial - Prosecutor's Power to Reduce Charges on Eve of Trial

1. The reduction of the charges against defendant on the eve of trial from class A misdemeanors to class B attempt misdemeanors punishable by up to 90 days in jail, thereby depriving defendant of a jury trial pursuant to CPL 340.40 (2), does not constitute an abuse of prosecutorial privilege where the People left ample time before trial for the defense to prepare a proper defense strategy, and did not otherwise attempt to hide the true nature of the charges from the defense. The attempt charges pursuant to Penal Law § 110.00 were not mere "hypothetical crimes" which were impossible for defendant to have committed. Furthermore, the mere act of reducing a charge from an A misdemeanor to a B misdemeanor does not in and of itself constitute prejudice to defendant with regard to the denial of a jury trial since that right arises from, and depends upon, the potential punishment after conviction. A defendant facing a maximum penalty of 90 days' imprisonment has no constitutional right to a jury trial.

Crimes - Right to Jury Trial - Constitutionality of Denial of Jury Trials to Persons within New York City Charged with Petty Offenses

2. Defendant is not entitled to a jury trial in the New York City Criminal Court where the People reduced the charges against him on the eve of trial from class A misdemeanors to class B attempt misdemeanors punishable by up to 90 days in jail. CPL 340.40 (2), which denies the right to a jury trial to persons charged with certain misdemeanor crimes in the City of New York "for which the authorized term of imprisonment is not more than six months," while according the right to a jury trial to those charged with similar crimes outside the City of New York, does not deny equal protection of the law to defendants charged with petty offenses living within the City of New York. The People have demonstrated a compelling State interest that would necessitate a disparity in the right to a jury trial in cases tried in the already overcrowded New York City court system. The denial of jury trials to persons within New York City charged with petty offenses with a maximum incarceration period of six months or less is a necessary means toward achieving the compelling State interest of eliminating unmanageable court congestion. Furthermore, persons charged with petty offenses have no constitutional right to a jury trial. Moreover, defendant's exposure to incarceration has been lessened by allowing the People to reduce the charges to B misdemeanors. Consequently, defendant has not been denied his constitutional right to a jury trial.

 

1781 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


In re Urcuyo


Index No. 9274/00, Index No. 14103/00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 836; 714 N.Y.S.2d 862; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 417



September 20, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondents' motion for a declaration that Mental Hygiene Law 9.60 is unconstitutional absent a requirement that a respondent lacks the capacity to make a reasoned treatment decision before an Assisted Outpatient Treatment order can be granted, is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent filed a motion seeking a declaration that Kendra's Law, N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.60, was unconstitutional because the statute did not require that the respondent lack the capacity to make a reasoned treatment decision before an Assisted Outpatient Treatment order could be granted, and therefore violated respondent's rights to due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners argued that Kendra's Law did not abridge a patient's fundamental right to make his own treatment decisions since there was no forcible administration of medication, that the purpose of the statute was remedial, not punitive, and that a patient's failure to comply with the court-ordered treatment plan resulted only in heightened scrutiny by a physician to ensure that the patient received treatment. The court determined that § 9.60 was constitutional and denied the motion. Section 9.60 did not violate respondent's due process rights because a patient who was subject to an Assisted Outpatient Treatment order had already been adjudicated a danger to self or others for failing to comply with treatment, and the 72 hour evaluation was a reasonable countermeasure under the State's parens patriae and police powers. Section 9.60 did not violate respondent's equal protection rights because the State had a compelling interest in taking measures to prevent patients who posed a high risk from becoming a danger to the community and to themselves, and § 9.60 was narrowly tailored to achieve those goals.


OUTCOME: Motion denied because patients subject to an Assisted Outpatient Treatment order under the statute had already been adjudicated as dangerous and the 72 hour evaluation was reasonable, and because the State had a compelling interest to prevent high-risk patients from becoming dangerous and the statute was narrowly tailored to do so.


HEADNOTES

Mental Health - Assisted Outpatient Treatment - Constitutionality of Kendra's Law

Kendra's Law (Mental Hygiene Law § 9.60), which, inter alia, authorizes a court to order certain mentally ill persons with a history of lack of compliance with medical treatment to follow a specified assisted outpatient treatment plan, does not unconstitutionally violate a patient's fundamental right to make his or her own medical treatment decisions, or result in a violation of equal protection because of the absence of a requirement that the patient be found to lack the capacity to make a reasoned treatment decision before an assisted outpatient treatment order can be granted. The object of Kendra's Law is to ensure that these patients retain their capacity, do not relapse or deteriorate, and do not become a danger to themselves or others. The statute provides that the physician who is advocating for an assisted outpatient treatment plan must provide the patient, the treating physician and, upon the patient's request, "an individual significant to the patient" with an "opportunity to actively participate in the development of such plan." Furthermore, an assisted outpatient treatment order does not unreasonably violate the patient's right to refuse medication by threatening arrest upon noncompliance with the court-ordered plan. Rather, the patient's failure to comply with the treatment plan leads to the heightened scrutiny of physicians for a 72-hour evaluation period, but only after a physician has determined that the patient may be in need of involuntary admission to a hospital. This is a reasonable response to a patient's failure to comply with treatment when it is balanced against the compelling State interest in taking measures to prevent high-risk patients from becoming a danger to the community and themselves.

 

1782 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Smolen v. Smolen


DOCKET NO. V-0051-00


FAMILY COURT OF NEW YORK, ONONDAGA COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 828; 713 N.Y.S.2d 903; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 404



September 15, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent mother filed a motion to dismiss petitioner grandparents' N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72 petition requesting an order granting them visitation with their six-year-old grandchild, whose parent was respondent.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners alleged that they had a close relationship with their granddaughter since her birth, that she and respondent lived with them in their household until she was two-and-one-half years old, that they saw her on an almost daily basis after that until she was four, and that they continued to see her frequently, including baby sitting for her two days per week, until all contact with her was cut off by respondent after a family dispute. Petitioners argued it would be in their granddaughter's best interest for them to continue to have contact with her. Respondent's motion to dismiss the petition was denied without prejudice on grounds a hearing needed to be held to determine whether petitioners had standing to proceed under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, and if so, whether visitation should be granted, in light of the presumptive weight to be afforded to parental decision-making. At the hearing, if respondent's objection to petitioners' visitation was found not to be related to the child's best interest, then the court would have considerably greater leeway to determine whether visitation should be granted.


OUTCOME: Respondent's motion to dismiss was denied so that the court could hold a hearing to address, inter alia, whether petitioners had engaged in conduct which would justify denial of visitation, whether respondent's stated reasons for denying visitation were related to child's best interest, and whether denial of visitation would have any harmful effect on the child.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents

 Domestic Relations Law § 72, which grants standing to grandparents to petition for visitation where one or both parents are deceased or where "equity sees fit to intervene," and provides that the ultimate decision to allow visitation be based on the best interest of the child, has not been rendered unconstitutional on its face by Troxel v Granville (530 US 57, 120 S Ct 2054). There, the Supreme Court held that a State statute governing nonparental visitation, as applied to the facts of the case, unconstitutionally infringed on the mother's fundamental parental rights to make decisions concerning the care, custody and control of her children, and that a State court may not usurp a fit parent's authority to make child-rearing decisions. The New York statute has been interpreted to require substantial deference to the authority of parents in determining whether grandparent visitation is in the best interests of the child. Only after standing is conferred does the court have authority to determine whether grandparent visitation is in the child's best interest. Accordingly, the petition by the maternal grandparents seeking visitation with their grandchild should not be dismissed at this juncture. There are factual issues to be decided before it can be determined whether petitioners have standing under Domestic Relations Law § 72, and, if so, whether visitation should be granted in light of the presumptive weight to be accorded to parental decision-making.

 

1783 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Iftikhar


Docket No. 2000QN014765


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, QUEENS COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 565; 713 N.Y.S.2d 671; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 377



September 11, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 23, 2000.



DISPOSITION: The accusatory instrument before the Court is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was charged, in the Criminal Court of New York City, Queens County (New York), with (1) Second Degree Menacing; (2) Second Degree Reckless Endangerment; (3) Possession of a Police Shield, Emblem, Uniform or Buttons; and (4) Second Degree Harassment. He moved to dismiss the accusatory instrument as defective.


OVERVIEW: Defendant moved to dismiss the accusatory instrument because it did not set forth adequate facts to support any of the charged offenses. In response, the People filed an affirmation in opposition setting forth additional unsworn facts not contained in the accusatory instrument. In reply to the opposition, defendant correctly pointed out the new facts contained in the opposition were not part of the accusatory instrument and could not cure any of its alleged jurisdictional defects. The court dismissed the accusatory instrument. The accusatory instrument did not even allege the physical proximity of defendant to the complainant during the incident. There were no facts that could be alleged to support the charge that defendant's conduct created a substantial risk of serious physical injury to another person and no circumstances demonstrating reasonable cause to believe the alleged act reasonably served to harass, annoy or alarm claimant, or threaten him with physical harm.


OUTCOME: The accusatory instrument before the court was dismissed; it did not set forth facts of an evidentiary character demonstrating reasonable cause to believe that the defendant committed the charged offense.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Menacing - Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument - Reliance upon Unsworn Facts Contained in People's Opposition Papers

1. A count of an accusatory instrument charging defendant with menacing in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.14 [1]) for having "fire[d] a gun placing the complainant in fear of serious physical injury" is facially insufficient and must be dismissed in the absence of any allegations that defendant pointed the gun at complainant, or used it in any way so as to intentionally put him in reasonable fear of "physical injury, serious physical injury or death," or that defendant "displayed" anything to complainant, much less something that "appears to be" some kind of firearm. Furthermore, the accusatory instrument does not allege the physical proximity of defendant to the complaining witness at any time during the incident. Moreover, the People may not cure the jurisdictional defects by utilizing the unsworn facts contained in their opposition papers (see, CPL 100.40 [4] [b]; 100.45 [3]) alleging that defendant entered complainant's motor vehicle, represented himself to be a police officer, demanded to be driven to a location, drew a firearm and then fired a shot as complainant was running away from the vehicle.

Crimes - Reckless Endangerment - Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument - Reliance upon Unsworn Facts Contained in People's Opposition Papers

2. A count of an accusatory instrument charging defendant with second degree reckless endangerment (Penal Law § 120.20) for having "fire[d] a gun placing the complainant in fear of serious physical injury" is facially insufficient and must be dismissed in the absence of any facts suggesting that the gun at issue was real or that it was capable of discharging live ammunition. There are no facts or circumstances from which it can be concluded that this was a real gun, as opposed to a pellet gun, starter's pistol or a theatrical imitation gun capable of firing only blanks. Furthermore, the accusatory instrument does not allege the physical proximity of defendant to the complaining witness at any time during the incident. The People may not cure the jurisdictional defects by utilizing the unsworn facts contained in their opposition papers (see, CPL 100.40 [4] [b]; 100.45 [3]) alleging that defendant entered complainant's motor vehicle, represented himself to be a police officer, demanded to be driven to a location, drew a firearm and then fired a shot as complainant was running away from the vehicle.

Crimes - Harassment - Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument - Reliance upon Unsworn Facts Contained in People's Opposition Papers

3. A count of an indictment charging defendant with second degree harassment (Penal Law § 240.26 [1]) for having "fire[d] a gun placing the complainant in fear of serious physical injury" is facially insufficient and must be dismissed in the absence of any allegations indicating that defendant's act of "fir[ing] a gun" was observed by complainant, the physical positions or proximity of the parties, or any other circumstances demonstrating reasonable cause to believe that the claimed act reasonably could have served to harass, annoy or alarm complainant, or threaten him with physical harm. Moreover, the People may not cure the jurisdictional defects by utilizing the unsworn facts contained in their opposition papers (see, CPL 100.40 [4] [b]; 100.45 [3]) alleging that defendant entered complainant's motor vehicle, represented himself to be a police officer, demanded to be driven to a location, drew a firearm and then fired a shot as complainant was running away from the vehicle. If those facts exist they should have been incorporated into the accusatory instrument.

Crimes - Unlawful Use of Police Uniform or Emblem - Void for Vagueness

4. A count of an accusatory instrument charging defendant with unlawfully possessing a police shield in violation of Administrative Code of the City of New York § 14-107 based on the recovery of "a full size detective shield from the defendant's right rear pocket" following an incident in which he allegedly "fire[d] a gun placing the complainant in fear of serious physical injury," must be dismissed, since the statutory provision relied upon is unconstitutionally vague. The statute makes it "unlawful for any person not a member of the police force ... to have, use, wear or display without specific authority from the commissioner any uniform, shield, buttons, wreaths, numbers or other insignia or emblem in any way resembling that worn by members of the police force." By criminalizing the mere possession of any of the proscribed police items without any specific scienter element, the statute fails to provide the average citizen of "ordinary intelligence" any guidance or fair notice as to what form of possessory conduct is permitted and what is prohibited. The People may not prosecute defendant on the simple allegation that he had the shield in his pocket. To do so would permit a police officer to determine, without legislative guidance, that the shield, whether it was a near duplicate of a real detective's shield or a child's plastic toy, was criminal because it was covered by the broad "in any way resembl[es]" umbrella of the statute. Constitutional guidelines do not permit the law to be applied in that manner. Furthermore, the People may not rely upon the unsworn assertions in their opposition papers that defendant, upon entering complainant's motor vehicle, actually displayed the shield to complainant and misrepresented himself as a police officer in violation of that portion of the statute that prohibits a person from falsely representing himself or herself as being a member of the police force "with a fraudulent design upon person or property."

 

1784 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Gomez v. Evangelista


(NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 816; 714 N.Y.S.2d 636; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 418



September 11, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an indigent inmate, sought to commence a proceeding under the Freedom of Information Law and further sought leave to do so in forma pauperis, as a poor person entitled to have an index number issued by the clerk of the court without payment of a fee under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1101(f).


OVERVIEW: N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1101(f)(2) permitted court fees in proceedings by indigent inmates to be initially waived, but required the indigent inmates to later reimburse the court fees. The court sua sponte in a proceeding by petitioner, an indigent inmate, questioned the constitutionality of the statute because indigent non-inmates did not have to later reimburse court fees that had been waived. The court saw no rational basis for discriminating between poor persons who were not incarcerated and poor persons who were incarcerated. The legislative history of the statute revealed no intent to curtail frivolous prisoner suits. The purpose of the statute, to protect the rights of poor persons to bring civil actions, was thwarted. Prison inmates were to be afforded unhampered and equal access to the legal system. The statutory mandate violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the clerk of the court to issue an index number to petitioner inmate free of charge as he was a poor person within the meaning of the statute and the statutory mandate requiring inmates to reimburse court fees was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Poor Person Status - Filing Fees for Prison Inmates - Equal Protection

 CPLR 1101 (f), which authorizes the court to permit a prison inmate applying for poor person status in commencing litigation to pay a reduced filing fee, but removes any discretion to waive court costs, is unconstitutional insofar as the statute mandates immediate or deferred payment without possibility of discretionary waiver only if the movant is an indigent inmate. For equal protection purposes, poor inmates are similarly situated to poor noninmates through the common denominator of indigency and the impact of that indigency in commencing civil litigation. The fact that an inmate's essential needs are provided by the State does not make that inmate any less poor for purposes of applying for poor person status in filing an action. Furthermore, requiring only inmates to pay filing fees cannot be deemed to be rationally related to a legitimate State interest in discouraging frivolous lawsuits by prisoners. The legislative history of CPLR 1101 (f) reveals no such intent to curtail frivolous prisoner suits within the State. Rather, the purpose behind CPLR 1101 is to protect the rights of poor persons to bring civil actions.

 

1785 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weinkselbaum


INDEX # BATO 869-00


DISTRICT COURT OF NEW YORK, SECOND DISTRICT, SUFFOLK COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 889; 714 N.Y.S.2d 860; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 432



September 8, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the charges against him of violation of Babylon, N.Y., Town Code §§ 213-398, -397.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating Babylon, N.Y., Town Code §§ 213-398, -397 after he erected a free standing sign on the lawn in front of his house addressing the issue of police harassment and brutality against children and teens. Defendant moved to dismiss the charges. The charge of violation of § 213-398 was dismissed because the section required a permit for signs in business and industrial district, and defendant erected the sign in a residential district. The court denied the motion to dismiss the charge of violation of § 213-397 because it found that the regulation was narrowly tailored without regard to content and accomplished legitimate governmental purposes. Thus, the court rejected defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of § 213-397.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss the first count of failure to get a permit for a sign in a business and industrial district was granted because the information was insufficient. The motion to dismiss the charge of erecting a sign in a residential district was denied because the town code section was found constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Freedom of Speech - Regulation of Signs - Reasonable Time, Place and Manner Restrictions

A motion to dismiss a charge that defendant violated a town ordinance prohibiting the display of signs on one's property without first securing a permit is denied where defendant posted a sign containing a political message on his front yard. The government may properly limit the noncommunicative qualities of signs to promote the legitimate government aims of aesthetics and traffic safety, and the prosecution has submitted a number of applications for the temporary sign permits indicating that political messages are customarily displayed by this procedure. The town is trying to promote aesthetic values and to promote traffic safety by avoiding visual distractions, physical obstructions and mistakes or confusion with authorized traffic signs. These time, place and manner restrictions on speech must not be based upon the content of the speech, and the statutes must be narrowly tailored, so that the predominant effect is to limit the secondary results of the speech. Defendant has not rebutted the strong presumption of constitutionality. The zoning ordinances in question are narrowly tailored without regard to content, and accomplish legitimate governmental purposes.

 

1786 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


State v. Della Villa


Index No. 94-0717


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SCHENECTADY COUNTY


186 Misc. 2d 490; 717 N.Y.S.2d 831; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 483



August 31, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 6, 2001.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued a decision upon the trial of the matter. Plaintiff State sought an order permanently enjoining further use of defendants' site as a construction and demolition debris landfill and directing defendants to close the site pursuant to requirements established under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §27-0703.


OVERVIEW: Defendants voluntarily ignored a consent order's prohibitions against receiving additional construction and demolition debris (C&D) materials at their site without having a permit. The agreement was clear and straightforward on its face. Defendants could not have escaped civil liability for admitted violations based upon their expressed intent to "use" rather than to "dispose" of C&D material at the site. Defendants materials were classified as "solid waste" despite their contention that the materials were not spent, useless, worthless, or in excess to the owner at the time of such discard or rejection. Even if defendants claimed ownership of the material, certain portions of it were found to be "in excess." N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 6, § 360 was not so vague or overbroad to deny appropriate notice of the proscribed conduct and the regulation did not invest officials with unlimited discretion to enforce the regulations. The defendant corporations were paper shells created solely to limit liability; thus their corporate veil was pierced, and the individual defendants were personally liable.


OUTCOME: Defendants were directed to prepare and submit a closure plan for the site within 90 days. Plaintiff's request for a bond was denied. Court made permanent the preliminary injunction which enjoined and prohibited defendants from further depositing solid waste, including construction and demolition debris at the site. Individual defendant who disregarded the consent order was personally liable for penalties.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation - Solid Waste Management Facility - Illegal Disposal of Construction and Demolition Debris Regardless of Intended "Use"

1. Defendants may not escape liability for illegally disposing of construction and demolition (C&D) debris (see, 6 NYCRR 360-1.2 [b] [38]) and other "solid waste" on their landfill site by alleging that they were legitimately "using" crushed and compacted C&D materials on their site to prevent the further contamination of their land from an adjacent municipal landfill. The quantity of C&D materials deposited at the site and the manner in which such materials were spread around the site indicates that the C&D materials were more than a frontline barrier against the encroachment of the municipal landfill. In any event, regardless of what defendant's intended "use" of the materials was, the materials defendants deposited on their site without a permit and later bulldozed into more compact form and mixed with earth constitute "discarded" "solid waste" subject to regulation by the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) (6 NYCRR 360-1.2 [a] [2], [3]). Furthermore, defendants have failed to demonstrate that the DEC regulations at issue were so vague or overbroad as to have denied defendants appropriate notice of the proscribed conduct.

Environmental Conservation - Solid Waste Management Facility - Illegal Disposal of Construction and Demolition Debris - Personal Liability of Corporate Owners and Officers

2. Defendants may be held personally liable for their closely held corporations' illegal disposal of construction and demolition debris (see, 6 NYCRR 360-1.2 [a] [2], [3]; [b] [38]) and other "solid waste" on a landfill site without a permit. It is appropriate to pierce the corporate veil to hold the corporate owners and officers personally liable, since the evidence demonstrates that the named corporations were little more than paper shells created solely to limit liability and that the individual defendants exercised complete domination of their respective corporations.

Environmental Conservation - Solid Waste Management Facility - Illegal Disposal of Construction and Demolition Debris - Violation of Consent Order

3. Defendant may be held liable for continuing to operate a solid waste disposal facility and accept construction and demolition (C&D) debris at the site without a permit (see, 6 NYCRR 360-1.2 [a] [2], [3]; [b] [38]) in violation of a 1989 consent order prepared by the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) in which he, as operator of the site owned by his father's closely held corporations, agreed to "cease accepting solid waste" at the site and to cease operations "until a permit to operate is granted." The fact that defendant may have signed the consent order in order to avoid a possible $ 1,000 per day fine or the possible loss of ownership of the site is insufficient to set aside the order on the grounds that it was obtained by fraud or duress. The record demonstrates that defendant sought and received legal advice prior to signing. Moreover, defendant never sought to have the consent order set aside until the commencement of this action by the State seeking a determination that the consent order was violated. The record also shows that defendant caused and directed activity aimed at potential compliance with its terms. Furthermore, defendant cannot avoid liability by arguing that the later received materials were not C&D materials within his understanding of "solid waste." Defendant knew the nature of the materials which were deposited at this site and which prompted DEC to pursue enforcement of the consent order.

PEOPLE v. DEF-28, JOHN F. OWENS


Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with # 414/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 628; 2000 NY Slip Op 40012U



August 30, 2000 Decided
