DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion denied.

 

1788 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Blair


20-417


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, MONTGOMERY COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 398; 713 N.Y.S.2d 657; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 347



August 28, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgments of the Town of Minden Justice Court (Patterson, J.) finding defendant guilty of Disorderly Conduct (PL § 240.20(3)) based upon incidents which allegedly occurred on August 9 and 13, 1999, are reversed on the law and facts, convictions vacated and informations dismissed. The judgment of the Town of Minden Justice court (Patterson, J.) finding defendant guilty of the offense of Harassment in the Second Degree (PL § 240.26(1)) based upon an incident allegedly occurring on August 30, 1999, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgments of the Town of Minden Justice Court (New York), which found defendant guilty of disorderly conduct and harassment in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§240.20(3), 240.26(1).


OVERVIEW: The court reversed defendant's conviction for disorderly conduct under N.Y. Penal Law §240.20(3), but affirmed his conviction for the offense of harassment under N.Y. Penal Law §240.26(1). Defendant had been charged with these offenses by separate informations. On appeal, defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction for disorderly conduct. The court found that no proof was offered by the prosecution that the incident was anything other than a private dispute between individual disputants. Because the evidence was devoid of any proof demonstrating a conscious disruptive intent on the part of defendant, the convictions for disorderly conduct were not supported by the findings of fact. As to the harassment charge, the court found that the conviction was based upon adequate, competent evidence legally sufficient to support defendant's commission of every element of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for disorderly conduct because the evidence was insufficient to support the conviction, but affirmed defendant's conviction for harassment because there was competent evidence to support defendant's commission of every element of the offense.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Disorderly Conduct - Alternative Theory of Liability

In a prosecution for two counts of disorderly conduct in which defendant was accused of using obscene language (see, Penal Law § 240.20 [3]), defendant's convictions of those charges are reversed because no proof was offered that the incident in question was anything other than a private argument between individual disputants over a parking space, or that defendant had used obscene language. When a defendant is charged with violating a specific subdivision of the disorderly conduct statute, a judgment of conviction will be affirmed if the evidence established a violation of any of the subdivisions of that statute. However, the evidence establishing such a violation must have been supported by the factual part of the "information." To hold otherwise would deny the defendant the right to be informed of the nature of the acts constituting the charge so as to prepare for trial and would be tantamount to allowing the People to amend the factual part of the information during trial. The prosecution had jurisdictionally sufficient informations to try the defendant for disorderly conduct under subdivision (3) of section 240.20 of the Penal Law. The People have not advanced any alternative theories, and defendant's convictions based on evidence not supported by the informations shall not be affirmed.

 

1789 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Professional Truck Leasing Sys.


Docket No. 1999SN077898


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 734; 713 N.Y.S.2d 651; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 363



August 9, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 7, 2000.



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved the Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County, New York, to dismiss a prosecuting instrument charging it with a violation of New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 34, § 4-12(j)(1) regarding restrictions on commercial advertising vehicles.


OVERVIEW: Defendant leased, to a mobile advertising company, a truck operated on the streets of New York City, N.Y. New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 34, § 4-12(j)(1) prohibited the operation of a vehicle on city streets for the purposes of advertising. Defendant was issued a summons for violation of this ordinance and moved to dismiss on U.S. Const. amend. I grounds. The ordinance met the requirements of the four-part test for determining the validity of the regulation of commercial speech in that it did not purport to ban only advertising that concerned unlawful activity or was misleading, it sought to implement a substantial governmental interest in promoting public safely by reducing the congestion of automobiles on the city's streets, directly advanced that interest, and reached no further than necessary to accomplish its objective, in that it did not seek to regulate the content of speech. The use of the term "commercial advertising" was not impermissibly vague, as that term has been well defined by commercial speech doctrine.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was denied because the ordinance in question was a permissible regulation of the time, place or manner of commercial speech, as opposed to aa impermissible regulation of its content, and it effectuated the significant government interest in public safety by reducing the congestion of automobiles on the city's streets.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Regulation - Prohibition of Vehicles Used for "Commercial Advertising"

1. A Traffic Rule which bans the operation or parking of vehicles in the City of New York "for the purpose of commercial advertising" (34 RCNY 4-12 [j] [1]), except for advertising notices appearing on vehicles owned by the advertised business, is not unconstitutional as an impermissible regulation of commercial speech by a municipality. The Rule does not impermissibly differentiate based upon the content of the message advertised. Rather, the Rule makes no effort to regulate the content of the prohibited speech. The City adopted the Rule in order to promote traffic safety and alleviate traffic congestion, not to suppress any particular advertised message. Therefore, even though the regulation is based on a particular medium of expression and distinguishes between messages carried on that medium, it remains neutral as to the expression's content. The ban on the use of advertising-only vehicles is narrowly tailored to remove a class of vehicles from the City's streets. The removal of these vehicles from the streets fulfills the City's purpose of reducing the over-all number of vehicles congesting the City's streets. Accordingly, the Rule is not unconstitutional as a ban of commercial speech.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Regulation - Prohibition of Vehicles Used for "Commercial Advertising"

2. A Traffic Rule which bans the operation or parking of vehicles in the City of New York "for the purpose of commercial advertising" (34 RCNY 4-12 [j] [1]), except for advertising notices appearing on vehicles owned by the advertised business, is not unconstitutionally vague in failing to provide fair warning to a person of ordinary intelligence as to the precise nature of what is prohibited. Although the Rule does not define the term "commercial advertising," the distinction between "commercial advertising" prohibited by the Rule and other noncommercial forms of advertising is clear. Under the Rule the focus is not upon whether defendant, the operator of a mobile advertising business, charges a fee for advertising on its vehicles. Rather, the prohibition is upon a vehicle carrying advertising which proposes an economic transaction that is unrelated to the business for which that vehicle is used. Consequently, without fear of prosecution, defendant can operate vehicles with political advertising or advertising for nonprofit entities.

 

1790 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Susquehanna Dev., L.L.C. v. Assessor of Binghamton


Broome County Index No. 1999-00-1726; RJI No. 99-1192-X


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, TOMPKINS COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 267; 712 N.Y.S.2d 817; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 283



July 18, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motion to dismiss plaintiff's petition denied; plaintiff's remaining causes of action for declaratory relief dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss plaintiff's action for relief from tax assessment on its property, and plaintiff sought declaratory judgment that assessment was too high and that moratorium upon new tax certiorari proceedings, imposed by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 727, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: When defendants, a city and its assessor, assessed plaintiff's property, plaintiff petitioned defendant city to reduce the assessment as excessive and to fix the value of the property at its purchase price. The petition was denied. Plaintiff brought a declaratory judgment, alleging that defendants' assessment was 400 percent of the value of the property and that the three year moratorium upon any new tax certiorari proceeding imposed by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 727 was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to plaintiff. The court found that the assessment exceeded full value and that § 727 was unconstitutional to the extent that defendants sought to apply it to defeat plaintiff's claim for relief from an assessment in excess of the limit imposed by N.Y. Const. art. XVI, §2. Also, plaintiff was not complicit in a prior reduction of assessment within the moratorium period.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion and granted summary judgment to plaintiff, declaring assessment exceeded full value and moratorium on tax certiorari proceedings was unconstitutional to extent defendants sought to defeat plaintiff's claim for relief from assessment in excess of constitutional limit.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Assessment - Changing Assessment Following Litigation

Where a commercial property owner settled a tax certiorari proceeding involving the subject property and the tax assessment was reduced from $ 1,675,000 to $ 1,600,000, plaintiff, who purchased the property for $ 400,000, is entitled to a declaration that the challenged assessment of $ 1,600,000 is in excess of the limit imposed by NY Constitution, article XVI, § 2, which mandates that assessments cannot exceed full value. Plaintiff is also entitled to a declaration that RPTL 727, which provides that where an assessment being reviewed is found excessive by final court order or judgment the assessed valuation cannot be changed for the next three annual succeeding assessment rolls, is unconstitutional to the extent that defendants seek to apply the moratorium to defeat plaintiff's claim to reduce the assessment, thereby granting plaintiff the right to prosecute the underlying tax certiorari proceeding. A subsequent owner, though on actual notice of the facts or of the prior excessive assessment, should be free to test the validity of a prior assessment, unless the subsequent owner had waived any right to complain by some complicity in a prior adjustment within the moratorium period. Plaintiff was not privy to the tax certiorari negotiations and its interests were not considered in the final deal. Also, the assessment far exceeds the full value limit imposed by the NY Constitution.

 

1791 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Young


Ind. No. 2000-0021C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 365; 712 N.Y.S.2d 792; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 334



July 12, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application of the defense attorney to be compensated at the same hourly rate as that to be paid to the special prosecutor is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's assigned attorney applied, under N.Y. County Law §722(b), to be paid for services at same hourly rate prosecutor intended to charge county for his services. County replied, and criminal defense lawyers' association averred, in amicus curiae memorandum, that county lacked standing to intervene.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with rape, unlawful imprisonment, and assault. Due to a conflict of interest, a special prosecutor was appointed, and he planned to charge the county $ 200 per hour for in-court and out-of-court time. Defendant's assigned attorney, according to N.Y. County Law §722(b), was to be paid $ 40 per hour for in-court time, and $ 25 per hour for out-of-court time. He claimed the disparity in hourly rates denied defendant equal protection of the law. The court determined that if the argument prevailed, § 722(b) would have been rendered a nullity, because the rate paid would have been dependent upon the rate of each special prosecutor, or the position the regular prosecutor held in the district attorney's hierarchy. Also, caselaw permitted an increased hourly rate only if extraordinary circumstances were demonstrated. Such a showing could not have been made until the case was concluded.


OUTCOME: County did not have right to intervene, but its reply was considered to be amicus curiae memorandum. Defendant's attorney's application was timely. His request was denied, because it was premature to determine extraordinary services were present, and, if his request were granted, statute governing fees would have been rendered a nullity.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Intervention - Intervention by County in Application by Assigned Criminal Defense Counsel for Increased Fee Award

1. The affected County lacks the right to intervene pursuant to CPLR 1013 in an application by assigned counsel for an indigent criminal defendant prior to trial to be compensated at the same hourly rate as the Special Prosecutor that had been appointed in the case, even though it has a financial interest in the outcome of the proceeding. The County's opposition to assigned counsel's application is premature since a fee award is amenable to an administrative review, not an appellate review, and only after the allowance has been made. Although the County lacks the right to intervene, but has the right to complain through administrative channels at the proper time, its reply in opposition will be considered as an amicus curiae memorandum.

Attorney and Client - Assignment of Counsel - Compensation - Reimbursement of Assigned Counsel at Same Hourly Rate as Special Prosecutor

2. An application by assigned counsel for an indigent criminal defendant prior to trial to be compensated at the same hourly rate as the Special Prosecutor that had been appointed in the case is not premature. Although an attorney assigned to represent an indigent defendant in a criminal action must normally describe in detail the extraordinary circumstances which would justify a fee in excess of the statutory rates (County Law § 722-b) and request appropriate compensation at the conclusion of the representation of the defendant, assigned counsel herein is merely seeking a determination that he should be reimbursed at the same hourly rate as the Special Prosecutor. If assigned counsel is entitled to such a favorable ruling, it can be made at this pretrial juncture, since the hourly rate will be applicable to past and future services.

Attorney and Client - Assignment of Counsel - Compensation - Authority of Trial Court to Increase Maximum Statutory Hourly Rate Based on Extraordinary Circumstances - Reimbursement of Assigned Counsel at Same Hourly Rate as Special Prosecutor

3. The trial court presiding over a criminal proceeding has the authority to increase the hourly rate of compensation for assigned counsel for the indigent criminal defendant in excess of the statutory maximum hourly rates upon a showing of extraordinary circumstances (County Law § 722-b). The court's discretion to award compensation in excess of the statutory amounts encompasses both the hourly rate as well as the total amount. However, in this case, the application by assigned counsel to be compensated at the same hourly rate that the Special Prosecutor intends to charge the County must be denied at this pretrial juncture. The mere disparity alone between the hourly compensation paid to the Special Prosecutor and the defense attorney for their services in the same criminal action does not support an increased hourly rate for assigned counsel. If compensation for assigned counsel were based in every instance upon the compensation paid to the prosecutor, whether it is a Special or regular prosecutor, section 722-b would be rendered a nullity. Furthermore, defense counsel cannot make the requisite showing of extraordinary circumstances since the criminal action has not been concluded. The court reserves the right to award assigned counsel an hourly fee in excess of the statutory rates if extraordinary circumstances are shown after the criminal action is terminated.

 

1792 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Owens


Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with # 414/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 490; 713 N.Y.S.2d 256; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 358



July 7, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged under N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(vii), with first degree murder and sought to have the first degree murder count of his indictment dismissed because he argued the law was unconstitutional because it was vague and overbroad, irrationally under-inclusive, and had an unconstitutional disparate racial impact.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to have the first degree murder portion of his indictment dismissed because he claimed N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(vii) was unconstitutional. Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad because it included certain phrases that defendant argued had inconsistent definitions. The court disagreed and found that the statute provided defendant with adequate notice of the conduct prohibited but afforded law enforcement officials with an objective standard to avoid the possibility that the law would be arbitrarily applied. The court also disagreed with defendant's argument that the statute was irrationally under-inclusive because the distinctions between crimes committed during murder were rational. The court summarily dismissed defendant's argument that the law had a disparate racial impact.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss the first degree murder portion of his indictment was denied because the court determined that N.Y. Penal Law § 125.27(1)(a)(vii) was constitutional because it was not vague or overbroad, irrationally under-inclusive, or did it have an unconstitutional disparate racial impact.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Vagueness and Underinclusiveness of Statute 

 Penal Law § 125.27 (1) (a) (vii), which provides that a person may be guilty of first degree murder if the victim was killed "in the course of" and "in furtherance of" the commission of certain enumerated felonies, is not unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The above-quoted phrases, which defendant asserts are constitutionally infirm and are used in the definition of noncapital felony murder, have implicitly passed constitutional muster in the past and nothing compels a different result. Moreover, the statute is not irrationally underinclusive because it fails to include certain felonies which purportedly are equally as serious (i.e., digital rape) as those which are listed as rendering a murder death eligible. A capital punishment statute need only genuinely narrow the class of persons eligible for the death penalty and must reasonably justify the imposition of a more severe sentence on the defendant compared to others found guilty of murder. Sufficient reasons exist, including the level of violence associated with the felony committed during a murder, to justify the distinctions drawn by the Legislature.

1794 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Aubin v. State


Index No. 6312-99; RJI # 01-00-060881


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 338; 712 N.Y.S.2d 781; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 300



June 30, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss plaintiffs' action for a declaratory judgment that defendant New York State's conservation land acquisition was invalid. The defendants claimed the action was barred by the statute of limitations of N.Y. Exec. Law § 818 applicable to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings, as it had not been commenced within 60 days after the issuance of the permit by defendant Adirondack Park Agency (New York).


OVERVIEW: The defendants State of New York and its Adirondack Park Agency acquired lands and conservation easements. The plaintiffs included various persons and fishing and hunting clubs. Plaintiffs commenced actions for a declaratory judgments that defendants' land acquisition was invalid. Plaintiffs alleged violations of several laws and the constitution. Plaintiffs also sought monetary damages. The court granted defendants' motions to dismiss the actions. The declaratory judgment actions were to be treated as N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings. The applicable statute of limitations was N.Y. Exec. Law § 818. The actions had not been commenced within 60 days after the issuance of the permit by defendant Adirondack Park Agency (New York). The action for damages was no longer incidental to the dismissed N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceedings. Therefore, the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction to determine the damage claims. The damage claims had to be brought against the State in the court of claims.


OUTCOME: The motion was granted and the action was dismissed. The 60 day statute of limitations applied. The part of the action seeking damages was dismissed because it was no longer incidental to the action seeking to invalidate the administrative action. The court therefore lacked jurisdiction. The action for damages had to be brought against the state in the court of claims.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer - When Remedy Appropriate - Challenge to State's Acquisition of Fee and Easement Interests in Forest Lands within Adirondack Park

1. Proceedings brought by petitioners challenging, on various grounds, the validity of the State's acquisition on July 1, 1999 of thousands of acres of forest lands and a conservation easement over more forest lands located within the Adirondack Park must be maintained as CPLR article 78 proceedings. A declaratory judgment action is generally not the proper procedural vehicle to challenge an administrative procedure or determination where judicial review by means of an article 78 proceeding is available. Petitioners' causes of action allege various procedural defects or violations in the administrative process by which the State acquired the fee interest and the easement interests of the subject forest lands. Consequently, petitioners' "causes of action for declaratory judgment" are clearly subject to judicial review as article 78 proceedings.

Limitation of Actions - What Statute Governs - 60-Day Limitations Period Applicable to Proceedings against Adirondack Park Agency

2. CPLR article 78 proceedings brought by petitioners in October and December 1999 challenging, on various grounds, the validity of the State's acquisition on July 1, 1999 of thousands of acres of forest lands and a conservation easement over more forest lands located within the Adirondack Park must be dismissed as time barred pursuant to the 60-day Statute of Limitations applicable to proceedings challenging the issuance of a permit by respondent Adirondack Park Agency (Executive Law § 818 [1]). Since the Adirondack Park Agency was the lead State agency in the administrative approval of the land and easement acquisition project, the proceedings herein are subject to the shorter limitations period running from the date of the Agency's issuance of the necessary permit on July 1, 1999. Moreover, even if the normal four-month limitations period for article 78 proceedings applies to the other State respondents and/or to the private respondents as necessary parties, the proceedings as to those respondents are also time barred due to the failure to have obtained personal jurisdiction over any such respondents within 15 days after the expiration of the four-month limitations period ( CPLR 217 [1]). Furthermore, petitioners have failed to demonstrate "good cause" to extend the time for service, nor have they shown any other reason to be granted additional time in the interests of justice.

Courts - Jurisdiction - Jurisdiction of Supreme Court over Monetary Claims against State

3. Where Supreme Court dismissed as time barred CPLR article 78 proceedings brought by petitioners challenging the validity of the State's acquisition of thousands of acres of forest lands and a conservation easement over more forest lands located within the Adirondack Park, petitioners' causes of action seeking monetary damages for respondents' alleged interference with property and contractual rights must also be dismissed. Generally, the State can be sued on monetary claims only in the Court of Claims. While it is possible to receive money damages from the State in an article 78 proceeding where such damages are only incidental to the main relief requested, the dismissal of all of the article 78 proceedings herein renders the exception to the general rule inapplicable. Supreme Court thus clearly lacks subject matter jurisdiction over the claims for monetary relief.

 

1795 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


In re Mandel


SORA #: 00003X2000


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


184 Misc. 2d 897; 711 N.Y.S.2d 313; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 292



June 29, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent appealed the order of the New York State Board of Examiners of Sex Offenders finding that respondent was required to register as a sex offender under New York's Megan's Law, N.Y. Correct. Law art. 6-C.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was convicted of a violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 2252A(b)(2) dealing with the knowing possession of child pornography. After respondent was released on parole, he was notified by the New York State Board of Examiners of Sex Offenders that his case was identified for review under N.Y. Correct. Law §138-a, 2(b). The board found registration to be necessary and notified respondent and the court that a risk level determination was required. Respondent argued that registration was inapplicable because he was not convicted of an offense in another jurisdiction which included all of the essential elements of a felony covered by the registration statute. Since the requirements of § 168-a, 2(b) were not satisfied, respondent's registration was questionable; however, the issue had to be resolved through a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78.


OUTCOME: The court held that, since the applicable statutory requirements were not satisfied, the requirement of registration by respondent was questionable but had to be resolved through an administrative proceeding challenging the board's determination.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Sex Offenders - Sex Offender Registration Act - Registration Requirement for Person Convicted in Federal Court

The New York State Board of Examiners of Sex Offenders (Board), and not County Court, has the authority under the Sex Offender Registration Act (Correction Law art 6-C) to determine whether respondent, who established residence in New York after being convicted in Federal District Court of possessing child pornography and serving his sentence, is required to register as a sex offender with the Division of Criminal Justice Services (see, Correction Law § 168-k [2]). There is no avenue of review by the court of the Board's determination, and the court's role is relegated to that of determining respondent's risk assessment level. While it is questionable whether respondent should be required to register, that issue must be resolved through a CPLR article 78 proceeding challenging the Board's determination.

PEOPLE v. OWENS


Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with # 414/99, DEF-19

1797 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Owens


Ind. # 547/99 Consolidated with # 414/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, MONROE COUNTY


184 Misc. 2d 600; 710 N.Y.S.2d 790; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 228



June 5, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought an order declaring both N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 320.10 and that portion of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2, which denied a capital defendant the right to waive a jury in the guilt phase of his trial invalid, or in the alternate, inapplicable to the guilt phase of defendant's trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought an order declaring both N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 320.10 and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2, cl. 3, unconstitutional, or, in the alternative, inapplicable to the guilt phase of defendant's trial. The challenged laws prohibited a capital defendant from waiving a jury in the guilt phase and the punishment phase, respectively, of the proceedings. Defendant's motion was denied. Because defendant had yet to request a jury trial, his case was not ripe, however, any such request would have been denied. The court, therefore, considered the merits of defendant's argument and denied the motion. Defendant had not proved beyond a reasonable doubt that the challenged provisions were unconstitutional, or should have been invalidated. Neither the federal nor the state constitution guaranteed a defendant a right to a bench trial, capital defendant's were not a suspect class, and the provisions were rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest.


OUTCOME: Motion for order declaring statutory and constitutional provision unconstitutional, or, in the alternative, inapplicable denied. Defendant had not proved beyond a reasonable doubt that the challenged provisions were unconstitutional, or should have been invalidated.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - Capital Murder - Right to Bench Trial

Although defendant's indictment for first degree murder brings him squarely within the statutory scheme which he argues is facially defective, viz., NY Constitution, article I, § 2 and CPL 320.10, which prohibit a capital defendant from waiving his right to a jury trial, the issue of the unconstitutionality of the aforementioned provisions is not ripe inasmuch as defendant has yet to request a bench trial. Even if defendant were to make a perfunctory request for a bench trial, the same would be denied. The Federal and State Constitutions guarantee a defendant a right to a jury trial, but no similar guarantee is bestowed upon the right to a bench trial. Nor are capital defendants a suspect class under an equal protection analysis. Since there is neither a suspect class nor a fundamental right at issue, the Equal Protection Clause merely prescribes that the prohibition bear a rational relationship to a legitimate government purpose, and the prohibitions against nonjury trials allow a jury to act as a safeguard between an individual and the tyranny of the State, and reflect the legitimate reluctance to entrust plenary powers over the life and liberty of the citizen to one Judge, rather than to 12 jurors who represent the conscience and moral judgment of the community.

 

1798 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Seneca Ins. Co.


Index No. 31317/99, Indictment No. 4937/99


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


185 Misc. 2d 15; 710 N.Y.S.2d 776; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 223



May 30, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to vacate and set aside the judgment of forfeiture is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to vacate the judgment of forfeiture of bail entered against it on the grounds that defendant never received notice of defendant's arraignment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant surety's client was arraigned on a felony complaint. Defendant surety posted a bail bond for defendant client, who was released from custody. Later, defendant client was informed he had been indicted and a notice of arraignment to defendant client and counsel was sent. Notice was not sent to defendant surety. Defendant client failed to appear, a bench warrant was issued, and bail was forfeited. Judgment against defendant client was entered without notice to defendant surety of his failure to appear or forfeiture of bail. Defendant surety then moved to vacate forfeiture. The court granted the motion, finding N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.10(2) required notice of arraignment to defendant surety. The motion addressed the inherent power of the court to vacate its own actions. While defendant surety did not have due process rights in regard to bond forfeiture, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.10(2) required actual notice of arraignment to defendant surety and personal service. Neither notice or personal service was made by the superior court.


OUTCOME: The motion was granted because the superior court failed to provide actual notice, through personal service, of defendant client's arraignment date.


HEADNOTES

Bail - Remission of Forfeiture - Court's Failure to Notify Surety of Date of Defendant's Arraignment

A surety is entitled to vacatur of a forfeiture order where the court failed to notify the surety of the date of the alleged criminal defendant's arraignment, and the defendant failed to appear. CPL 210.10 (2) provides that the superior court must notify the defendant, his or her attorney, and his or her surety, of the date of the defendant's arraignment. The word "must" is mandatory language as it relates to the notice to the defendant, the attorney and the surety. The best way to ensure that the surety fulfills its obligation to produce defendant is to notify the surety of the arraignment date. In addition, CPL 210.10 (2) creates a nondelegable duty on the superior court to comply with the requirements of this section. Moreover, the court must give actual notice to the surety by personal service.

McCulloch v. New York State Ethics Comm'n


Index No. 805-00, RJI No. 01-00-ST0625


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


184 Misc. 2d 546; 708 N.Y.S.2d 845; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 195



May 25, 2000, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted and the respondent's determination dated October 27, 1999 is vacated and annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding seeking a judgment annulling a determination of respondent, New York State Ethics Commission, that petitioner violated N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 73(8)(a)(ii) and/or a judgment declaring N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 73(14) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: While working for a state agency, petitioner prepared a grant application for a wastewater treatment system for a town. The town was awarded the grant, and petitioner administered it until he left state employment. The town then terminated its contract with the agency, and hired petitioner to complete the grant administration, through his private company. Respondent New York State Ethics Commission determined there was reasonable cause to believe petitioner violated N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 73(8)(a)(ii) which prohibited any former state employee from practicing or appearing before any state agency for life regarding any matter which was directly related to that person's former employment. That statute did not prohibit the conduct of the petitioner because he did not appear or practice before any state agency. The question was one of pure statutory reading and analysis.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition, holding even though what petitioner did appeared to be improper and unethical, because he did not appear before a state agency as prohibited by statute, he did not violate state law.


HEADNOTES

Public Officers - Conflicts of Interest - Postemployment Business and Professional Activities - Prohibition against Appearing or Practicing before State Agency

Petitioner, who secured and administered for a town a Federal grant for a wastewater treatment system while he was employed by the Tug Hill Commission, a State agency, and was then hired by the town after he left the Commission in order to continue administering the grant in place of the Commission, did not violate Public Officers Law § 73 (8) (a) (ii), which prohibits a former State employee from practicing or appearing before any State agency for life regarding any matter which was directly related to that person's former employment. What petitioner did appears improper and may well be improper and unethical, but since petitioner did not appear or practice before any State agency, his conduct did not violate Public Officers Law § 73 (8) (a) (ii).
